The Red Page
crowding, begin to go out. The doors are
burst open, and a flood of Black Maskers
pour into the ball. Lorenzo, trying to smile,
finds that he cannot. He discovers that lie
is wearing a mask, too. The scene ends
with the window being broken open, and
more Black Maskers pouring in—ends in
darkness and horror.
In the next scene Lorenzo lies in his
coffin, dead —and Lorenzo stands, dressed in
black, behind the coffin. The living Lorenzo
talks to the dead one, who, as is natural,
has nothing to say. His widow —or is it his
wife?—comes to weep over the corpse. The
scene is conceived in a mood of the bitterest
irony. Then once more we are shown the
great ballroom, where Lorenzo, now de-
mented, believes he is giving another masked
ball. He takes his servants and even his
wife for masked guests. He even asks his
wife to take off her mask. And this strange
play ends with the castle being consumed by
fire, Lorenzo dying in the flames.
The whole thing, of course, is an allegory
of the soul of man. Andreyeff tells us: “The
castle is the soul: the lord of the castle is
man, the master of the soul; the strange,
black maskers are the powers whose field of
action is the soul of man, and whose mysteri-
ous nature he can never fathom.” The sim-
ple maskers, the guests of Lorenzo, are ordin-
ary people; yet even these are transformed
and lose their real semblances, and, like the
black maskers, they become mysterious, in-
comprehensible and terrifying to Lorenzo.
The other Lorenzo is his other soul: this
recognition by Lorenzo of the duality of his
soul leads inevitably to insanity. Yet
Lorenzo, in that concluding scene, having
slain his double, the dark and evil elements
of his soul, has won through, has achieved
—only to perish at the moment of attain-
ment of his faith.
Even a reading of this play indicates its
tremendous power: it is a horribly modern
Hamlet. Its production on the stage must
have been a thrilling event.
The third play included in this volume,
The Sabine Women, is, we are told, a satire
upon a Russian political party. The Sabine
women, for instance, are “the constitutional
promises wrung from the Government by the
revolution of 1905 and 1906.” Our troubles!
The plain fact is that in The Sabine Women
this Russian dramatist has written a play
that might have been the product of Bernard
Shaw. It is twin to Androcles and the Lion.
It is a brilliant and delightful burlesque on
ancient history, witty and absurd. What
could be more Shavian than the stage direc-
tion for the beginning of the second act: the
grieving Sabine husbands, whose wives have
been forcibly abducted by the Romans, are
seen wrapt in gloom. “A part of the Sabine
men are engaged in gymnastic exercises.
As they move their arms they repeat with
rapt attention: ‘Twenty-five minutes’ daily
drill will banish every pain and ill.’ In the
middle of the stage, on a long bench, sit in
a row the husbands who have children. Each
one holds a baby in his arms. Their heads
droop wearily to one side, and their atti-
tudes dramatise despair.” The meeting be-
tween the Sabine husbands and their
abducted wives in the camp of the Romans
is delightful extravaganza. To the husbands’
surprise, their wives are quite content to re-
main with their abductors. Proserpina tells
one sorrowing, deserted husband that there
is only one means left to bring his wife back
to him. “Abduct her!” Marius questions:
“But will she come?” “How can she help
coming,” Proserpina replies, “if you abduct
her?” To his reply that to commit violence
would affront his legal conscience, Proser-
pina replies: “If I am to remain faithful I
want a strong man, the strongest man there
is. Do you think we are so fond of being
abducted and stolen, and asked back and re-
turned, and lost and found? I no sooner
get accustomed to one man than along comes
another, and carries me away, and I no
sooner get accustomed to the new man than
the old one appears and says: ‘Come back.’.
Really, if you want your wife to be your
own, then all you have to do is to be" the
strongest. Struggle for her tooth and nail.
In short, die in her defence. Believe me,
Marius, there is no greater joy for a woman
than to die upon the grave of a husband
who has fallen in her defence. And be
assured that a woman proves false only after
her husband has proved false.”
One wonders if Bernard Shaw knows
Russian ? Or is Shaw’s real name Andreyeff ?

James A. Philp, a distinguished old-time
contributor to this paper, has collected Some
Bulletin Stories, from his pen (Edwards,
Dunlop, Sydney; Is.). “The Stinkin’ Pride
of Harold Daveney” has a fine, mellow
flavor; and the M.L. stories are interesting
and humorous. All that need be said of the
volume is that the contents were worth
republishing in book-form.
The Russian Drama
Already we have mentioned a remarkable
play by Leonid Andreyeff, The Life of Man.
This play, with two others, equally aston-
ishing, is included in the volume of Plays
by Leonid Andreyeff (Duckworth, per Dym-
ock’s Library).
Andreyeff, like most modern Russian
authors, suffered in youth from lack of
means, and attempted to commit suicide.
In fact, he made three attempts to kill
himself, which suggests that the youth
was either remarkably lucky or remarkably
incompetent. He tried vainly to earn a
living by painting and by the law. That
wide haven, journalism, ultimately received
him; and from feuilleton writing he was led
to literature. It was Gorki, then at the
height of his fame, who “discovered” this
new genius. The publication of a small
volume of his stories made him famous.
His plays are extremely popular; they have
long runs in the theatres; and in the printed
form they have an immense sale. An edi-
tion of 18,000 copies of his drama, King
Hunger, reviewed long ago on this page,
was sold out in a single day.
Andreyeff declares that his plays are
panpsychic, “all thought.” He does not con-
sider action, in the accepted sense of move-
ments and visible achievements, necessary
to the theatre. We may translate his pan-
psychic drama as the psychological drama
of Maeterlinck, Barker, Shaw and the new
Repertory school. In illustration of his
thesis that modern drama tends to with-
draw farther and farther from external
activities and deeper and deeper into the
recesses of the mental life, Andreyeff con-
trasts the lives of two men of two widely
divergent centuries, Benvenuto Cellini and
Nietzsche. The life of the medieval artist
and adventurer is crammed with events.
“Cellini encounters more events in a short
walk from his home than the average mod-
ern man does in his entire life.... His life
is a personification of the older theatre.”
Melodrama is still medieval. There were in
Nietzsche’s life, there are in our lives, few
events, activities or physical acMevements—
unless, of course, we enlist. “The real
drama of Nietzsche’s life begins just at the
time when his life withdraws into the
silence and inactivity of the study.” That
is where the drama of modernity begins.
Andreyeff’s complaint is that the modern
theatre has never thrown open the door that
leads to the psychological experiences of
mankind. It is his life-work to unlock that
door. And in his plays he not only opens
that door, but he smashes it to pieces.
He does not imply that events have ceased
to occur —his dramas are crowded with
events; but the dramatic value of exterior
events has fallen. Life has become more
psychological. “Not love, nor hunger, nor
ambition, but thought in its sufferings, joys
and struggles, is the true hero of the life of
to-day.” Andreyeff’s last play is entitled
Thought. A bibliography of Andreyeff’s
dramatic and literary work covers so many
pages in this volume that he must be a man
of extraordinary productivity.
j*
The Black Maskers is a remarkable pan-
psychic drama, even for Andreyeff. A noble-
man, Lorenzo, is giving a great masked ball
in his castle. But when the invited guests
arrive in their disguises he is unable to
recognise anyone. He attempts to identify
one of his friends, Signor Basilio. The
masker addressed replies: “Signor Basilio is
not here. Signor Basilio is dead.” Lorenzo
laughs. “Why, he is as much alive as I
am.” The masker retorts: “Are you, then,
alive?” Another masker informs him that
she is his heart: her costume is a red one
with a black snake twined about it. More,
maskers arrive, and Lorenzo is the only one
undisguised. Soon his perplexity deepens;
he cannot find his servants, his jester, nor
even his wife. Everybody wears a mask.
Thinking he has identified his wife, he finds
two other masked women similarly disguised
and speaking with the same remembered
voice. Lorenzo begins to have grim doubts
about this seeming jest. Then the first of
the Black Maskers appears—“a strange, de-
formed creature like a living fragment of
darkness,” frightening even the other mask-
ers. Other Black Maskers steal in. A fright-
ened guest cries, “Uninvited guests are com-
ing?-” Another confronts a Black Masker.
“Off with your mask, sir!” He tries to pull
the black mask from the face of the stranger,
and springs back in terror, crying: “They
are not masked, gentlemen!’ In tbe. general
confusion everything is enveloped in dark-
ness.
In the next scene Lorenzo meets himself
in the castle tower. Both appear to be
Lorenzo; each in turn demands that the
other should unmask; each asserts he is
Lorenzo and the other an impostor. They
fight, and one Lorenzo kills the other.
Once more we are shown the ballroom, in
which there are now a number of Black
Maskers, “touching the white marble
columns with their black, hairy fingers.”
From the talk of the other maskers, who
are afraid of them, it seems that the Black
Maskers are strange creatures from the
night, attracted to the castle, like moths,
by the illuminations. One Black Masker
says: “I do not know who I am. Someone
lighted up the tower, and we came.” They
crowd round the fire, which immediately
begins to go out.
Lorenzo appears, with clothes torn from
his duel, and on his bared chest a large
blood-spot. A Black Masker says to him:
“You roused the whole night. Everything is
astir there, and now the night is coming
hither.” A voice cries: “All the darkness of
the night is transformed into living crea-
tures, and from every side they are coming
hither. Bar the doors.” The lights, around
which the shivering Black Maskers arc
The Bush through French Eyes
Things Australian occupy but little space
in the French reviews. Of late years one
recalls only a fantastic tale of the horrors of
Melbourne life in Fan, an excellent article on
Henry Lawson in the Mkbcube,. a notice of
Stevens’ Australian Anthology in La Piia-
J.ANGE, and a story or two by Paul Wcnz in
La Nouvelle Revue Fkaxcaise. So it is
pleasant to meet L’homme au soleil eou-
vhant by the, last named in one of the greater
reviews, La Revue de Paws for April and
May (per Angus and Robertson, Sydney).
“The Sundowner” is, I think, his first novel
and fulfils the promise of Contes Ausira-
liens (1911). In the case of a novelist
writing of xYustralia for foreign readers,
there is always a danger lest the story
should be clogged by overmuch local color;
but Paul Wenz has avoided this pitfall, and
Ms story carries its exposition of Austra-
lian conditions, custom and slang, with ease
to a natural close.
The plot is simple, and for us the interest
lies in the treatment of bush life by a
Frenchman, whose intimacy with his sub-
ject has not blunted his sense of contrast
nor his power of observation. This latter
quality seems occasionally at fault, as when
the author lets us infer such an unusual
proportion of Irish people in his mining
town. And who ever heard of am Irish boy
named Betts? But there’s quare things in
the bush. Possibly he is the result of one
of these mixed marriages. In the matter of
dates, too, the book is puzzling; M. Wenz
has apparently synchronised events and
types of the early ’9o’s with those of later
years.
The hero, Harry Preston, Englishman, is
refused by an Australian heiress wliom he
has followed to Sydney, wastes his substance
in despair, and takes to the track, which
ultimately, after many turns, .leads him to
the original girl. On the road he encounters
Alf Lambert and Tom O’Neil, who befriend
him and try to teach him the ways of the
sundowner. Alf is a philosopher and mis-
ogynist, who early left home, vexed by too
much piety, “to see what sin was like.” In
the bush he finds shelter from the three
plagues of man —work, worry, woman —and
can there “take life in little spoonfuls with-
out bothering whether any will be left in
the bottle for the morrow.” His mate Tom
would agree with this formula, with a reser-
vation as regards the third plague. Tom,
born on “The Rocks,” was driven outback
as the result of a push row, is fascinated by
the road, but is still attracted by any little
bit of skirt (morceau de jupe). In their
travels the three rub against bush types
such as the publican, the distrustful station
storekeeper, the prospector, the teamster,
and many of their own kind. These are
familiar to us, but are introduced to French
literature in the work of M. Wenz with
understanding, sympathy and humor.
For the swagmen M. Wenz has many an
admiring word: “These swaggies were all
dirty, with the dirt of the bush, which is not
the filth of the town, for it is combined of
dust and mud, it smells of eucalyptus and
tbe smoke of camps Their poverty is a
poverty of the open air. Their very fleas
would not consort with hotel fleas. Their’s
is the aristocracy of poverty; they levy trib-
ute rather than ask for alms.... Australia
is a land of Canaan which feeds them and
will feed them till the end of their days.
They have, in a manner, the halo of the
traveller of old times, who was always a
welcome guest. Their welcome is no longer
so hearty nor so earnest; it has become
rather a duty.”
His appreciation of the “alluring soli-
tude, wild beauty and monotony of the
bush” is tempered by the persistence of flies
and mosquitoes. He misses the mental and
material background of older countries; Cap-
tain Cook, Peter Lalor and the bushrangers
are a poor substitute for the far-stretching
tradition of his own land: “This continent,
geologically one of the most ancient in the
world, is too new for the white man. It has
no history. The primitive legends which
peopled its lonely spaces have perished with
the native tribes.... ruins which are the
tangible past, old castles, old temples which
form such a rich part of a people’s history,
all these are wanting to Australia... . the
only ruins in the bush are the armies of dead
giants, slain by the axe of man, who
destroys a forest to feed his sheep. These,
indeed, are beautiful, by night when the
moonlight whitens their great" gaunt skele-
tons ; or at evening when the setting sun
bathes them in rosy light, and clouds of
screaming cockatoos perched on their naked
branches transform them for an instant into
monstrous almond trees in blossom.”
J. J. Q.
Max Stlrner
-i" the ranks of rebel philosophers Max
S liner occupies a conspicuous place. Hen-
rik Ibsen is his literary descendant, and i
thought is alleged to have influenced Treitz-
schke and the von Bernliardi school of
dreamers. Anyhow, he is the ethical parent
ot .Nietzsche who, however, was only on
listening terms with his father. And Niete
sche had a large, ready ear for Stirner’s
teaching as the researches of FriedrS’s
txiend, Overbeck, bear astonishing witness
Stirner, whose real name was Johann fas!
ivnft SC w. ld r n W ? S at Bayreuth, in
i S() 6. He died m Berlin in 1856. Apart
nom his intermittent starvation and his
reckless marriage with a lady named Marie
Dahnhardt who after his death, visited
Australia, married a laborer, and became a
washerwoman, Stirner’s life was singularly
uneventful. He was a mild, abstemious
character, congenitally shiftless and self-
effacing, and had the pallid-eyed myopia of
the typical leu ton pedagogue. For several
years lie found, life easy in the refined atmo-
sphere ot a girls’ school, but improvidence
and incapacity twice cost him imprisonment
for debt. Yot behind the meek eyes of this
small professor was the soul of a slinking
Superman. Between 1843 and 1845 lie pub-
lished his astounding effort, The Only One
and His Property, which many subsequent
Anarchists probably set to the music of
dynamite.
Now this anarchistic book, known in
English as The Ego and His Own, is not a
great work, nor is Stirner a commanding
writer. If he had been a real craftsman he
might have achieved the Nietzschean dis-
tinction of becoming a Penny Dreadful to
the Great Unread. But Stirner lacked the
manner of the litterateur and the physical
resolution of a vigorous heretic. He could
not translate his message, like Bakunin,
into the melodrama of pistol and poison.
Nor could he, like Nietzsche, sacrifice sense
to the beguiling sound of beautiful words.
Yet admitting his premises, Stirner’s argu-
ment is consistent, Nietzsche’s never. That
explains "why Stirner is never entertaining,
while reading Nietzsche is like a tour
through Chaos on a char-d-bane. Still, with
all its literary deficiencies, the book has the
sure note of revolution, sustained with a
staccato intensity that seems a new kind of
idiom. “Might is right,” exclaims Stirner,
bawling to an attentive Teuton posterity.
And Might is an abounding Egoism seeking
Satisfaction by the amiable principle of
force, majeure. The wayback French King
who said “-The State is I” gave Stirner the
material for the modern Kaiserism, “I am
the Universe.” Listen to Max: —
What you have the might to be you have the
right to be. I deduce all right and entitlement from
myself; I am entitled to everything that I have
might over. I am entitled to overthrow Zeus,
Jehovah, God, etc., if I can; if I cannot, then these
gods will remain in the right and in the might as
against me Away with every business that is not
altogether my business I You think at least the
“good cause” must be my business? What good,
what bad? Why, T myself am my business, and I
am neither good nor bad. Neither has meaning for
me. What is divine is God’s business; what. is
human, Man’s. My business is neither what is divine
nor what is human; it is not what is true, good,
right, free, etc., but only what is mine; and it is no
general business, but it is—unique, as I am unique.
Nothing is more to me than myself.-
Stirner rejects all authority, gods and
moral values. He admits the institution of
property only under the following condi-
tions :
Property should not, and cannot, be done away
with; rather, it must be torn from ghostly hands
and become my property; then will the erroneous
consciousness that I cannot entitle myself to as much
as I want vanish —“But what cannot a man want?”
Well, lie who wants much, and knows how to get it,
has in all times taken it to him, as Napoleon did
tho Continent and the French Algeria. Therefore,
the only point is that the respectful: “lower classes’
should, at length, learn to take to themselves what
they want. If they reach their hands too far for
you, why defend yourselves.”
Other extracts could be given, but those
are sufficient to indicate the material of the
grim, philosophic garment that Stirner
wove, so to speak. Nietzsche coming more
than a generation later cut this garment
to the needs'of a ballet dancer, and still left
sufficient stuff for a winding sheet. Stirner-
ism was too much for Nietzsche. It gave
him the idea for The Transvaluation of All
Values and Beyond Good and Evil, and drove
him to create the mountebank Zarathustra
who showed appropriate gratitude by wor-
shipping his maker. Nietzsche is now com-
paratively dead, but Stirnei' is becoming
very much alive.
JEAN SIBI.

Rifle And Pistol Cartridges
When you buy rifle and pistol cartridges you want
them to be sure-fire and accurate, and to have the
proper speed and penetration—qualities which are all
essential for good shooting. There is nothing in the
appearance of a cartridge that indicates whether
it does or does not possess these qualities, for the
elements that make them cannot be seen. Therefore
when buying you have to rely upon reputation.
Winchester Rifle and Pistol Cartridges enjoy the
highest reputation the world over for quality and
reliability. When you buy, if you will insist upon hav-
ing Winchester make you can feel confident of getting
cartridges which are sure to give the fullest measure of
shooting satisfaction. Look for the Won the box. It is

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Lagoon.
If you wander the long beach by night
Where the tall breakers comb to their fall,
And the moon sheds her magical light
In a glamor of white over all,
And a ship on the distant sea shows
(As she floats through the mystical scene)
A side-light as red as a rose,
Or a side-light of emerald green,
You will come in a long league or so
To a silver-lit, hidden lagoon,
Where the tides of the sea never flow
Or ebb at the call of the moon.
It is choked, silted up, at its mouth,
And seldom its waters break forth;
A dune of white sand to its south
And a darkness of scrub to its north.
And there (if you muffle your feet
And bide in the tea-trees) anon
You may see, sailing on, like a fleet,
Black swan in the wake of black swan.
And it may be a mullet will leap
All silver of tail and of fin,
And the waters awake from their sleep,
And the ripples like dancers come in.
And then, as the bright ripples dance
And the weeds on the shoreline are stirred.
You will hear in the distance, perchance,
The song of a hidden night-bird.
It will float from the back of the dune.
And thrill you, and charm you, and hold;
For the notes of that night-haunting tune
Will be magic and music and gold.
And then, when the night-bird grows mute —
All still is tlie shining lagoon—-
A beetle (a bat in pursuit)
May drone in the light of the moon.
And it may be, by bending your ear,
Your fancies and senses astir,
Alone in the dark you will hear
Subtle movements of feather and fur.
The cry of a thrush that awakes
To the green of death’s eyes in the gloom;
Or the pad that the spotted cat makes
As it creeps, certain-footed as Doom.
Or it may be ’twill be the far howl
Of a dingo the dark will betray ;
Or the velvet-soft, wings of an owl
As it swoops through the night on its prey.
And thus, as you hark to the strife
Around and about and above,
You will say : “Here is death amidst life.
Where alone should be beauty and love.”
And then, like a voice faint and far,
The sea with a chant that annoys
May tell of a man’s world at war
And the beauty and love it destroys.
N.S.W.
RODERIC QUINN.
In the Whin.
I wouldn’t go out iu the whin at night
The way that sister goes,
For I saw the dance of a blue corpse-light
And the grass was hidden in fog bands white,
Yet she’ll no be heedin’ those.
For out by the lean of the climbin’ fir,
In the murk of the cattle byre,
Somebody whistled a call to her
And I saw the plaid on her bodice stir
And her cheek put up its fire.
A slopin’ roof has our attic room
And a bit of a smokin’ light,
A yellow halo to break the gloom,
And it showed her fine as a gowan bloom
When she slipped to the whin to-night.
Our mither has lain in the auld kirkyard
Five year with my feytlier’s kin,
And my. feyther is dour and aye works hard,
For he left his smile in the green kirkyard
At my mither’s bury in’.
And little he cares for a maid’s desire
And little of maids he knows,
But his wrath would burn as a kil-n fire
If he knew the tryst by the cattle byre
And the path that sister goes.
I wonder if she has coaxed a charm
From the black-eyed, wrinkled witch.
Or what she has found to keep her warm
And brave to ghosties and safe from harm
Where the daftie corpse-lights twitch.
I ken she has set her Bible high
On the nook-shelf many a day,
And she watches the kirk folk straggling by
With a mocking lip and a wistful eye,
And I never hear her pray.
The moon lifts over the orchard wall
Like a dusty lantern’s light;
Her pillow bides empty. . . .no latch’s fall. ..
But far in the heather a screech-owl’s call
Comes over the whin to-night.
QUEENSLAND.
M. FORREST.
The Things I Do Not Know.
My little boy at evening comes
To clamber on my knee.
And asks about so many things
That he bewilders me;
And as his oueries come and go,
I learn the things I do not know.
Though rags and tags of many things
I carry in my brain.
To satisfy his eager quest
My answers are in vain.
Why follows why in constant flow
To show the things I do not know.
With Smith or .Tones, or Brown or .Tames
On many things I spout;
They think that I am well-informed
And gravely hear me out;
But every night at six or so
I learn the things I do not know !
N.S.W.
HAMER.

THURSDAY, SEPTEMBER 9, 1915.
BUCK UP!
In the beginning the Commonwealth was
creeping with frothy persons who rearranged
the map of Europe, indicated the exact spot
where the Kaiser was to be hanged, and fixed
the execution and general clearing-up for a
date in the near future. Now that things have
not progressed entirely according to the speci-
fications which they made for themselves with
the worthy assistance of the Censor’s office,
many of those who were wont to froth have
started to moan. The enthusiast who used
to throw his hat in the air with joy when his
daily paper told him that the German army
had been routed again, has begun to say in
his depression that the enemy is too well
organised for us to defeat him. This person
is beginning to be concerned about his securi-
ties. The thought of the British Navy no
longer thrills him as it did when he pictured
the fleet bombarding Heligoland, and surging
on to Kiel and Hamburg. The army has not
fulfilled his expectations by kicking the Ger-
mans across the Rhine to the , sound of a
good old British cheer. Nothing has hap-
pened quite as he anticipated, and his heart
is a little sore and his feet a trifle cold. It
is not claimed that he exists in vast numbers,
but there are certainly enough of him to make
an easily-discerned mark on the social land-
scape, and the effect of his dismal wails is as
pernicious as his flamboyant confidence was a
year ago.
In point of fact, the position of the British
Empire is so good, after 12 months of war-
fare, that it is an embarrassment to discuss it
with an intelligent ally. The Germans have
the advantage of position on the European
Continent now as they had when their armies
settled down last year behind the Marne; but
they are not so near their main objective—
the overthrow of Britain —as they were two
months ago ; and by all appearances they can
never get so near again. The road to Calais
is a necessary by-path on the long, long way
to London, and unless the military scientists
err, it has been rendered impassable. There
are trenches between Nieuport and the
French border such as the most imaginative
engineer-officer never pictured to himself
before this war began, and if, at devastating
cost in men, they are taken, there is still, so
far as Britain is concerned, the Channel.
The moment for the invasion of England, if
jt ever existed, has passed. The still sum-
mer days and nights have gone, and in a
little while the equinoctial gales, in which
the cumbrous Zeppelins cannot live, will have
arrived. _ The submarine campaign, which
was to insure the success of a landing in
England by sea, is a failure —an admitted one
if the new German attitude towards the
U.S.A. and the slighting by the Kaiser of
Yon Tirpitz go for anything. An Admir-
alty device has caused the seas around Britain
to be death-traps for enemy submarines,
and the Germans have apparently wearied
of losing costly ships and highly-trained
crews to no warlike purpose. Germany has
long since passed its highest point of effici-
ency—a remark which applies also, unfor-
tunately, to the whole of its enemies, barring
Italy, on the Continent—-but Britain was
stronger in August than it has been since
the war started, and its power is increasing
daily. If a German army had had the
incomparable luck to get a footing in Eng-
land this time last year, it might have had
a lighting chance for its life. If the impos-
sible happened, and it got there now, its end
would be certain and awful.
* *. * . *
It is true that the Russian steam-roller
has not proceeded to Berlin in the manner
expected of it. If the rate at which it is
being pushed in the wrong direction is any
criterion it will never see Potsdam. But
there are compensations. The enemy of the
civilised world is destroying itself in the
effort to destroy Russia, which is indestruct-
ible ; and meanwhile the Britisher, who is
able to regard the progress of the war with
the cold and far-seeing eye of the business
person, may console himself permissibly with
at least one reflection. If Russia had been
as strong as the blitherers averred and
hoped, if it had been capable of routing the
land-forces of what everybody regarded as
the world’s greatest military power, it might
have been well for the Allies’ cause, but it
would probably have been ill, in the long run,
for the British Empire. National aspira-
tions live longer than the most firmly welded
alliances. At the end of the war Britain’s
hold on India and British interests in Persia
would have been in no wise strengthened by
the circumstance that Russia, in addition to
its natural resources and its strength on a
population basis, was efficient enough in the
military sense to over-run Western Europe.
These are natural and obvious sentiments
which need not conflict in any way with
sympathy and warm admiration for a brave
ally. Similarly there is no escaping the fact
that while Continental Europe is being
bankrupted of men and trade, Britain is
doing business more or less as usual, while
the casualty lists have barely affected so far
the fringe of its men-power. This is not in
any sense Britain’s fault, though the mad-
dened Teuton would naturally like the world
to think that it is. Its navy is so strong
that no one can face it; its army, which was
raised by voluntary methods, is still so raw
that for the most part it can face no one.
The regular British Army went cheerfully
to its doom the moment the neutrality of
Belgium was violated, and the Territorials
have followed. But Kitchener’s army of
three millions or more continues to be trained
and augmented, and it is going to supply the
men who will end the trouble.
* * * *
Here, then, are a few plain facts for the
calamity-howlers and for those who would
encourage Germany to now talk of peace
terms. The Empire’s sea-power, which was
in question five years ago, and from then
onwards till July, 1914, is shown to be abso-
lute. The German navy has been exposed
as the most ruinously bad investment in
history. Britain has begun to acquire
the two virtues which it most needed—
sobriety and economy. Intra-Imperial rows
have been either ended or patched up for
the currency of the war. Irishmen, Boers,
Indians —all are struggling fraternally for
the common end. Class feeling, which all
the palliative legislation of the Radicals was
doing little to stem, has died wholesale on
the battlefield. Barnacles, from the Party
system to the;, superstition that a working-
man couldn’t become a useful officer, have
been dropping off the ship of state to its
ultimate great benefit. And the nation con-
tinues to be a gigantic manufacturer and
trader. The measure of the Empire’s strength
is the world’s weakness. When this war is over
no European nation except Britain, sitting
inviolate and inviolable behind the Channel,
will be in a position to handle the biagest
undertakings. For example, the administra-
tion of Turkey. Seapower; Britain’s ad-
vantage in being guarded by a moat; cer-
tain lucky chances, such as the circumstance
that Germany decided on war before the
invasive air-craft had arrived —these are
some of the factors which promise to. still
further swell the Empire. Emphatically
there is nothing in the situation to squeal
about.
NATIONAL SERVICE.
Senator Lynch looks the big question in
the face: —
Dear Bulletin,- —Between voluntary service and its
alternative there is no compromise, no neutral ground
where the perplexed theorist may harmonise the
virtues of both extremes and indicate a middle course.
It is either voluntary? service based on individual
choice, or national service which leaves the individual
no choice.
Our present system is a British inheritance, having
a definite starting point, but which long usage has
changed from an accident to a principle. Had the
British Isles, instead of being encircled by a friendly
sea, been jambed up to the European mainland, with
its rulers never free from the nightmare of invasion,
we would probably never have heard of a voluntary
army. Chance circumstance, therefore, has exalted
voluntary service towards the level of a moral virtue.
Its advocates think it is Truth itself, and any rival
scheme must originate in mental darkness. On
closer examination, however —and it is only in war-
time the supreme test can be applied and results
brought strikingly home —-this sturdy relic of mouldy
medievalism reveals itself as an impostor and a
fraud —as big a fraud as the twin superstition that
“an Englishman’s home is his castle.”
In Australia respect survives for the volunteer
method, doubtless due to hereditary influence, though
the average person seems unaware or unmindful that
for the purpose of Defence it has been struck out of
our plan. Conscription is written in bold characters
in our Defence law to-day. There it stands by popu-
lar choice, as silently enacted as the movements of
the gods who were shod in wool. To apply the prin-
ciple of that law to the conduct of a war waged in
Australia and deny it to the present death-struggle in
Europe suggests one of at least two awkward conclu-
sions. Either Australia has but an incidental in-
terest in the distant contest and adjusts its attitude
accordingly, or it has a vital interest, but has no
faith in its own matured policy. The first finds its
contradiction in the all-pervading opinion on the war.
The second cannot be seriously urged. How then
can the impasse be dodged? Only by the conclusion
that Australia identifies the Allies’ cause as her own,
but has not yet screwed her determination to the
point where effort should match her deep understand-
ing of the position.
Are we doing our share, then? Recalling the
primitive and chaotic attempt at effective defence so
late as five years ago, this country has already earned
a long chapter in the annals of military achievement.
But comparing our quota of 100,000 men not yet
mustered with Britain’s reputed 3,000,000 trained
men, we are seriously under-insured, and need to
more than double our efforts to avoid the reproach
of shirking our part. It is clear that unless volun-
tary enlistment gives unexpected proof of its efficacy,
and does it at once, national service is inescapable if
this country keeps in the war and resolves to bear its
due share of the burden.
But conscription! No, not conscription! Con-
scription shocks a lot of people who are joyously un-
conscious of its virtual operation around them, where
hosts of young men are being discharged and plainly
told to go and enlist. Conscription they detest on
account of its birthplace, as though the Britisher had
always lived a segregated existence and never bor-
rowed for the chief elements in his national life.
The truth, on the contrary, is he has been so in-
veterate a borrower that much of himself is Con-
tinental. The foundation of his laws is Continental;
likewise a great part of his language and literature.
His architecture is wholly so, while his wearing
apparel never gets the proper hang until the
despised foreign touch is applied. Even at meal time
he discards native fashion and prefers alien language
and style. Being such a borrower of foreign condi-
tions that change, the wonder is that he balks at
borrowing a thing that is so well approved that it
has undergone least change of all.
Again, conscription is against the “genius” of the
race. It is true the manhood of Britain as a whole
has given a magnificent response to the call of duty.
It is equally true a big section of it has not done
so. Women in thousands (honor to them !) are going
into laborious and dangerous occupations—-including
harvesting, ploughing and mechanical control —to
free the nobler animal for his fit and bounden duty.
Yet the noble animal hesitates. The “genius” of a
race that permits such hesitation in the face of all-
round superhuman sacrifices has outlived its useful-
ness, and ought to be locked up under the Vagrancy
Act.
It is such sentimental objections as these that have
for ages put conscription outside practical discussion.
Alongside may be placed some of its positive advan-
tages.
Conscription as a means of mobilising a nation’s
fighting strength is equitable, scientific and patriotic.
It begins with the recognition that a country’s
defence is the duty of the physically fit male' popula-
tion living in it. The man who is not prepared to
take his allotted part should be not in it, but out-
side it. National defence, being a common duty,
knows no self-elected privileged class. We inake no
such distinctions in the comparatively minor activities
of civil life. A citizen must join in a common scheme for
defending the community against invasions of disease.
But when his country is in jeopardy from an armed
enemy he is told that he can please himself about
defending it. Compulsory service reverses that order.
It cancels the individual’s pleasure, and so adjusts
the burden than an equitable balance is maintained
on the shoulders built to bear it. The system is
scientific, ensuring the methodical assembling of the
nation’s fighting strength, and enabling that strength
to be used without waste of blows. The fatal element
of uncertainty in raising reinforcements is removed,
and the cruel sacrifice of life in launching a slender
force against overwhelming odds is minimised. The
volunteer system is uncertain and unreliable, and
dangerous because it is both.
And the services of married men would not be
accepted until dire necessity demanded it. In Aus-
tralia there are now about 12,000 fatherless homes
that need not be. As to the number of dependents that
will be thrown on the State for maintenance, and the
varied effects of such a vast multitude so circum-
stanced, it is unhappily premature to speculate. But
with 500,000 single men of military age and a sane
policy in operation, the injurious economic and social
effects produced by this large and growing draft on
our married population could be obviated.
Finally, conscription makes an equal levy on all
shades of patriotic feeling. If there is one thing that
a nation needs to jealously guard it is the spirit of
its people. Courage, fortitude and the other natural
virtues, with honesty as the seminal element, make a
national character of an enviable type. The slopes of
Gallipoli bear witness to our success in approaching
that type. Compared with it, the fertility of soil,
mineral riches or teeming millions of people are
deceptive guides to a nation’s might. It is on this
golden national asset that the vicious, stupid volun-
tary system lays excessive toll. The brave, fearless,
patriotic spirits are beckoned onward to be sacrificed
to the Moloch of war, while the indifferent or selfish,
sluggish or cynical, remain at home in safety. Is it
a wise policy that makes such a suicidal drain on our
best blood? How can the spirit of the race be main-
tained at high standard if its best constituents are to
be deliberately marked for destruction, and the race
propagated from the inferior remainder? It can-be
done by reversing the laws of heredity, not other-
wise.
Even at this advanced stage the call is to the
people to lay aside their reverence for old shibbo-
leths and brace themselves for the task of performing’
their full part in the war, until an honorable and
lasting peace is secured, with justice to our com-
rades in the struggle, and justice to the future of the
race. —P. J. Lynch.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Buying Back Australian Soil with P.N.'s
Apparently the new doctrine will be that
as Australia floated many loans before the
war to keep men employed and launched
God knows how many more to send men
away to fight, things would be nicely-
rounded off by liberating a large supply
when the soldiers come marching home.
For a start the Federal Government
has invested J. C. Watson with a
roving commission to organise employ-
ment for the returned men. Without
question there is useful work for
Watson to do in this direction. Accord-
ing to a statement he made in Sydney the
other day, he seems to intend impressing
upon the State Governments the wisdom of
making land available for his new charges.
The N.S.W. Government is stated to be sym-
pathetic and ready to do something—with
a p.n. The idea given expression to is that
the dreadful old humbug of buying out big-
landowners should be put into operation,
and the areas so acquired cut up and thrown
open to soldiers. In other words the State
Governments are to enter upon a huge
scheme of land resumption with long-dated
bills. Which means an inflation of land
values against the public, the lining of land-
owners’ pockets with gold, the creation of
more mortgage-ridden cockies, and huge
additions to the public debt.' If Watson
is to go round Australia counselling
public policy of this sort, he is going to do
no good either for his friends or the soldiers
or for the people who are not soldiers. Every-
where they have been tried these easy-looking
schemes of land resumption have proved
not only futile but a cause of serious em-
barrassment to Governments and to people
and impossible of continuance. They repre-
sent political opportunism of the cheapest
kind, throw wide open the door to patron-
age and graft, and rest not on the secure
foundation of honest work but on debt —
are welcomed with joy by every boodler
and monopolist in the community they are
foisted upon. Has Labor wholly forgotten
that it has a plank in its platform about
land values? Is it going to scrap the best
of all instruments for causing subdivision?
Expensive schemes of resumption and traf-
ficking in p.n.’s, whether for soldiers or
others, can only be embarked upon by men
who have no faith in land taxation. But
surely the strangling of Labor policy might
be left to its confessed opponents.

Blogg and Co.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The people who have cause to know
About the fate of Blogg and Co.
Are not at all prepared to say
That methods old, and cut, and dried
By long tradition will provide
Always the best and safest way
To manage things. They even might
Declare the old ways were not right.
Blogg was “a man of great ideers.”
In the beginning, it appears,
He gathered up a crowd with cash
Prepared to trade in any way
In everything from gold to clay
(The scheme made in its time a splash),
A sort of minor trust; and so
Emerged the firm of Blogg and Co.
Now Blogg, whose head was full of dreams.
Who revelled in peculiar schemes—
A very curious sort of man —
Propounded for the firm’s success
A system new: the business
Upon the British party plan
He thought might best be run. Said he,
“This is how it occurs to me!”
Well, Blogg had hopes of cornered wheat,
While Blither’s instincts turned to meat;
And Blogg with vigor fought to get
Men well informed on wheat and chaff
Elected to the office staff;
While Blither, who had quite a set
On Blogg’s pet scheme, sought men replete
With knowledge of the trade in meat.
Each canvassed all the folk he knew
Amongst shareholders, till there grew
A sort of feud between them; they
Held meetings and explained with heat
The vital claims of corn and meat.
Yet, on the staff election day,
So even did the voting run,
Blogg’s faction got most votes by one!
The office staff at once got down
To business. A .chap called Brown
Secured the managerial seat
By just one vote; he hoisted then
To all the highest billets men
Who were well up in chaff and wheat;
And, calling in the office staff,
Proposed a policy of chaff.
The Blither’s fancy man, Jake Jones,
Then rose and said, in tragic tones, _
That it to him seemed scarcely right
To plunge into a deal in chaff,
When all but half the office staff
Opposed that madness. He “would fight
This wild idea.” He was “appalled.”
“A general meeting should be called !”
The staff then argued many days.
Each side its case in many ways
Put forward ; till at last from Brown
A junior clerk (his name was Bones)
Deserted to the side of Jones.
Then, on the votes, Brown’s case went
down !
(The staff, while this was going through.
Did nothing much but draw its screw.)
Brown then the managerial chair
Gave up to Jones, who, seated there,
Picked out men well informed in meat
For’ all the top-notch billets. Jones,
Alas ! forgot the youngster Bones,
Who nursed the slight with angry heat.
His vengeful vote to Brown he shied.
Down came Jones’ castles and his pride.
The wild shareholders then uprose
To solve the problem bellicose,
And, more in anger than in hope,
They balloted again. Alack !
They got the same old gangsters back
Except young Bones, who had to slope.
But when the voting was all done,
The Blither faction scored by one!
The thing went on for months, until
The mad shareholders had their fill.
But, oh ! it was too late to save
Much from the wreck! ’Tis best that you
Praise not the party system to
Those ruined men, or to the grave
You will go swiftly. Blogg and Co.
In liquidation had to go.
N.S.W.
HAMER.


most of our industrial legislation, and a
hundred other things were uncalled for, so
it is claiming now that the Constitution
does not require to be changed. The bleat
of every Conservative paper is that the
Federal Parliament already has full power
to organise the Commonwealth for war pur-
poses. For example, the S. M. Herald
U/9/T5) :—•
The Prime Minister added, in his reply to Mr.
Cook, that the amendments would add to the security
of the Commonwealth. Mr. Fisher should give one
single instance to show how the Commonwealth will
be strengthened if these Bills are carried.
Yet in issue after issue, during the preceding
10 days, this same journal had been showing
in its news-columns the impotence of the
Government in certain matters intimately
related to Defence. In the munition areas
of the United Kingdom the National Govern-
ment has compelled all public houses to
remain closed till 4 pan. The British
Government was able to do this out of hand,
since its authority is as great in Lanark-
shire as it is in Yorkshire or Essex. The
Australian Defence Department is equally
anxious to assail the drink problem. The
Commissioner whom it appointed to inquire
into the state of the Liverpool Camp recom-
mended that “all hotels should be closed
against recruits” ; also that no liquor should
be sold near Liverpool. But this is a matter
i.a which, under our Constitution, the Fede-
ration lias to wait upon the States. We
find therefore that in Westralia the Assembly
is debating in a leisurely manner the holding
of a referendum on the question of shorten-
ing the trading hours of the public houses;
that in South Australia a Licensing Bill,
closing all liquor bars at 6 p.m. as from
March 26, 1916, has just gone to the Legis-
lative Council; that in N. S. Wales nothing
at all is to be done —lest, as the Sydney
Telegraph (25/8/T5) put it, “the big
brewing interest which can hire motor-cars
for elections should suffer”; and so on.
The National Government can’t hurry up or
in any way coerce the State politicians in
this matter, and the State politicians are not
going to do anything in a rash and hasty
manner which may cost them votes. A few
days ago the Australian Government stopped
the export of meat from N. S. Wales. The
idea was that it was needed for consump-
tion elsewhere in Australia. The N. S.
Wales Chief Secretary’s response was a
threat of a writ and an action for damages.
Victoria has. been buying, poor and dirty
wheat from the U.S.A., in part at least
because N. S. Wales was in a position,
despite the fact that the Commonwealth is
at war, to treat the fellow-Australian next
door as an enemy subject. In a similar
spirit Victoria has been refusing to let its
wheat go to South Australia, and Queens-
land has heen hanging on to its meat. When
a private citizen is attacked, he needs to
have his arms free, if he is to avert disaster,
and the same applies to a nation. The Aus-
tralian Government is debarred under the
Constitution from protecting Australia in
numberless directions such as those above
mentioned. If the Referenda questions, are
accepted at the end of the year the position
will be relieved. But they may be turned
down. And in any event a lot of valuable
time and money must he lost. The
Bulletin’s alternative —namely, a Bill
conferring on Parliament all the requisite
powers for the duration of the war , the
measure to be at once validated in toto by
the High Court, as provided for by the Judi-
ciary Act —affords a reasonable compromise.
It would be adopted were it not that both
parties are battling, in tbe name for patri-
otism, to score a political triumph.
“Mammon, Cast Not Thy Servants Out
Lest They Perish.”
By a majority of nine votes the N.S.W.
Assembly decided last week to swallow the
Norton-Gkiffiths contract. Therefore the
shindykit can now go ahead in peace and
comfort to spend the money the State will
borrow for it, and to draw commission and
salaries and otherwise behave after the man-
ner of shin'dykits. Holman’s speech in
justification of the awful bargain was on the
lines of the familiar wail that comes from
the deadbeat called upon to explain his bul-
bous nose and devastated boots. The essence
of it all was that he had been
living from hand to mouth, borrow-
ing here and cadging there; that he
had to get money somehow; and that
unless he kept on piling up the debt many
people would be thrown out of employment.
The merits of this contract —the des-
pairing rush with the family blankets to the
pawnshop —are no longer subjects of discus-
sion, for the best of all reasons that there
are no merits. Even the half-truths, down-
right perjuries and assorted subterfuges which
served the purposes of a few months ago,
have been abandoned by their originators.
All the yarns about Norton Griffiths and
Co. and the vast capital behind them and the
terms on which the borrowing was to proceed
have gone into the ash-barrel, and the trans-
action stands naked (or almost naked) for
what it really is—the final step in the de-
moralisation of a Parliamentary party. The
last rag of principle has been thrown to the
winds. Even the disreputable old gags about
“developing the national resources,” increas-
ing the public wealth, and all the rest, of it
have been abandoned, and the confession is
made that after all these years Labor finds
itself compelled to rely for sustenance on
Cohen —that without a regular dole from
Fat the people cannot earn their daily bread!
And this in a State richly endowed with
almost everything required by man, with its
lands calling for fuller occupation, and
warehouses crammed with imported goods.
According to Holman, the unhappy feature
of the transaction is that “it means depart-
ure from the principles of day labor.” If
this were the only lump of coke in the
damper, toleration would not be impossible.
The fundamental disaster is that a pro-
longed orgy of boom and bloat, of
pandering to parasitism in its worst
form, has brought the State to a
pass at which constituted authority finds
itself compelled to import at enormous cost
a speculator to hold the
tottering superstructure up. The fooling of
Labor by the politicians having finished, they
are now fooling themselves. And this is
their trinity—Cohen, Ragtown and Shindy-
kit!
Critics and the Law.
“Garnishor” on critics, libellous and other-
wise :—-
The Sydney journalist who recovered substantial
damages against a critic of his book was in luck i
one measures the result by the cases. The critic is
given remarkable latitude by the law. That whic i
would be ruinous if published of a grocer s buttei
may safely be written of a piece of painting, or wilt-
ing, or sculpture. In these matters “every latitu e
must be given to opinion and to prejudice. Meie
exaggeration or even gross exaggeration would no
make the comment unfair. However wrong the
opinion expressed may be in point of truth, or
however prejudiced the writer, it may still be wit in
the prescribed limit.” Lord Justice Bowen said
that in such matters it must be assumed that man
is entitled to entertain any opinion he pleases, how-
ever wrong, exaggerated or violent it may be.
Here are some examples of the lengths to which
critics of various sorts of written works have been
allowed to go. It was said of a publication called
the True Briton that “it was the most vulgar,
ignorant and scurrilous journal ever published in
Great Britain.” The Times was sued for saying
this; but Lord Chief Justice Kenyon held that the
words could not be libellous. Handbills distributed
by tradesmen are open to the comment of the critic.
Of a marine-store dealer’s handbill a writer once
said that it was “pernicious in its effects, as offering
great inducements to servants to rob their masters ;
but the dealer lost the action which he brought,
despite the fact that in addition to other abusive
remarks there was a flaming headline which ran:
“Encouraging Servants to Rob their Masters.” Many
such examples might be quoted ; but these two are
sufficient to illustrate the contrast with the Sydney
libel, the salient part of which described the book
that was the subject of it as “the hastily strung-
together extracts from a copious but not very intel-
ligently constructed series of tales.”
It would be fair criticism to say of this sentence
that it is so badly constructed that most schoolboys
would be ashamed of having written it. It is nearly
meaningless. But it has probably just enough of
the right sort of meaning to contain the bacillus of
libel. The test is this. Are the words an attack
upon the personal character of the writer instead of
an attack upon the literary quality of his hook?
This plaintiff’s case was that the words branded him
as a literary thief, and it was in virtue of this con-
tention that he got his verdict. He said that if you
took away the adverbs and adjectives which bind the
words together, you were left with a statement that
his book was “extracts,” and nothing else. In such
cases it is a question for the Judge whether the
words can be libellous in law, and then for the jury
whether in fact they are so. Tfie Judge was in-
vited to nonsuit the plaintiff, but he was probably
right in saying that the words could raise the issue
of fact which the plaintiff said they raised, and of
course if they could raise such an issue the jury
alone was competent to pronounce as to whether they
did.
The interest the critic has for lawyers is that a
variation of the law on “Fair Comment” is made in
his favor. The phrase “fair comment” as applied
to the everyday sort of libel has led many an earnest
young editor into trouble. He interprets it to mean
that any comment is fair; whereas a plea of “fair
comment” is on exactly the same footing as a plea
of justification. In “justification” your defence
alleges that the words are true, and in “fair -com-
ment” it alleges the same thing, and goes on to say
that in those circumstances your comment on the
facts was fair. It is only common-sense. If the law
were otherwise a writer could set up any old pack
of lies about a public man and then start comment-
ing on the lies. None of these technicalities apply
to the critic. He really can comment just as he
pleases; and as long.as he keeps clear of an author’s
personal character and confines himself to objurga-
tions of the author’s books, then any comment is
fair.

Our Pampered Scourge.
Dr. Ralph Worrall, a Sydney specialist in
women’s diseases, gave evidence last week
before the usual old Parliamentary Select
Committee which spends diligent weeks in-
quiring into the already known. Most of
what he had to say about the appalling
effects of syphilis is now platitudinous; but
speaking as a gynaecologist he made some
comparatively fresh disclosures. He men-
tioned that about 80 per cent, of still births
and 50 per cent, of cases of sterility in
women were due to venereal disease. He said
that a large proportion of patients were
innocent married women who had been in-
fected by their husbands, and who came to
the doctor not knowing what was wrong
with them. “The disease,” he went on, “is
quite a common thing among married
women of all classes.” He considered that
it should be a criminal offence to spread
venereal disease; that the Registrar should
register no marriage unless it was accom-
panied by a certificate of health on the part
of the man ; and that the Rathburn pamphlet
and accessories should be made generally
available. llow typical of the professional
attitude towards this scourge! The doctors
are genuinely anxious to see syphilis effec-
tively tackled, though success must mean
severe inroads upon their incomes. But
there is a point beyond which''few of them
are prepared to advance. Here is one who
will make criminals of people for spreading
disease; who will deny diseased people the
right to marry; who is prepared, in short,
to convulse the whole of our social system
in order to minimise this horror, but who
fears to recommend the one course which
will wipe it right out. If the authorities had
attacked bubonic plague when it appeared
in Australia 15 years ago by prescribing
hard labor for every citizen who —perhaps
unwittingly—passed it on to someone else,
our great cities might to-day be fair imi-
tations of London in Charles Ilfs reign.
The exposure of bottles of formalin, with
instructions how to use it, and a pamphlet
on Rats, would have done little to check the
epidemic. What the doctors, with the
eager concurrence of the politicians, did was
to fall on the plague with utter ruthlessness.
No one’s feelings were considered. If a
visiting duke had developed it during break-
fast he would have lunched the same day in
a lock hospital. If the most costly house
in the Commonwealth had been shown to be
insanitary or rat-infested, alterations would
have been required of its owners on the
spot. Under this treatment the plague
languished and died. The same applies,
more or less, to most other serious infectious
and contagious diseases. Small-pox, typhus,
diphtheria-all would be Taging. b risldy now
lint for the one measure which, m regaru
S soil's, the doctors hate to —i
and which the politicians fear to take, lhat
measure is compu sory notification It s
far more merciful m the long:um. to s( igrc
e-ate a svphilitic than to render him or ner
Sable to gaol; it is obviously better to wipe
out a disease which makes a or a
tragedy, or both, of marriage, than to deal
with it through a Registrar; and to talk of
preventives and prophylactics in sudi a con-
nection is tantamount to trying to put out
a bush fire with a garden hose. .1 his
disease which, on the word of the doctors, is
devitalising the race, will not get easier to
attack as the years pass. What just tic
tion have the State Governments foi' not
attacking it, in a serious spmt, now .
How It Is Done in Japan.
From “Morimoto,” an old dweller in “the
East” : ,
I am writing this with a fountain pen made m
Japan, bought there for 18 sen—4 s d. And it is a
perfectly efficient little pen, with an imitation gold
nib and rubber-and-glass syringe for cnarging, put
„p in a neat cardboard box. I have been using
it side by side with an English pen costing over
15 times more, and line for line it behaves as well
as the expensive article. A fountain pen foi 4jd. .
This is not the only article Japan is making nearly
as good as its European counterpart, and at a
fraction of the cost. In a country village I bougot
pocket-books in imitation morocco for 24 d. and
lead-pencils for a farthing; and once when it
rained I secured for 7*d. an umbrella which not
only kept off the rain but carried an invitation
to "the gods to abstain from hurling thunderbolts
at me. Then, at an art shop in Kyoto, I bought
for 2Jd. color prints by Hydeyoshine which are a
priceless possession and a joy for ever. The beauty
of a bottle made from a single piece of wood, with
a strip of bark left on the outside, captivated me.
It cost 3id. I bought for 6fd. a pencil-case ex-
quisitely inlaid. It was impossible not to buy when,
the simplest articles were, by the simplest of means,
made beautiful —when nothing was so small as to be
unworthy of the artistic touch.
But one could not help thinking that behind all
this clieap-and-good production there must be
black forests of factory chimneys, peopled by narrow-
chested children and mothers with child bending
over machinery and coughing and spitting amongst
the dust of manufacture. And so it is. A new
industrial era is only just dawning in China. In
Japan it is at high noon. But in her leap at civili-
sation Japan has got no further than the Victorian
era. She has innumerable factories, but no effec-
tive factory legislation. Half a million females are
employed in factories and mills in Japan, and
300,000 of them are under the age of 20 years. In
most of the mills the employees are lodged within
the mill-compound, which they are not allowed to
leave except on special occasions. Here they work
for 14 to 16 hours per day; here they spend what
little time is left for recreation, sleep and meals.
The beds are never aired, for each has to accommo-
date four persons, two on the day shift and two on
the night shift. Tuberculosis is rampant. Children
under 15 years work from 66 to 78 hours per week.
If Sunday should be treated as a holiday at the
factory the employer is permitted to extract two
hours more work per night to make up for the lost
day. Two hundred thousand girls are annually
“recruited,” and it is reckoned that only 80,000 of
them return to their homes after their period of
labor is completed. Of the other 120,000 most
drift into other factories; many into prostitution.
Japanese employers are obsessed with the idea,
exploded in most Western countries, that the
longer the hours of labor the greater must be the
output. School children persist at their lessons for
SO hours a week, as if their country’s life depended
on it. Officials hang about their desks for nine
hours a day and get through work that could
equally well be done in five. These are all phases
of the exhausting effort Japan is making, on her
poor soil, with all her self-sacrificing patriotism, to
catch up to Western civilisation, which had a
thousand years’ start.
. Conscripting the Volunteer.
“Whitehall” introduces a friend: —
It is the correct thing to spurn' -the female who
distributes white feathers to those young men whom
she has reason to believe are''afraid to 1 go to the war.
But why? The white -feather, in one or other of
its is an inevitable corollary of
the voluntary-service system. Voluntary service, in
time of national peril, amounts to conscription of the
self-respecting. The of our young men are
already fighting. The self-respecting ones that are
not fighting have some good reason —a family, ill-
health, poor physique—for being where they are. In
any event they, like the best of ottr elderly men
and our women of all ages, are helping with money,
with personal service, and in other ways. The type
that, in about 90 per cent, of cases, does nothing
whatever for his country is the young waster who,
though fit to serve, has no more desire or intention
to do so than he has to poison himself.
A case in point is my more or less youthful
acquaintance Algernon Brigges. He lives on an
allowance from his people. He is a fair golfer and
an average tennis player. From time to time he
gets drunk. When the war started he said that
those who had volunteered—the men who are now
in Gallipoli —were posing humbugs who knew that
they would never smell powder. For about 10
months thereafter he said that he would gladly go
only he knew the trouble would be over before he
could get there. Latterly he has come round to the
view that every man is wanted. But he won’t
enlist in Australia. His idea is, at some time in
the dim and unspecified future, to “go to England
and fight with his equals.”
This young man —and there are thousands of him -
is being handed white feathers every day. He finds
them in the paper at breakfast. His girl friends
present them to him in all innocence by means of
remarks such as: “Were you the Mr. Brigges who
enlisted last week?” “Father said that you had only
joined a rifle club, but I told him it was the A.I.F.
I was right, wasn’t I?” And so forth. The recruit-
ing politicians shower them on him as he hurries
past their platform. Nice old ladies give them to
him with kindly diffidence, and irritable old gentle-
men dash them rudely in his face.
What does it matter that they have not .been
plucked from the fowl? The principle which is at
the root of the voluntary-service system—namely,
that those who won’t fight of their own accord must
be shamed into fighting—is the same. There are young
men whom a thoughtless glance or intonation of the
voice will send in wild haste to the. nearest recruit-
ing dep6t. There are thousands who have needed
no push at all. There are others who will go readily
as soon as the fact that they are really needed is
put to them in an effective way. But there are
many—the Brigges type—who require the 4.7 gun of
argument which is contained in the universal symbol
of cowardice personally aimed by one who is in a
position to hit the mark. What if the weapon does
occasionally wound the wrong man ? The injury
cannot hurt him much in the nature of things.
What no one can question is the effectiveness of its
work in the right quarter. And so long as we are
satisfied to recruit our armies by defying our men
to face the ignominy of staying at home, no enlist-
ment weapon which does what is asked of it should
be barred.
4.
When Australia last week received the
body of General Bridges' it got the biggest
lesson it is ever likely to receive on the value
of straight rifle-shooting. Bridges was
some Turk’s carefully-selected -victim; and
when the fine soldier fell the brain of the
Australian division temporarily ceased to
work. It is hard to understand how any
intelligent soldier can bring himself to
under-rate the importance of straight shoot-
ing with the small arm; yet there is—or
was until recently—a section which argued
more or less vaguely against it. As if
mediocrity could ever be anything but a
disadvantage! The danger of that theory
was that it provided a liandy excuse for
sending partially-trained riflemen to com- •
pete with the sharpshooting Turks —those
enthusiastic collectors of Australian identi-
fication discs. However, after a few months’
hard fighting, the school of theorists who do
not place, much value on straight individual
shooting but put their trust in “the volume
of fire,” must have thinned out considerably.
Now another fashion has arisen. Possibly
because One general has expressed the
opinion that two men with a machine-gun
could hold up a division armed with rifles,
some phrase-maker has dubbed the rifle “the
infantryman’s toy.” It is a silly gibe at
best, and the lesson stands good. The Turk
who did such valuable service to his side by
dropping the Australian commander could
not have lugged a machine-gun with him any
more than the Frenchman who shot
Nelson could have dragged a cannon up the
rigging of his ship. Because they each pos-
sessed the necessary skill their “toys” proved
deadly to the men they were turned against;
and the victims were singled out (as
only the marksmen can single men out)
because they were leaders whose loss might
conceivably mean everything.
4.
The distribution of funds such as have been
raised in Australia during the last year
always gives rise to controversy. The ex-
perience is that in most cases the adminis-
tration is narrow and penurious, and the
fund frequently outlives the purpose for
which it was raised. Generally speaking,
it is best to distribute the money quickly;
and this idea is at the back of much that is
being chorused now about various patri-
otic and Red Cross collections. The
Bulletin has already said that these funds
are to spend and not to - hoard, and that
present necessities should not be turned
down simply because there may be future
necessities. Yet liberality must for the pre-
sent be tempered by prudence. In the case
of an ordinary disaster the worst is usually
known at once. But in relation to the war
and the war funds the problems of to-day
are not to be taken as the measure of those
that • will be on- hand a year or two years
hence. Therefore.in cutting up the joint
it is well to at least recollect that the job
will he done best when the carver sees how
many plates are to be passed along. And
though no one can yet tell what that num-
ber will be, most people know that the
capacity of Australians to respond volun-
tarily or by compulsion to future demands
is being materially altered.
»
If any member of the Federal Parliament
feels that he may do so without offending
the Party Joss, he could do good service to
Australia by securing some up-to-date in-
formation from the Defence Minister about
the age groups of men (1) serving in the
army, (2) wounded, killed or sick. The
purpose of this information would be to see
how the lads_of 18 and 20, whom Australia
has been sending away, have been getting
on. Colonel Mackesy, a Maoriland officer,
states that experience of the campaign has
shown that the enlistment of youngsters
should be discouraged, if only on military
grounds, and that effort should be directed
towards sending forward a more mature
article.. The boys, says Mackesy, crack up
too soon under fatigue, and lack judgment
and restraint where those qualities mean
everything. He merely says what might
have been expected. A lad of this age is-,
not likely to be as cautious as a. man. a
little older; and not only about his skin,,
but in regard to health and so forth. His
considerable ignorance seems likely to be-
come a danger; his enthusiasm to rujf on to
recklessness. It would therefore be good to.
know how the adolescent Australian is actu-
ally faring, more especially as a considerable
number of his elder brothers are sheltering
behind the kid’s knapsack, and might like to
mobilise themselves if they heard that the
youngster was getting hurt more plentifully
than was good for him.
Red Sunday.
(APRIL 25, 1915.)
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Fame watched Australia as she dreamless
Remote**in the unstoried South. He cried :
“Where shall her soul be born?” and
searched the wide
Spaces of earth. “What place but Troy,
where wept—
And still weeps—Helen, or the strait that
kept .
From Persian hordes all Europe and denied
Xerxes —the Hellespont that in his stride
To conquer India Alexander leapt?”
So on that calm, momentous April morn,
That sacred soil where Agamemnon stood,
By this new race, united and made whole,
Was made more sacred with Australian
blood.
Blind Homer thrilled, remembering, while
that soul
In pride and hitter agony was born.

The Long, Long Fight.
Tiie gibe of the First Lord of the Admi-
ralty that the German’s pledge to Uncle Sam
in regard to submarine warfare rested on
recognition rather than on repent-
ance of murder, would have been more to
the _ point if the launching of torpedoes
against passenger ships had been stopped.
But as the baby-murdering campaign has
been re-opened against a vessel with 900
people aboard, it looks as if the Hun is still
seeing red. Either that or he has not been
able to yet give all liis cut-throats amended
'riding instructions. The difficulty of reach-
ing them is, of course, considerable, so old
v on Tirpitz may get into even more serious
trouble with Hollweg and the Kaiser and
such diplomats as the Huns possess. There
is good reason, however, to believe that there
is more than meets the eye in Balfour’s
veiled statement about the campaign under
the water, and that the Hoggenzollern has
found that frightfulness can overleap itself.
lor a month or so the submarine attack
was successful in a brutal, savage sort of
way ; but after getting its second wind the
British navy has beer taking from the Hun
a terrible toll. In fact,' the only certain
thing about the war is that on sea Britain
holds the. enemy in a suffocating grasp. If
the position by land was anything like so
good as it is by water, peace would be close
at hand. But Constantinople is still far off
and the Rhine looks no closer. Almost daily
the weary old yarn about the Turk being
in a panic bobs uo from somewhere; but as
ho was. reported to be in the same frame of
mind six months ago and has been fighting
like a demented cat ever since, these vague
assurances- seem entitled to disrespect. The
truth appears to be that unless more men
and spades and crowbars and Ivddite are
available against him, the Mussulman will
continue to be in a panic and hold his
ground. Elsewhere the unpleasantness is
more or less a rumor, though the Bear is
making the German fight hard for every mile
of .wilderness he achieves, and every day the
rains and the bog come nearer. When in the
winter William sits down to hold on to
what he has got, he may find it not unlike
the tail of a tiger. At that stage Round 1
will have ended.
One of the tragic events of last week
was Willy Kelly’s appearance on a patriotic
platform with the proposition that all pre-
sent should pledge themselves not to buy
German goods for five years. And there are
those who complain that we are not fierce
enough in our proposals for punishing Ger-
many!
At the Front Door.
“McNortii,” after a look at the front door:
Ihe people who scoff at (lie idea of attempting to
settle the North with whites are fond of creating the
impression that Port Darwin—which they hold up as
a sample of the whole Territory—is a dilapidated
collection of hovels eaten by white ants and situ-
ated beneath a blistering sun on low-lying, sandy,
flat country, where nothing but miserable, stunted ti-
trees grow. They hint that a general air of depres-
sion fills the gaps between drinks, and say dreadful
things about the enervating effect of the climate on
Europeans. These advocates of a Discolored Aus-
tralia also assert that any attempt to rear children
in such a place is murder. In short, the Territory
(that jjs to say, Darwin) is only a fit habitat for
Chinamen and niggers and super-whites. Now for
some facts. Darwin is situated on a level plateau
i Oft. above the sea, and overlooking it. The town
is laid out in squares. Its wide streets are lined
with banyans and other shade trees. With the ex-
ception of the street known as “Chinatown”—a blot
on an otherwise beautiful place—neat, substantial
bungalows with bamboo-screened verandahs, a;nd
stone-built stores, hotels and Government offices are
the general rule. Green foliage is a feature. I
visited Darwin recently, and will always remember
watching from the deck, as the steamer entered the
harbor, the green and fresh appearance of the in-
digenous flora growing out of the abruptly-sloping
walls of the plateau, with here and there a white-
painted house nestling amongst the trees. This, too,
in the Territory’s “dry” season, when most of that
part of Australia which lies “down South” was
stiuggling in the throes of drought. Darwin’s numer-
ous healthy, sturdy-limbed youngsters—nearly all
local products, some of the third generation—dis-
prove the statement that white women and children
cannot survive the climate. Darwin being on the
coast has a hotter and more humid climate than the
inland country, and for that reason it—and therefore
the rest of the country—is held up by Black Aus-
tralians as being a kind of death-trap for those
Europeans who make a living by anything more
strenuous than banging a typewriter or wielding a
pen. Yet the fact that there are, many hundreds of
white men doing hard manual labor in Darwin hasn’t
created a demand for another hospital or an exten-
sion of the cemetery. These men are just ordinary,
everyday individuals such as may be seen working
in Melbourne or Sydney. By spending £200,000 in
a meat-works at Darwin, Vestey Bros, are giving
evidence of their belief in the ability of white men
to do things in the Territory; and they didn’t reach
that belief without strict inquiry on the spot. And,
as already stated, Darwin hasn’t the best section of
the Territory’s climate. Inland, almost any climatic
condition and degree of temperature can be found.

“High Jinks” returned to Her Majesty’s.
Sydney, on Saturday night. The best work
is done by Australians. Dorothy Brunton
plays Sylvia with the utmost charm and
humor, and Marie Eaton gets some fine
chances to use her large and tuneful voice
and display her considerable acting abilities
as Marion Thorne. Field Fisher is divert-
ing as Dr. Thorne; so are W. H. Rawlins,
as a Yankee lumber king, and Alfred Frith,
who gives a taking representation of a
drunk. Florence Vie is uproarious, and
Gwen Hughes demurely lovely, which is all
they are asked to be. But whoever cast Paul
Plunket as a comic Frenchman when C. H.
Workman was available for the part deserves
to be fined half a crown at least, and who-
ever made C. H. Workman a jeune premier

lady who sews the garments of the chorus
gentlemen : could do with a chiding. They
are uniformly dreadful.
* He
Patriotism and gore are the watchwords
at Sydney Adelphi, where “Our Boys of the
Dardanelles” was staged on Saturday night.
The hero is dismissed from the service with
ignominy for being drunk and disorderly in
the presence of the enemy. As a matter
of fact he has been dosed with Cairo wine,
which is notoriously capable of doing any-
thing to a man. Dejected he trails back to
the family homestead, but is met by Jack
Cosgrove (John is nowadays a colonel)
and spurned. Merely delaying long enough
to swear off alcohol for life, he en-
lists under another name. How he rises to
fame upon the ashes of his dead drunk;
how the Colonel, his father, meets him
again ; how a reconcilement occurs and love
enters into his apparently blasted life —
these are the incidents which W. Hunt,
Ethel Buckley, Frank Crossley and others
depict. The part of Captain Petland, the
German spy, is well played by Frederick
Neilson.

“Mum’s the Word” came td Sydney Royal
on Saturday night to spend a week. This
successor to “Stop Your Nonsense” is a
musical version of that brisk and clever
three-act comedy, “The Chaperon,” produced
first by Beaumont Smith at the Palace some
time last year. The experiment of introduc-
ing songs and danbes isn’t very successful,
simply because there isn’t the room for them,
and, with one or two exceptions, they hold
up the procession just when the audience is
eager to know what is going to happen next.
Still, the complications of the plot aren’t
numerous enough to make the onlooker’s
head buzz, and the end comes with a slam
in the right place, just when the interest has
run out.
Frank Bradley gives another tine exhibi-
tion of doddering. Tom Shelford rattles
along cheerfully as a self-possessed young
man of leisure. Sinna St. Clair presents
a clever study of the aggressive type of
brain-bursting female. Ethel Dane is once
more the disturbing butterfly. And Edmund
Sherras deserves a; round of applause for a
sympathetic rendering of “Somerset,” a
pleasant English ballad. “The Barrier,”
played by a different co., is due on Saturday.

Sydney James, chief of the Strollers, at
the Palace (Sydney), has been off the bill
for some nights owing to throat trouble. As

paper goes to press, wherefore the solitary
anxiety of the week is removed.

“OUR, BOYS OF THE DARDANELLES,”
