In the rush of ; musical-play revivals at
Melbourne Majesty’s an extra week is
allotted to “Our Miss Gibbs,” and the recep-
tion which that fortunate stores-assistant
got from a crowded house last Saturday
seemed to justify the J.C.W. management in
billing her for 12 nights. The revival
doesn’t quite explain why Mary Gibbs from
Yorkshire broke the record in Sydney four
or five years ago-, but it is at least a brighter
show than many another of its sort. The
story hangs together, and is attached to a
notable joke. Hughie, the amateur crook,
who engages a professional to coach him in
burglary and petty larceny, has lost none of
his humor since his satirical meaning faded
away. Raffles is dead, but the absurdity of
the Hon. Hughie remains a live subject, and
his “pinching” of the Ascot Gold Cup is still
a sparkling source of complications. A pur-
pose had to be found for dragging Mary’s
low-comedy cousin Timothy into the pro-
ceedings. Something was necessary to keep
Timothy from degenerating into a conven-
tional nuisance, so he was made to fall a
victim to the wrong kitbag, containing the
Gold Cup and a crowbar, instead of two
dozen long-laid eggs and a property cake
with blobs of ink at the bottom of it. Such
ingenuity of plot is rare in musical comedy.
Judged by ordinary rules of etiquette “Miss
Gibbs” is a masterpiece of construction.

At Melbourne Royal the plump, emphatic
grafter and the tall, calm sleuth are still
baiting their respective traps with a neck-
lace of Paris pearls. “Under Cover” is
only in its second week at present, so the
bloom has not yet been rubbed off its dram-
atic surprises. One of these surprises
amounts to spoof. When the audience dis-
covers a sleeping apartment adjoining Mil -
ward’s airy sitting-room it naturally looks
for something to happen; and when he sheds
his coat a thrill of expectation runs through
the multitude. But lie merely puts himself
into a smoking jacket, and a burst of aston-
ishment follows.

The polite art of home-wrecking is revived
with enthusiasm in “Not Understood,” the
latest attraction at Melbourne King’s. Two
old offenders, known as Griffith Wolfe and
Agnes Trevor, burst up the happy home, of
Paul Fetherstone and his" missus by playing
on the couple’s mutual readiness to believe
the worst of one another. Grif and Agnes
add cayenne to a warm situation by posing
as the successful co-res. in each case, and
later on endeavor to improve their positions
by paying furious addresses to the sundered
couple. But Paul, suddenly blind from
grief, maintains an implacable virtue in
Aggie’s torrid society. Likewise Mrs. Paul
develops a refrigerating rectitude that turns
the ardent but sheepish Griffith into some-
thing resembling frozen mutton. This senti-
mental stalemate continues to the inevitable
reunion in the last act. Two other couples
meantime endeavor to lighten the tedium by
publishing allegedly funny versions of their
own love encounters. Bert Bailey is the
outstanding figure in these proceedings., got
up as a doddering-{philosopher armed with a
clarionet. Miss Bevan’s mannishness is loud
and unrestrained in the part of the maiden
aunt who listens to the doleful strains of
Bailey’s instrument and later . succumbs
to a base canard concerning his wealth.
Arthur Bertram and Miss Roberts presume
to be a filbert and a flapper in a state of
love; but as their “business” has already
been done to death, they may only be said
to be turning over the remains. Guy Has-
tings as the artist Fetherstone loses his
wife and his sight in a manner befitting the
double misfortune; but Miss Adrienne as
Mrs. Fetherstone has small emotional equip-
ment for her job. Careful auditing of the
parts of R. Bellairs (Griffith Wolfe) and
Miss Adeson (Agnes Trevor), also reveals
various deficits. It only remains to mention
Fred Macdonald, who as a superfluous French
Count often reaches a state of frenzy most
dangerous in these explosive times.

The fourth Orchestral Concert of the..
Taits’ series comes due at the Melbourne
Auditorium on Saturday afternoon, with the
Beethoven Symphony in C minor for chief
item, and Laver in front of the band. Two
more appearances of the Brussels Concert
Party are announced, and Saturday night
will see the last of them at the Audi.
Of the new turns at the Melbourne Tivoli
it may be said that everything in the bill is
lively. . Crouch and Welch —the lady being
patriotically economical in her use of
underwear—are fairly good as frivollers and
quite better as acrobatic dancers. May
Erne and He are a musical pair whose man-
ner of work is as odd as their results are
satisfactory, and Walter Weems blackens his
face before coming on to crack wheezes that
seem worth while, as a rule. Other features
of the programme are Ella Caspers, Gold-
spink, Annie Kent, Ida Barr and Stafford
and Co. The Bulletin, when passing com-
plimentary remarks on Stafford last week,
spoke of his trusty dogs as pointers. It
should have called them setters. The dog
responsible for the paragraph gets no bones
with his biscuit for a’week.

“The Midnight Club” is still the noisiest
end of Melbourne Bijou programme, with
Victor Prince and others shouting in concord
or otherwise. Elsewhere the Indian snake-
charmer is in evidence, along with the melo-
dious sundowner and the quick-change lady
billed as Madam Marion. Madam is the
most imposing of the lot, but she has the-
objectionable habit of talking freely in
Amurka’s disagreeable substitute for the
English language.
Last week Hoyt’s (Melbourne) revived in-
terest in John Strange Winter’s “Booties’
Baby,” by screening a free and easy adapta-
tion of that long story. Hoyt’s other big
feature was “Beatris,” a Nordisk study < of
artistic life that hurries to a very pleasing
conclusion. This week Hoyt’s programme is
eloquent of “Mother,” wherein the virtues of
that noble institution are liberally contrasted
with the failings of the Other Sort of Person.
** * *
Jack Cannot is right in his element as
director of the Follies at the Adelaide
Tivoli. He and Isabelle Darmond do some
clever work together, but their burlesque of
“Bought and Paid For” seems to puzzle
Adelaide audiences, who don’t know the play.
Alfredo fiddles, Frank Greene sings, and the
Rego and Stopit team put in some incidental
foolery which is more attractive than their
old open-mouth specialty. It is a bright
show all round. Meanwhile, Brandon Cremer
lias had a merry week at the Royal with
“Charley’s Aunt,” which is old enough to
be new to many. “In the Soup” is to fol-
low, and after that melodrama again, the
tentative Adelaide season having “caught on”
unexpectedly well. At the King’s Daisy
Jerome and Miss Gassman (with piccanin-
nies) have been dropped into the bill, the
brightest remaining feature of which is the
bulky Stevens, who balances precariously on
tottering supports. The Orange Dandies —
Pace, Naomi McQuoin. Roland Henry and
co.—are at the Town Hall, and a Conserva-
torium concert brought a big success for Jack
Fisher, baritone, who is to be given a chance
in Europe.

From an Adelaide critic: —
To Wadham’s one-man show in the Society of
Arts rooms succeeds Will Ashton’s. Will is the son
of James, sound painter of seascapes and successful
teacher. Ashton junior learned what his father could
teach, and has since been in Europe picking up
other points. He now limits his range, and his
work is so characteristic as mostly to need no signa-
ture. Figures he avoids, except as accessories; in
these 130 pictures, there is no figure over two inches
in size. Yet there are hardly any landscapes, in
the, ordinary sense. Water Will Ashton must have,
or buildings. If he can get both together he is
happy. .Venice suits him, or Holland, and in
bridges or barges or laundry-boats in the heart of
Paris he is at his best. His other effective line
is the seascape, with special dead-white foam.
Premier Crawford Vaughan opened the exhibition of
bright, effective, and mostly u moderate-priced work.
The catalogue mentions modestly that Mr. Ashton
has been hung in the Academy and both Salons, and
has taken a Wynne prize in Sydney.
Though Home-Affairs Archibald was able
to state last week that he was satisfied all
was well regarding the construction of the
Kalgoorlie-Port Augusta railway, certain cir-
cumstances have been referred to a commit-
tee of the Labor Caucus for investigation.
The charges advanced may be entirely reck-
less, and Archibald may be on firm ground;
nevertheless the committee will act wisely if
it takes nothing for granted. There is a dis-
tinct smell coming from this railway, and if
The Bulletin had it on the premises the
inspector of nuisances would call round to
make sure there was no corpse in the key-
hole. There is nothing like taking an un-
biassed nose to matters of this kind.
AT POVERTY POINT.
“Steele Rudd’s” new play, “Duncan
McClure,” has had a successful try-out of
three nights at Toowoomba.
The N. S. Wales Income Tax Commission-
ers tapped Muriel Starr for £54 on the day
her Sydney season closed. But they had been
very long-suffering about it. On and off she
had been earning income in N. S. Wales for
years, and apparently this was the first
time they had asked for their little bite.
“II.O.” : “Under Cover” furnishes another
and particularly bad example of the Yankee
trick of “spoofing” an audienee. “O. Henry”
taught his countrymen that a snappy, unex-
pected climax will often save an inferior
short story. But it will not do the same for
a three- or four-act drama. To allow one-
self deliberately to be misled through a few
pages of print is very different to having
one’s leg pulled, for three hours in a theatre,
the real facts of the story being disclosed
only in the last three minutes. To attempt
to keep a “secret” throughout a play is rot-
ten craftsmanship, as after the first night, it
will be known in advance to an increasing
percentage of each succeeding audience. To
anybody forewarned or the ending of “Under
Cover” the scenes leading up to it must
seem utterly futile; while the playgoer to
whom the “spoof” comes as a surprise
feels that any interest he may have taken in
the previous proceedings was aroused by
false pretences. The chief effect of the care-
fully-concealed climax in this instance is to
reveal the Sherlock Holmes hero, whom the
unsophisticated audience has been admiring,
as a bright and shining ass.

Ned Duggan’s warlike feelings found vent
in Melbourne Paunch last week. Ned’s
openiug verses were too weak to stand
alone ; for instance :
A wanton raid on commerce and trade
Is a plague on our social lives,
It absorbs our gain, and brings endless pain
To our mothers, sisters and wives.
But he got stronger as he grew more belli-
cose, and his exhortations culminated in: —
Strike till the beast is dead.
Ned very wisely stopped at that. No use
striking a corpse.
Maud Grantham has arranged a music,
song and story entertainment by way of
helping the Wounded Soldiers’ Fund. The
affair is to come off at the Assembly Hall,
Oollins-street, Melbourne, next Tuesday
night (14th). Maud Grantham will give the
dramatic Dickens tale of “Richard Double-
dick,” a couple of children stories, and “The
Making of a Red Cross Flag.” Her pre-
vious platform appearances as a one-woman
show were always interesting, and she is
now said to have improved her punch.
Four good vocalists will help.
“Te Babarantiko”: Though “The Sea
Wolf” is probably the best sea-yarn ever
filmed, one who has lived the life, cannot
help wishing that a little more realism had
been woven in. This lack is mainly owing
to the apparent impossibility of filming the
story in even a breeze of wind. There is too
much evidence of the hulk being towed
slowly (if moved at all) through calm water
—so slowly that even a distinct wake is
absent. The decks are too level for a small
craft under canvas, even in a light wind.
The cabin lamp is swung with pendulum
regularity. The lamp should swing more
perceptibly to leeward. Under no conceiv-
able circumstances could the boat capsize in
the weather pictured, unless deliberately
upset by the men in her. It is. probably
because of this difficulty of filming under
the proper weather conditions that some of
the most stirring incidents in the story are
omitted. In the “Remantling” scene ' Jack
London, who certainly knows, might have
introduced something less purposeless than
a pair of short sheerlegs to lift the jaws of
the gaff to nowhere. Yet these little flaws
are gladly forgiven for the sake of other
things that make the pulses beat with
longing for the times of not-so-very-long-ago.

“Harrison O.” : The first infant to appear
ia Mr. and Mrs. Davidson’s cradle of
national Australian drama at Oakleigh
(Vic.) was “The Fight,” by “George
Byfield.” Assuming that “George” wears
petticoats, I urge her to devote her energies
to knitting socks for soldiers, for the time
being at least. “The Fight” is a loose,
amorphous happening which claims to “deal”
with “the question of the segregation of
the unfit.” It is presumably intended to
point a moral and adorn a tale, but it does
neither. There is a consumptive young
man who wants to marry and is bombarded
with platitudes by a stodgy medico ; but in
the first act the consumptive’s inamorata
loses her sight through an explosion, and
for the remaining three acts the maiden’s
friends fill in time expressing sympathy and
bringing' the poor girl cream puffs. Between
whiles the doctor allows the platitude to
proclaim the bore, and in the last act he
partially restores the heroine’s sight, pre-
vents her committing suicide, and induces
her (the consumptive having been thrown
over for reasons quite apart from his “unfit-
ness”) to join him (whether or not in a
matrimonial manner of speaking is not quite
clear) in pushing his pet theory. The play
was a tiresome affair which arrived nowhere
by a circuitous route, afid demonstrated
nothing except that Missy Mrs. or Mr.
“Byfield’s” notions of craftsmanship do not
rise above the soliloquy and even the aside.
Unless a competent critical Board _of
Chuckers Out is appointed in connection
with the theatre, it will be in danger of
becoming merely a waste-paper basket for
MSS. judiciously declined by the Repertory
people.

The ballot for the selection of a Labor
candidate for the Willoughby (N.S.W.)
seat, vice Larkin, killed at Gallipoli, brought
John L. Chambers out on top, instead of
Hardinge, who was supposed to have the
best chance. Both were tramway men, and
prominent members of the Tramway Union,
Chambers is the son of an old Inky Way-
farer who owned the Cobargo Chronicle,
He is 34 years old, and has been in the
Tramway service for a third of his days.
The question of whether the last despair-
ing squeal of the N.S.W. pig shall be allowed
to rend the air of a neighboring State is the
latest constitutional problem confronting
the Australian Commonwealth. Happily
Isaacs is on the High C9urt bench, and the
issue can be awaited with calm confidence
that the law of the Federation will be
weighed with deliberate care and mercy will
season justice. The lawyers will supply the
apple sauce.
Scaddan’s incursions into commerce arc
disastrous nine times out of ten. The im-
portation of bran, maize and pollard from
Argentina has just resulted in the usual
deficit. Fearful that the weevil-infested
fodder would mistake itself for the Gadarene
swine and rush into the sea, the State
Industries Board offered several thousand
tons by auction the other day, and even the
local piggeries sniffed at it. About 300 tons
were languidly bid for at £3 12s. 6d. per
ton, or about £5 a ton less than it cost to
land, and efforts are now being made to
push off the balance.
Constituted authority, which made a shock-
ing mess of the drafting of the War Census
inquiries, made almost as bad a one in the
distribution of the cards. At Melbourne it
was announced that cards would l>e avail-
able on Friday, and Red Tape reminded the
public that unless it flocked to give particu-
lars of its cash and its ox and its ass the fine
would be a heavy one. But when the public
did flock, Red Tape found that the cards had
been lost, and proceedings had to be ad-
journed until they were discovered three days
later. At Sydney the cards were served out
at the G.P.O. in a small room with only one
door to panting, sweating crowds by a
youth stationed at a corner of the counter
over which inquiries about dead letters are
usually made. Later on the Courts will, no
doubt, deal with those who retired from the
scrimmage in disgust.
Frederic Albert Coghlan has been con-
firmed in the job of Auditor-General of N.
S. Wales after being Deputy for some time.
He is a Sydney native, born 1859, and lias
climbed the civil-service ladder step by step.
He was Under-Secretary in the Chief Sec-
retary’s Department from 1910 till lie was
perched on his present post. The Auditor-
Generalship is one of the oldest offices in
the service. It was first filled by William
Lithgow, who came to Australia in 1813 as
tutor in the family of David Allan, the then
Commissary-General. On June 14, 1825,
the Secretary of State appointed him Audi-
tor of General Accounts. The duties were
not heavy, so he also acted as private secre-
tary to Governor Darling. He hung on to
the Auditorship till 1852, when lie retired,
wealthy through pastoral speculations. His
successors have been Francis L. S. Mere-
wether, William Colburn Mayne, Christo-
pher Rolleston, Edward Alexander Rennie
(1883-1902), and John Vernon.
The Federal Government’s “war cards” are
now available; and there are thousands of
people who will not be able to fill in the
forms and give a true account of their
incomes. Every wealth-owner, male and
female, has to tell what his income is from
various specified sources. Then he has to
set out his outgoings under various headings,
and, deducting these from the total income,
the result is alleged by the card to be his net
income. But in thousands of cases it is
nothing of the sort. Suppose, for example, a
man draws a salary and has an income from
dividends and also runs a sheep station or
farm. And suppose, instead of making a
profit from the station or farm, he made a
loss from it, how is he going to show his, net
income except by deducting the loss? Yet
the card makes no provision for such a case :
it assumes that a man must make a profit
from every source. Half the farmers in
the country made a loss last year; and if
they merely answer the questions that have
been put to them, the result will be an en-
tirely incorrect statement, and the census,
so far as income is concerned, will be
quite valueless.
A hole in the law through which it is quite
possible that a few miners may fall to
destruction has been discovered in New-
castle (N.S.W.). Recently a mine-manager
was fined under Section 11 of the Goal
Mines Regulation Act for employing a man
who did not hold a certificate of competency
to take charge of an electric motor by which
men were raised up an inclined plane. Judge
Fitzhardinge decided, upon appeal, that it
was not necessary for a man in charge of
an engine, if he was competent, to hold a
certificate. Yet if a man does not qualify
by the ordinary methods, who is to say he is
competent? Even a man with so compara-
tively unimportant a job as a Judge must
not only be a lawyer—he must be a certi-
ficated and registered lawyer, the registra-
tion being the only proof of competency
which is taken any notice of when one is
charged with (say) accepting a fee for
doing some Real Property transfer business.
If Judge Fitzhardinge is right, and the law
doesn’t provide equal protection to the
miner, then the law should be altered
quickly.
ALONE AT LAST!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The hero strode from out the wings,
The lover of the play;
And he was free at last from kings
And lords who loved to slay.
A dozen scenes he had been through,
The heroine to win,
And many things he had to do
To do his rivals in.
But now, upon a garden seat,
Beside a painted tree,
The heroine sat down to meet,
The hero who was free.
Through fourteen scenes they’d tried in vain
To talk of love, and kiss.
But filthy villains came again,
And marred their perfect bliss.
At last the final curtain fell
Aud left them free to woo.
Instead, he muttered fiercely: “II — 1!
I’m fairly side of you!”
VICTORIA.
HAL GYE.

THE ONLY WAY TO STOP FOOD
FERMENTATION.
NEUTRALISE ACIDITY BY TAKING
A LITTLE MAGNESIA.
It’s the gas generated by fermenting food
that distends your stomach and causes you
to have a flushed face after eating, with
headaches, heartburn, flatulence, etc., and
the reason why drugs, patent medicines and
artificial digestants do not give you relief is
that they have little or no effect upon the
acidity which physicians have proved to be
the root cause of practically all forms of
stomach trouble. An absolutely pure
antacid or neutralising agent must be
employed to neutralise this harmful acid,
and the preparation invariably used in hos-
pitals and prescribed by physicians and
specialists is bisuratcd magnesia. This you
can obtain from all high-class chemists in
either powder or tablet form, and in the
majority of cases half a teaspoonful of the
powder or two tablets taken with a little
water after meals will be found quite suffi-
cient to instantly neutralise excessive acidity
of the stomach and thus prevent all possi-
bility of the food fermenting,*

“B.B.” : The 24th Battalion, 6th Austra-
lian Infantry Brigade, has stamped on its
stationery the figure of an elephant and the
words “No Surrender.” The no-surrender is
good Australian, but where does Jumbo come
in? I know our birds can’t sing and our
flowers are worth nothing, but I didn’t
know things were so bad that we had to go
to Siam or somewhere for an animal.
THE COLD SNAP.
“’Ere! Didn’t ye see the cow buck me orf?"
“Buck? She never bucked! She only sneezed!”
“0.K.” : A while ago I lost faith in the
doctrine that Britain was bound to come
triumphant out of this war. Her workers
in the mines and factories seemed as quar-
relsome dogs, concerned only with their
own petty personal interests, whilst their
opponents were as sheep flocking to the call
of duty. The sheep must win by their habit
of sticking together, I thought. Their
docility and weight of numbers must succeed.
They’d be driven to ultimate victory. Now
I think the growling, discontented, indepen-
dent-spirited British are going to win
through that very character which made me
despair of them. Perhaps the change of
thought is due to my having visited a dog-
show. Those ill-governed Britishers are
quarrelsome dogs, but they will co-operate
sufficiently to win the war in der long r-run,
as the Germans call it. When the dogs ulti-
mately cease growling and barking, we shall
hear the docile sheep bleating in despair.
Then the once-alarmed rulers of Great Brit-
ain will repeat the old truism : “Its dogged
as does it,” and go on mis-governing as
before. To the contentious that the Ger-
mans are wolves or hogs in sheep’s clothing',
and that their nation is a perfect machine
and their unity strength. I reply that poli-
tically they are sheep and their unity is not
strength, and the perfect national machine
was not constructed to bear the strain of a
long war.

“S.S.S.”: Northern Territory Adminis-
trator Gilruth said that “previous to the
advent of the Union (A.W.U.) these diffi-
culties [lack of loading facilities on the
wharf, little or no loading gear, lack of
proper supervision] did not appear to
exist.” And a unionist replied that this
was because the work was “previously
carried on by sweated Chows.” Sweated
Chows in Darwin! Why, the Common-
wealth Government pays Chinese cooks
£2 10s. a week and “keep.” The cook at
the European Hospital in Port Darwin,
whose cooking is of the plainest, gets the
same salary as the white matron, from
whom the Administration demands the high-
est qualifications as a nurse, housekeeper
and dispenser of medicines. The same insti-
tution gives its Chinese wash-boy £9O a
year and keep. He has no other work but
washing, and has his fires lighted for him
and his wood chopped. Also, the hospital
pays a 16-year-old Chow houseboy £7O and
“feund.” The duties of the two are usually
combined in one white-woman “cook and
laundress” at £1 a week in any other Aus-
tralian hospital of the same size. The
Government House “boys”—also Chow!—-
get much the same pay, even though the
place is “not in residence” for the greater
part of the year. When you hear of the
“cheap” Chow in the Northern Territory,
know that the man is talking of something
that doesn’t exist.
“Doc”: Who said the meanest man was
dead? He was very much alive when I
met him on his freehold farm last week. I
had been warned about him, and pitched it
extra-strong when I mentioned our wounded
soldiers and asked him how much I should
put him down for. He explained that
times were too bad; he couldn’t give any-
thing. Argument was wasted on him, and
finally I said, “Well, can I sell you a Bed
Cross button? It’ll cost you a bob.” He
looked at it and handed it back. “No,” he
said, “ ’tain’t worth it.”

A bureau which has undertaken the highly
important and very difficult job of hunting
up information concerning soldiers at the
Front will be opened in Sydney on Monday,
September 13. The headquarters of this
Sherlock Holmes will be 88 Pitt-street, and
the name, number, brigade, etc., of the
sought-after Kangaroo ( plus the cash for a
cable if there’s any urgency) will set the
bureau’s agents on the other side at work.
The Bed Cross Society is responsible for
the scheme, and had started to get it under
weigh, when the legal profession offered to
take it on as its little bit of national ser-
vice. Now there isn’t a Gent One in Syd-
ney worth mentioning who can’t talk Bed
Cross and the value of this new agency,
which gives its services without any charge
whatever. Adrian Knox, K.C., is now on
the other side, _ and will do the organising
there, possibly in conjunction with the Bri-
tish Bed Cross, which has over 1500 agents
in Europe, and includes even Constantinople
in its beat. The two, working together,
should be responsible for lifting a large
weight off the mind of the mothers, wives
and sisters of the fellows who have gone out
to meet the big trouble.
* * * *
Two years ago the barque Susanne Vin-
nen, flying the German flag, blew into New-
castle to load coal. A colliery strike delayed
proceedings for six months. When this was
settled and there was a prospect of getting
cargo aboard, a gale blew up and capsized
the ship. She was righted again and took
in her coal. Then she broke adrift from the
wharf, and wandered around till she was
caught and tied up again. No sooner had
she gathered up her skirts to move on than
war broke out, and Susanne for flying Ger-
man colors was interned. That was 12
months ago, and she had been tied up in
Newcastle till last week, when she was
taken in tow to Melbourne. If anyone says
that wind and weather hadn’t been helping
England to prepare 'for the war for 12
months before it started, the Susanne Vin-
nen is no doubt prepared to call him a liar.

The casual Australian commences early.
One of him the other afternoon, with all the
experience of seven years upon him, set off
in the Newnes Valley—one of the wildest
parts of N. S. Wales —to drive a calf home.
That was the last seen of him for that day
and night. At dawn about 80 men started to
search for the wanderer. After hours of
suspense and struggle in the rough country
they found him going strong. He thought
they had come to help him bring in the
other infant, so when they hove in sight he
cheerfully called out, “I haven’t found her
yef!”
Victoria’s C.J. Madden was lately called
ou to decide a case after his own heart and
to declare the rights of lovely woman. A
married woman of Northcote, owning pro-
perty, had been sued by a grocer fcr £29 3s.
Her lawyer in the lower court was about
to quote authorities showing that the hus-
band, with whom she was living, was liable
for household necessaries, etc., when the
Bench told him that “that was no good to
two old married men like them,” and gave
an order for the amount claimed. His
Honor, of course, upheld the lady’s appeal,
saying her separate estate was not liable
for groceries unless she distinctly made a
bargain to that effect. He also assured the
honorariums that they were no older or
more married than the House of Lords,
whose decision this was. It was all very
neat and proper, no doubt, but mighty un-
satisfactory to the grocer, the husband in
the case being a person cf poor health and
uncertain means.

From London :
Though the war has really made it more blase
than ever, “Society” succeeded in being thrilled the
other day when it learned that the Hon. Venetia
Stanley had become a Jewess. Venetia is a daughter
of Lord Sheffield and a sister of Sir Arthur Stanley,
Governor of Victoria. She is 28, and has been
offered marriage by a brother of Lord Swaythlirg,
who is head of the big Jewish banking firm of the
Montagus, and also chief of the strictest sect of
British Hebrews. As it was impossible for the bride-
groom to become a Christian, the bride acquiesced
in - the suggestion that the situation would be sim-
plified by her becoming a Jewess. The consideration
that at 28 there was no time to lose, and that the
chances of an eligible, noble and wealthy Christian
suitor coming along--were not too rosy, had of course
nothing to do with the case. She merely felt that
it was up to her to maintain the family reputation
for religious acrobatics. An uncle of hers, Lord
Stanley, of Alderly, turned Mahometan, lived a
hermit for the rest of his days, and allowed his
splendid mansion to go to rack and ruin. Another
uncle, the Right Reverend Monsignor Algernon Stan-
ley, seceded from the ranks of the London Church of
England clergy and joined the R.C.’s. He is the
titular Bishop of Emmaus, resides in Rome,
is a domestic prelate at the Vatican, an
assistant at the Papal throne, and, it is
surmised, will have the right to wear a red hat one
of these days. Lord Sheffield, the father of Venetia
and of Victoria’s Governor, has also some ideas of his
own about religion. As Mr. Lyulph Stanley, he sat
in the House of Commons, and while there special-
ised in uncompromising opposition to religious teach-
ing in schools. Make any proposal of that sort, no
matter how vague, and up he would spring like a
jack-in-the-box, and get purple in the face and
crimson in the language.
A PARADOX OF SKIRTS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“The dress with the seven-yards hem is popular.”
When times were good and peace with olive branches
Fluttered about the dove-cotes of the world,
When no one dreamt that hate’s black avalanches
From heights of Teuton Kultur would be hurled,
You Wrapped yourself so tightly round the ankles
111 hobble garments made of yards so few
That still the memory remains ar.d rankles
Of how with bitter sweat I carried you.
\ c-s, though I took the burden calm and smiling,
And swore I loved you better for the toil,
I spoke with art, your innocence beguiling,
While perspiration made my collar boil.
For you, sweet maid, although so nicely rounded,
Were twelve-stone-six at least those summer days,
And fiercely on my ribs my hot heart pounded
The while I kept my grip upon your stays.
Then extra yards of cloth could be afforded,
Ar.d fashion’s mandate bade you stint its use;
The Webs of busy looms were kept and hoarded
To be in these the leaner years let loose.
For now, when saving cloth might show some reason,
The order comes that gilds resist in vain ;
The stuff to make a proper skirt this season
Is three times longer than a counterpane!
Yard upon yard the thing must flutter round you,
And you, not I, this time must bear the load ;
And when the wind is high—-why, then, confound
you,
Your spread of sail will take Up half the road !
Yet mine the heavier burden still despite the
Plain fact that both your nether limbs are free,
lor, though the change of style perhaps delight the
Bright female mind, the bills Will come to me.
In days of hobble skirts I said I loved you,
And oft I proved it on the hottest day,
Through hours of stress in which I bore or shoved
you,
Pretending that the exercise was play ;
But now, dear girl, that you are my possession,
I carry greater loads, and try to smile.
Yes, though the drear accounts in long procession
Arrive by post, I find you still worth while.
x.s.w.

“Spectacles” : I am getting tired of trying
to inflate my brain with war information.
Take t!ie case of cotton. For months past
my feelings have been lacerated by the press
appeals in picture and prose to the British
Government to declare cotton contraband
and so starve the German munition fac-
tories. At last they have done it. Now I
can take my choice of three newspaper clips
—all collected within the last week :—-
No. I.—A German statement that Germany has
heaps more cotton than she is likely to require.
No. 2. —A neutral rumor 'that the German scien-
tists have succeeded in finding a good substitute for
cotton in wood pulp.
No. 3.—A British scientist’s scornful snort to the
effect that only fools believe cotton is necessary at all
in the manufacture of explosives.
I tell you what it is—l’m - sorry I ever
allowed myself to get worked up over the
subject. All I have accomplished is to spoil
mv beautiful ignorance of the subject. And
you know how blissfully-peaccful ignorance
always is !
N.S.W. Full Court has had to decide a
point to which Cold Tea had attached much
importance. According to the Liquor Act
' of 1912 applicants for licenses in electorates
which had survived the local-option poll
could obtain permits for “certain specified
premises.” The prohibition crowd argued
that this meant that the “premises” must be
“specified” in the application, and that,
therefore, a license could not be obtained
till the building actually existed. As nobody
would spend money in erecting a hotel un-
less a license was a certainty, the effect of
this reading of the Act would be that there
would be no new pubs. So that when all
the existing grog emporiums had been burned
down or eaten by white-ants or bombed by
the enemy the day of dry salvation would
dawn. The liquor interests argued that if the
Legislature meant to stop public-houses being
built it would have said so, and that it was
obviously more in the public interest that
new premises should be licensed than that
old ramshackle structures should be patched
up. This latter view has been adopted by
the Full Court, and Cold Tea is busy hunt-
ing up another brilliant point or two.

“Moana”: Apropos the suggestion of
“Jaques” (B. 29/7/’l5) about a simple un-
marrying ceremony. Nothing easier, as
Cochin-China can show. In that free land
when you get tired of your better half or
quarter or one-eighth, as the case may be,
you induce her to decide that she is full up.
Then you call in the nearest witness, gener-
ally the co-re., place a pair of chop-sticks
on a plate, and each party to the fracturing
breaks one in the presence of the witness.
Thus is the marriage tie promptly and irre-
vocably busted.

IMPOSITION ROMANCE.
EXTRAORDINARY STORY TOLD IN COURT.
At No. 1 Police Court yesterday three young
women were charged with conspiring- by divers means
to defraud Eve Merino. They admitted the allega-
tion, but denied having acted with criminal intent.
Constablette Bridget Brogue, who appeared to
prosecute on behalf of the police, in outlining the
case, stated that the three accused had previously been
employed as tram conductors, but were discharged
A TRAINED MAN.
JOHN HENRY : “Ned Writes
that he can sleep through
the roar of battle just as
if he were at home in his
bed. Curious, isn’t it?”
JESSIE MAY : “You wouldn’t think so if

on account of riotous conduct. Miss Eve Merino was
one of many persons who had placed advertisements
in the press for husbands. Her ad. (i-page) had
been running for seven months without yielding a
reply, when one morning last week she received a
group photograph showing three young men; A
covering letter stated that any one of them was
open to engagement. Miss Merino, after consulta-
tion with her aunts, who were also advertising copi-
ously, telegraphed to the address given in the letter,
suggesting that a personal interview would be desir-
able, and, further, that the three men should lunch
with her next day. They accepted the invitation,
stayed to dinner, and before leaving it was arranged
that the accused on the left should marry Miss
Merino within 10 days, and the other two should
within a month take to wife the two aunts. The
Bench would understand that under the circumstances
the ladies were delighted, and handed large sums of
money to the accused, for the purpose, it was
alleged, of paying their debts. In all £7624 was so
paid, but the sums involved in the present charge
amounted to only £4260. The suspicions of Detec-
tive Sarah McTavish had, however, been raised by see-
ing in the newspapers an announcement so unpre-
cedented as that three marriages were about to take
place. Following up an important clue, furnished by
a tailor’s cutteress, she was able to unmask the
accused as impostors.
Mrs. Billerkosts (instructed by Mesdames Squarem,
Fixit and Dodgerson), for the accused, said the
defence would be a denial of mens rea; also pleas of
infancy and duress. Her clients were not yet 21.
They admitted, having dressed in male attire, in order
to gratify a distorted sense of humor. But five
minutes after they reached the street they were
pounced upon, bundled into a motor-car, and carried
off to a house near Darling Point. There they were
offered money if they would undertake to get
married, and being in bodily fear of injury accepted
it.
The police intimated that Miss Merino and her
aunts were inmates of a private hospital, each suffer-
ing from a complete breakdown of nerves, cerebro-
spinal meningitis and measles.
Mr. McDublin, S.M., in remanding the accused on
their own recognisances, said that he always took
great care in cases of this sort. Persons in male
attire ran terrible risks. His own bodyguard had
with difficulty prevented a raid being made on him-
self. (Laughter.)
“Gus Gosli”: As a sort of relaxation
from war, Adelaide is on one of its period-
ical anti-Flossie crusades. A series of
articles in the ’Tisek on “Little Hells” set
the Holy City going this time, and fully
500 wowsers are loudly assuring the popula-
tion that they really did not know the char-
acter of some of the nice cool-drink empori-
ums where they went at odd times to quaff a
joyful glass. The young man responsible
for the articles painted a harrowing picture
of the simrile gir-r-1 brought to destruction
by the white slavers who. are in the soda-
water trade. Parliament is threatening the
scoundrels with all sorts of punishments, and
genuine widows who seek an honest penny
from the legitimate ginger pop and lolly
business go about in fear and trembling.
“Spike”: Particulars in the newspapers
of the daily haul made by Sikes and his
gang are becoming alarming, and the people
in my suburb are taking precautions. One
doesn’t mind when a burglar smashes a
jeweller’s window at midday and walks off
with a dish of gold watches. Such things
are part of the risks taken by jewellers, and
the police cannot be expected to attend to
trifles like that when their services are re-
quired hunting up the criminals who neglect
to claim a vote. Also every right-thinking per-
son understands that when a gang of desper-
adoes advances under cover of night and,
without giving fair warning, dynamites the
strongroom at Brandysnaps, it would be
improper to arrive at hasty conclusions
about the detective service. The service has
often to lurk for hours round pubs and
theatres. But when it comes to Sikes and
Co. getting away with sideboards, mangles
and Chesterfield couches from private resi-
dences in broad daylight, there seems reason-
able ground for criticism. Last Sunday the
remnants of the male population left in my
neighborhood spent many strenuous hours
bolting furniture down to the floors, screwing
pictures against walls and fixing padlocks
on the crockery. Also in at least 10 streets
the training of famished bulldogs was in pro-
gress, to the great scandal of the vicar.
Jock McPhairson, of McPhairson and Mac-
parritch, who lost three chests of drawers, a
dining-room table and a grand piano last
week, has rigged up a couple of infernal
machines just inside the front door, and the
first man who tries to sneak Jock’s drawing-
room carpet is going to hit the ceiling before
Mistress McPhairson lets the family tyke
loose. It’s a pretty serious business, this,
anyhow, and somebody ought to see if bikes
cannot be induced to flock to the colors.
Should he resist argument and object to
conscription, then the Government ought to
float a loan and get some superannuated
policemen over with the next lot of foreign
wheat, because, with all the fighting men.
going out of the country, the police will be
left to deal with the situation alone, and
some of us who are staying behind to do the
nation’s work and vote for the referenda
won’t have a chair left to sit on soon.
“Ornis,” from somewhere in Turkish
Asia: —
Some of the Mesopotamian laborers could give
many a hefty Australian wharf-lumper a point or
two in carrying heavy weights. The Arab, normally,
is dirty and lazy, but in the coolie class, although in
many cases an old man and small, he is a plodder.
I have seem them at Busreh, the town where the dates
come from, and which can be spelt in any old way
that you please, unloading mahalas (dhows) and
carrying bales of compressed fodder and sacks weigh-
ing up to 3001 b., and sticking at it all day in a
temperature which for months hovers round 115 deg.
in the shade. When “lumping” they wear a small
hessian bag stuffed with straw, on which the load
rests on the small of the back and suspended by a
piece of webbing round the forehead. Thus they carry
the weight in the best position, bending forward, and
without the necessity of raising the arms above the
head in an unnatural position to steady the load. I
saw the other day an Arab carry a bale of clothing
weighing 3cwt. It was much bigger than he, was
heaved into position on his back by four others,
and was kept in place solely by the web band. Also,
he had to walk over rough ground, barefooted, for
some distance.
“Dove”: Did anyone ever dream that
Peace, the gentle girl with the olive branch
in her arms and the dove perched on her
finger, would be the sort of draggled fugi-
tive she is to-day? Anyone who mentions
her in a kindly way r is at once suspected by
the right-thinkers, and only confirmed luna-
tics mount soap boxes in public parks to
voice sentiments that would have been the
baldest platitudes 18 months ago. The
world is very mad if we judge it by the
way it treats the poor lady. She is “pre-
served” with all sorts of care until war has
definitely set in ; when that stage is reached
she is chased off the premises With high
explosives and poison gas, and any old
friend who tries to remember her kindly is
hurried off to a horse-trough. When she
gets back home after this lot I guess she will
look like the Girl Who Took the Wrong
Turning in Act 111.

The other day the police broke into the
studio of an Australian artist whose old
published drawings had been used in a pro-
hibited way. They were there to “seize” all
works and material suspected of being dan-
gerous—and they did. Amongst the stuff
captured and carried off was a sixpenny
edition of Huxley’s “The Evolution of
Species” ! After this any guilty person who
owns a Euclid or a Webster’s Dictionary
had better bury it in the woodshed.

“J.G.”: Where are Irvine and the other
spruikers against preference to unionists?
A gross case occurred the other day, yet
the Iceberg has not even started to melt
with wrath. A Kew East (Vic.) man pur-
chased a property through a firm of estate
agents, and, as usual in such cases, the pur-
chaser had to pay for the cost of conveying
the property from the seller to himself. So,
the buyer asked the agents to have the deeds
fixed up for him. This was done, and
£4 4s. was paid for the job. But the
Gent. One Union, represented by the
Law Institute of Victoria, fell on the
blackleg who drew up the deeds and
dragged him before Madden, C.J., who asked
him to explain why he shouldn’t go to gaol
or be hanged for that, not being a bar-
rister or solicitor of the Supreme Court or
a certificated conveyancer, he prepared with
expectation of gain and reward a convey-
ance of certain land. Stripped of legal
frills, the charge meant that the man who
did the work was not in the lawyers’ union,
and he took on a job that only a law unionist
must touch. After Madden had read the
villain a lecture he fined him £2O with costs.
THE ONLY WAY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“The Call is to the unmarried.” —Recruiting poli
tician.
When sounds the crash of warring arms
The lad who selleth rag,
Who shopwalks, tailors, wields a pen
Or pick—in fact, all single men
Should rally round the flag.
Unwept, unhonored and unkissed,
He’ll be who seeks not to enlist.
The youth who does not wish to hide
A much-diminished head,
And would not cowardice display,
Yet from the Front would keep away,
No choice has but to wed.
For then Will one and all agree
He does not lack in bravery 1
Victoria.

A. S. XeiJl, dead in Adelaide at 78. was
nephew of the Dr. Sinclair, of the Beagle,
Darwin’s exploration ship. He was in
Adelaide from the beginning of things, his
father being accountant to the S.A. Com-
pany. Prominent himself as a public
accountant, member of the Marine Board,
Swedish consul, and manager at one time
for Elder, Smith and Co. af the Port, lie
came most into the limelight in the ’!)o’s,
when for some seven years he. with chair-
man J. 11. Smith and .John Hill, managed
the State’s railways.
Dead in Adelaide. R. C. Mitton, who had
a long and blameless existence as a teacher.
He did not reach Australia till he was a
married man of nearly 30, but it was long
enough ago for him to join in the rush of
1852 to the Victorian goldfields, and he
afterwards put in 40 years in the cause of
education before retiring for 20-odd years
of leisure. This means that he was 92 at
the end. Mitton will be chiefly remembered
as the most inveterate of unpaid writers for
the newspapers. On every conceivable sub-
ject he turned out letters, long-winded but
well-informed.
“Gent One,” from the southern Speck:—
During part of the two years he was on the Bench
just-deceased Justice Dobbie was a stopgap for Mcln-
tyre, who was sampling six-months’ leave on full pay
while he qualified for a pension. In appointing
Dobbie as Mclntyre’s locum tenens the late Liberal
Government merely dropped on a convenient way of
escape from difficulty. An appointment of some sort
had to be made. The political candidates for the billet
presented an embarrassing choice. So the Ministry
shelved trouble by temporarily appointing Dobbie,
then a civil servant, and fervently praying that some-
thing might turn up before Mclntyre’s leave ex-
pired. It did turn up. Before the six months were,
out a Labor Ministry had taken office. Having no
lawyer of its own to oblige it concluded that Dobbie
was equal to the work, and decorated him with a
permanent wig-. When Dodds died, the same Govern-
ment promoted Nicholls to be Chief Justice, and put
up Harold Crisp, who was never suspected of having
any particular views on politics. Thus with one
Judge well under 60 and two others nearer 40 than
50, it seemed that another vacancy on the Bench
was a matter of a decade at least. Kwing garnered
much of the business liberated by Crisp’s elevation,
and consoled himself for hopes deferred by claiming
a silk gown. However, Dobbie’s death from heart
failure has resurrected the old controversy as to the
rival merits of Ewing, Lewis and co., and again a
Labor Government has on hand a piece of patron-
age which cannot benefit any of its supporters. Lewis
is the choice of the “very best” people, but even
those who regard Ewing as possessing more talent
as a nisi prius battler than as a jurist cannot forget
that Lewis’s reputation has not been supported by
achievement in recent years. Doubtless there are
better lawyers than either. But, as with other
things, it takes more than ability to get a Judgeship.
Of a dead bishop :
Bishop Christopher George Barlow, just dead at
Cooina (N.S.AV.), aged 56, was the holder of three
records. He was the first shepherd of purely Aus-
tralian training and ordination to be made an Angli-
can bishop; his election to the see of North
Queensland in 1891 was the first instance in
which the Anglican clergy of any diocese chose
one of their own number to become their
chief; and he was the first Australian bishop
to possess no University qualifications or de-
grees. Barlow was a factory clerk in the London
parish where Stanton, the first to wear Anglican
gaiters in N.Q., was rector. When Stanton was
imported in 1881 to the then newly-formed
bishopric, Barlow followed him to Townsville, and
was ihere trained and afterwards ordained by him.
His first skirmish with Beelzebub was at Mackay, as
curate to Albert Haclaren, who was afterwards the
first Anglican missionary to Papua; and he subse-
quently gave Satan such a hot time on his own
account at Charters Towers and Townsville that in
1891, when Stanton came south to bish at Newcastle,
the Northerners unanimously chose Barlow' to succeed
him. Conservative Anglicans in Sydney and Mel-
bourne were aghast at the idea that, instead of taking
the usual course of importing from the Cold Country
some unknown English rector wdth a total ignorance
of Australian conditions, the North should choose as
its bishop a mere Australian parson who had served
10 years in the ranks of the diocese which he was
now to command. Protests were raised, nominally
on the ground that Barlow was not a “ ’Varsity
man” ; and the then Primate (Saumarez Smith) was
asked to refuse his consent to the election. Eventu-
ally, to partially remove the ostensible cause of com-
plaint, the Archbishop of Canterbury granted Bar-
low' the “Lambeth degree” of D.D.—a power which
survives in the Archbishops of Canterbury from the
pre-Reformation days of papal legates in England.
The present Anglican bishops of Bathurst and Tas-
mania (Long and Stephen) hold similar Lambeth
degrees, as, though they are both M.A.’s of Mel-
bourne, no Australian university grants degrees in
divinity. Barlow bished at Townsville for 11 years,
and then, in 1902, on the death of Bishop Chalmers,
of Goulburn, was chosen by that diocese to succeed
him. He remained there till last Easter, when
he resigned through ill-health. In point of service,
Barlow was the senior Anglican bishop in the Com-
monwealth, though in point of age he was junior to
half a dozen or more. lie died on the day before
his successor (Radford) was enthroned in Goulburn
Cathedral; and Radford’s first official act was to read
the burial service over him. Deceased prelate was
unmarried, though gossip over Sydney and Goulburn
afternoon-tea cups was always busy at selecting a
suitable wife for him. Persistent rumors of his
engagement to Gov. Rawson’s daughter caused both
parties considerable annoyance a few years ago.

Killed by the Turk. Cornwallis Charles
Wyndham Maude, youngest son of a British
General. He served as an officer in the
Worcester regiment in South Africa, and
when peace was declared came to Australia
and settled in N. S. Wales. Immediately
war broke out he enlisted in the Light
Horse as a private, but soon obtained a
lieutenant’s commission in the 9th. He
was killed at Suvla Bay.
A DIRGK OF GLOOM.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Tis a Dirge of Gloom
For an empty tomb
Where there’s plenty’ of room '
For the Things that the War Cannot Kill!
( the war !
As 1 said before:
• *** ! ! ! Vin i'll!)
But these Things that the War Cannot Kill:
The sights" and the sounds as you go your rounds
In search of “trays” or in search of pounds,
In a city where beer and thirst abounds ;
The alarming sights like the sudden lights
That bushmen see on recovery nights ;
And the sudden sounds
That ring in vour ears like the baying of hounds,
Or other more human and terrible sounds
Of the Morning After the Month Before.
But I’m out of my depth and out of bounds.
( the War!)
The nerve-racking rattle that takes you aback—
Like a rattlesnake in a narrow track
Or a snake in the morning in one of your socks—
Of the everlasting collection box
For a “Belgian” band that comes from “the Bocks”—
Or a Servian family on the rocks,
Or a British blare with a Prussia n brogue,
Or some other sort of tin-whistle rogue,
Playing the tunes that are most in vogue.
(I'm sick of the tunes that are most in vogue.)
We can’t have peace, and we can’t have war,
Or a prison gate, or a lock-up door.
Or hospitals for the rich and poor,
Or a church wherein to worship God
( l ve seen the box in the temples, too,
Of the Gods of Asia), but this is true:
B'e can’t do anything, old or new,
But Maud or Marion, Mabel or Madge,
From Mosman or somewhere, puts on a badge
And goes on the everlasting cadge.
And now it is War, and the same old “lay,”
As if we were Turkey, and couldn’t pay,
To keep a war going for half a day,
1 nless AY e helped Us in every way,
From raffling cushions at AVatson’s Bay
To rattling boxes on Circular Quay,
And breaking out on a teapot spree,
Instead of sewing on hooks and eyes,
As we used to do under peaceful skies,
And rattling dishes at wash-up time:
And nailing us down, from den to block,
To a cup at eleven and four o'clock.
(I’m short of rhythm and short of rhyme.)
And 1 Red Cross” branches in shop and hall
Have “Help!” writ largely on window and wall
In letters several inches tall ;
With notes of hysteria which appal
The weak and timid, and raise the gall
of real Australians (damn it all!),
And amuse our enemies, great and small.
AVhy can’t we have pride and be dignified.
And sweep all this feminine rubbish aside?
They mostly belong, it can’t be denied,
To the poodle-christening, Dame-worshioing side,
And they seize every ghost of a chance to have
Their Little Importance magnified.
“10very woman's a barmaid at heart!”
But not every one has the barmaid’s pride,
Nor the barmaid’s heart, nor the barmaid’s tact,
Xor the barmaid’s unusual gift to see
The General Fitness of Things and Fact,
And much less the barmaid’s sincerity.
I thought political women were dead.
But find they are sharpening their axes instead;
And each, as hopelessly off her head,
Determined lo go on just as before,
Only more,
“After the AVar.” (God bless the War!)
In the meantime, .Airs. Labor Brown,
And Mrs. Liberal Half-a-Crown,
And several Suffragettes of note,
AVho don't know women have got the vote,
Are chasing each other around the town,
And pleading wildly for pillow-slips
And table-napkins and necktie clips,
- v "d chocolate creams—the same old stunt—
And other rubbish to send to the Front,
To help ’em to bear the battle’s brunt.
While Tommy Cornstalk, whenever he halls
• u Pleading mildly (with all his faults)
For “ ’arf er packet or F.psom's Salts!”
N.S.V.
HENRY LAWSON.

The Commonwealth Government asked
for the loan of £5,000,000, and was
offered £12,082,410, with perhaps a
few more thousands to be added in
respect of applications which are the
subject of inquiry. For three days after
the list closed on August 81 applicants were
being heard from ; but they had to be turned
away for the present. Their change will
come later on. The applications came ~lh
this way:—•

The largest individual applicant was the
Mutual Life and Citizens’, which asked for
£1,0(10,000; the Commonwealth Bank
applied for £500,000; the A.M.P., Bank of
N. S. Wales, Bank of Australasia and
Union Bank took £250,000 each; and among
private persons the biggest lot —£150.000
was asked for by R. Barr Smith, of
Adelaide, the tine old squatter and merchant
who so generously helped to build the
Adelaide Trades Hall. The issue is already
at a small premium.
The Maokiland State Land Bank,
officially knoivn nowadays as the Settlers
Branch of the State-Guaranteed Advances
Office, whose deficit to begin with Avas due
to preliminary expenses and loss on loan
flotations: —

The State Advances Office runs other
branches which makie loans to workers for
building or buying homes, and to local
authorities. The settlers’ branch is, however,
the first-born and still the most important
member of the family.
The profits shown under that heading in
the table are not the real thing. The prac-
tice is to borrow money at so low a nominal
rate of interest that the loan can only be
placed at a discount. In other words, in
consideration of paying only (say) 31 p.c.
this State bank promises to accept (say)
£97 and call it £lOO and pay a full £lOO
back when the term for which the money
is lent is up. Plainly a profit shown by
charging only the bogus rate of interest is
fictitious. However, this bank does more,
than a fair thing in regard to the discount:
out of the year’s profits it usually provides the
whole of the discount on the loans raised
during that year. The total of these “loan
charges” standing in the balance-sheet as
an asset —that is to say, not made good out
of past profits—is now £30,000. Provided
nothing goes wrong with the securities, the
effect should be to permit the bank to show
very much superior net profits in the future,
because, though the discounts have now
been nearly all made good, the interest pay-
able will be the very low rate at which the
loan was floated in the first place.
And the bank can quite comfortably do
with bigger profits and much larger reserves.
For the reserves constitute all the capital;
and £362,000 is no tremendous cover for
risks running to eight million pounds. The
outstanding mortgages have moved up like
this:—
1904 ..£2.795,849 | 1910 ..£5,662,973
1905 . . 2,753,182 | 1911 . . 6,140,984
1906 . . 3,125,069 j 1912 . . 7,295,768
1907 . . 3,551,876 | 1913 . . 7,451,127
1908 .. 4,180,616 | 1914 .. 7,722,143
1909 . . 5,114,300 j m 5 . . 7,976,974
And there is another £300.000 or so risked
in “temporary investments” and other
things not quite so insufficiently described
as that. Remembering that the advances
are as a rule to be reduced by annual pay-
ments, £360,000 might be regarded as quite
good cover. But land values have risen so
preposterously in the Dominion that ordi-
nary rules don’t apply. Only high prices
for farm and station produce could possibly
have maintained the level of values of the
Past few years; and even with high prices
that level has only been maintained by the
ease with which mortgage loans have been
obtainable through this and other agencies.
Which means that such an institution as
this, projected by John Ballance for the
benefit of the small settler, has been pressed
into the service of the speculator, and has
been a screw-jack for lifting land values.
And high land values are a curse. However,
for present purposes all this is only of in-
terest as indicating that Maoriland land
values are unstable, and that still bigger
reserves are needed by this bank if some
(lay the State is not to be compelled to go
to its rescue, as it had to go to the rescue
of the Bank of New Zealand after a former
boom.
. There is one outstanding piece of quackery
in the accounts. The assets show —"Sinking
funds, £485,653.” Not one penny of this is
sinking fund in the proper sense of the
term. Not one penny of it is profit put aside
tor the extinguishing ,of debt. The whole
process seems to be afiffcst ip,credibly fool-
ish. Ihe bank borrows fKjii. Ifistead of
using the whole lot in its business it pays £1
or so either to the public trustee or to the
Ac.\ances Office Sinking Fund. Having
been thus diverted, the money is used for
precisely the same purposes as if it hadn’t
been diverted at all. Who is this designed

The directors have put by £3OOO towards the
war tax, and this amount has been de-
r-nav! . from the half-year’s profits. If this
£oooo is counted in, the profits show up
better than those disclosed for any previous
winter half-year. The important question
always is, Were the disclosed profits really
earned: No big co. knows exactly how much
profit it makes in any given term, because,
tor one thing, in a business like this it
doesn t know exactly how much revenue it
ought to write* off to cover depreciation, and
tor another thing it doesn’t know how much
ot its book debts it will be able to collect.
As a general thing this co. has provided
amply for wastage. Year after year the
directors have told shareholders: "The
machinery and plant have been main-
tamed in a high state of efficiency
and all expenditure in connection there-
with has been defrayed out of cur-
rent revenue as heretofore.” Moreover
not satisfied with “maintaining” plant—-
which, especially in time of prosperity, is
liable to embrace all kinds of additions—a
halt-yearly appropriation has been made for
depreciation. This appears to be an extra
provision—an additional half-pint run into
the policeman s _ keg after four quarts had
been measured in. to make quite sure that
the John was getting a gallon.
But a Board, even when it appears to be
allowing very liberally for depreciation, may
be overlooking something that doesn’t seem
to be liable to decay, but is really dry-rotting
mside. For instance, 18 months ago who
would have. thought that before this year
was out Britain would be closing the pubs
in some of the “best” districts —“best” from
the liquor trade point of view—till four in
the afternoon? Or who would have thought
that Victoria would have so soon shut all its
pubs at 9.30? These changes must mean
very serious depreciation of assets; and
there are only two ways in which that loss
of value can be provided for —(1) by writ-
ing oil capital as having disappeared; (2)
by wiping out accumulated or current profits.
Ihe Bulletin doesn’t know whether
Tooheys have beeii providing for such
a contingency as this. But, in anv
case, the investor lias' to keep in mind
the special risk of this business—the
large depreciation of assets throu<di
legislation, which is trending all in
one direction. It is no answer to say—“Ho
the breweries will be all right. The law
won’t keep people from drinking.” Even if
the people did drink as much at home, there
would still be heavy losses in value to be
made good by those brewing cos. which are
the landlords of many pubs. The pendulum
which has been swung towards anti-grog dur-
ing the war may possibly fly back after the
war. That can only be a matter of opinion.
But the man who is prepared to take the
risk isn’t so much an investor as a specu-
lator.
Looking back over Tooheys’ accounts for
five years these movements show up:—

There’s no shadow of doubt about the fat-
ness of the past five years. While liabilities
have increased by less than £50,000. the co
has £34,000 more in stocks and £64.000 more
on its books ; and anything over £lOO.OOO has
been spent on freehold pubs and new works
etc. Meantime the gross profit on brewings’
etc., lias more than doubled —which gives
some sort of hint of the real size of the
expend i tui e 011 plant and other revenue-
producing assets.

The trading profit for the year was £21,015,
as against £31,988 in the preceding 12
months ; but though that showed a drop of
£10,828, administration and standing
charges could only be reduced by £2585.
Hence a serious shrinkage in the net profit,
followed by the passing of the div. on the
ordinary shares for the last quarter of the
year “on account of the war and the
drought.” Why war and drought should
have punished this co. so very severely isn't
obvious. It seems almost incredible that
tlie demand for boots and shoes should have
slumped so badly as the figures relating to
profit suggest, even allowing that the policy
of the average retailer has doubtless been
to work down his stocks ny reducing his
purchases. However, there are the figures,
and there is nothing in the accounts which
can explain away their significance. In fact,
it is doubtful whether the true net profit
was a.s much as £8612, because, though
depreciation may possibly have been pro-
vided for, that provision has hitherto been
made by additions to a plant renewal
account, and there has been no addition this
year. So far as plant is concerned, there
-. probably isn’t any serious depreciation to
provide for. The Marshall Go. used to work
its own machines, unlike the majority of
manufacturers in the business, who find it
better to lease the plant from the patentees.
A couple of years or so ago it is said, that
this co. joined the majority; and that,, by
the way, was put forward by the friends
of the patentees of the mostly-Yankee
machines as the explanation of the bigger
profit shown on June 80, 1914. Since credit
was thus taken for the bigger profits of
1914, it is to be hoped nobody will be so
unkind as to suggest that the same thing
should be now debited with the lesser profits
of 1915.
Still, if the profits have shrunk so much,
the co. is in no worse position otherwise.
Liabilities in 1914 were £22.200; they ; are
now £24,088. But while there has been
that increase of only £2423 in debt, some-
thing like £4500 has b’een put into premises
and plant. £7OOO into stock, and a trifle in
the till. This has only been possible by the
reduction of book-debts, which now amount
to £26,808. as against £42,400 last year. So
far as this is not due to a diminished turn-
over the reduction is one of the good features
of the accounts.

Now the uever-againers have their chance.
They can’t kill the trade of the enemy with
their mouths. But they can kill it by in-
sisting on Australian goods. To merely
refuse to take enemy goods is not enough.
You can never be sure that what is marked
“Swiss” or “Made in Holland” isn’t Ger-
man. But when you see the name and
trade-mark of an Australian firm on the
goods, then you can be sure that you are
getting your punch in all right. Cos. like
this one have quite enough troubles without
having to convert Australians to the use of
Australian goods. They are a long way
from the pulse of Fashion, for one thing,
and can’t tell very well how that uncertain
cat is going to jump; and that is liable to
mean heavy loss, as it did last year, when
the cat did a somersault. Further, labor
hasn’t quite found itself yet in Australia:
it hasn’t wholly got rid of the idea that you
can make money by the beautifully simple
process of punching the atmosphere. There
has been a lot of sneering lately at the
I.W.W. cranks for calling on the workers
to go slow and make the work go round;
but the I.W.W. cranks put into words what
thousands of others put into their daily
lives. In one breath these people say “Labor
produces all,” and in the next they advise
Labor to produce as little as it can. though,
since it produces all, if it produces but a
little there plainly can be but a little for
it to have. However, these things will right
themselves in time, and the Australian work-
man will recognise that the more he pro-
duces the more there is for him to get. And
when he realises that, and “bogs in.” indus-
tries like the Austral Hat Mills will have a
much smoother passage.
When a co. shows a fair profit and doesn’t
pay a dividend, the first thing you may
usually suspect is that its banker won’t let
it. In this case that Gorgon can’t be. stand-
ing in the way, since liabilities of all kinds
total only £484, while the co. has £3556 of
cash and over £3OOO of semi-liquid assets
(book debts, etc.). Presumably the direc-
tors see early and good use for the £3OOO-
- of cash in hand, otherwise shareholders
might very reasonably have had a taste.
For the rest, the capital is represented
mostly by stock, but partly by freeholds and
plant; and the plant seems to have been kept
adequately written down.

This co., which ought to be doing well under
all conditions,' has done very well, as a rule,
only when the country has been on a bor-
rowing spree. * Instead of settling down to
steady development the party politician
comes along and produces a bogus prosperity
to show how much better he is than the
other fellow. Then the bottom falls out, and
a few grey years are spent in cleaning up
the mess. Australia has been having one
of its wild sprees. In 1902 and onwards
for the first five years shown in the table,
the total public borrowings of Australia
averaged under £4,000,000 a year. For the
12 months before the war it was £21,000,000.
Ic had been bounding up year after year—
seven, eight, eleven, sixteen, eighteen,
twenty-one. Next year, it seems, we shall
have sackcloth and ashes, and maybe a
hardly-secured eight millions amongst the
lot, excluding, of course, the Common-
wealth. And then Walker’s, which has
“been fully employed throughout the year,
principally on contracts for locomotives for
the Commonwealth. S. Aus. and Queensland
railways.” will probably have to fall back
to the old odd job and the attenuated divi-
dend. We shall know quite well that we
ought to go on building locomotives all the
time: but. unless we turn over a new leaf,
we shall put up with what we have until
we must shift a bigger load than usual, and
then we shall rush away orders to the
U.S.A.. or Britain, or Japan, or Germany—
anywhere, in fact. Walker’s, Ltd., have
done wonderfully well during the past six
years ; but he is an optimist who thinks that
what they have done is a. fair average of
what they are likely to do in the next seven
years.
Meantime, however, the co. has put a lot
of fat on its bones to help it through a lean
time. Look at this :

a ne t Profit has been exhibited!
lhe dotation—guided from England—was
almost as bad .as it could be, and the man-
agement for some years afterwards was no
better. Then there was & reconstruction
and the control was transferred to Sydney •
whereon things improved, though, if tire cook
is to be judged by her pudding, it is still
nothing to boast about. The goodwill account
was not completely wiped out in the recon-
struction, although plainly goodwill was not
really worth a cent. This item still stands
at £2903, and represents the bulk of the
£ooB6 of capital which, according to the
table, lias no tangible assets against it. On
wi® ' °th e r side, there is a “reserve account,
£o<~’ ; but that has been disregarded, as it
is apparently merely a reserve against doubt-
ful _ debts, etc. Book debts run to £1994.
which seems an extravagant figure in view
of the nature of the business and seeing
that the revenue for the half-year was

factory. Liabilities on all accounts amount

< ?.<^ oc k s ’ f 1 9 ,°43; leasehold and plant,
; book debts, £1994 ; cash, etc.. £2038.
The security for the capital thus depends
mainly upon the actual value of the motor-
cars and other plant; and that can’t be told
from the published figures. The best that
can be said is that, in spite of adverse con-
ditions, the Profit and Loss account has
greatly improved; and shareholders, most
of whom had no doubt given up hope long
ago, may even find a flicker of the lost sub-
stance returning to them.

Occidental, near Cobar (N.S.W.), cannot
boast of much profit, but the directors are
hopeful, and intend sinking the main shaft
another lift and purchasing a better plant.
The deepest level at 640 ft. is supplying the
hulk of the crushing stuff, and a winze sunk
58ft. below No. 6 is on a good shoot, aver-
aging £2 19s. 2d. per ton. Still the mine in
the sulphide zone has been patchy and re-
fractory. Recoveries are only 85 p.c. of the
gold value, which means 17s. out of 20s.
During the half-year to August 4 9340 tons
wore raised, averaging £2 3s. 9d. per ton.
of which £1 19s. Old. was recovered. The
profit was only nominal, but a strike has to
take some of the blame.

ALUMINOFERRIC is used by the Sydney Water
Board, New South Wales Public Water Supply Depart-
ment, and many country Water Boards and graziers
for precipitating impurities and clarifying water in
reservoirs, cattle dams. etc. It is simply thrown
into the reservoir in the form of a solid cake, or
dissolved and added as a liquid. In three hours’
time the most discolored water will be as clear as
crystal. One pound will clear over 2000 gallons of
water. It is exceedingly cheap and quite harmless.
Sole agents: William Adams & Company Limited,
Sydney, Melbourne, Brisbane and Perth.*

Providence tempers the wind to this mature
lamb by arranging that in time of drought,
when things in the Deniliquin district are
liable to be awful, owners shall struggle to
save the last of their starving stock by truck-
ing them south or railing fodder up north to
them. So, though in the past half-year
passenger and general traffic fell .away badly,
the total receipts were £10,960 as against
£8857 in the first half of 1914 and £7548 in
the first half of 1913. This, freight isn't
very profitable; nevertheless it was a help,
and the co. made a much better .profit than
usual in the first half of the yaAr. Taking
it over a long period the co. offers an invest-
ment with neither shocks nor thrills in it.
For many years past it has paid a steady
7 p.c., and, besides, has improved its cash
position like this :
1905. 1915.
Liquid assets .. .. £9,0004 £14,883
Liabilities 31,02$ 1,918
Excess liabilities .. £22,028-,
Surplus assets.. .. -- £12,965
Meantime whatever money has been spent
on permanent way and equipment has come
out of revenue and been charged up to cur-
rent exes. Even assuming that there has
been some depreciation which lias not been
covered by current renewals and additions,
it is hardly likely to amount to £35,000,
seeing that the total capital expenditure is
only £162,672. Hence the margin earned
beyond the dividend looks very comfortable.
* * * *
When difficulties were overcoming the
Hydro-Electric Co., which was going to
supply enough light and power from its
Great Lake works to make llobart hum, the
Hobart Gas Co. did a good stroke of busi-
ness by buying out its reticulation scheme
in Hobart and going into the electricity
business pretty strongly. Now the Govern-
ment, having taken over the hydro-electric
scheme, is offering to buy out the Gas Co.'s
electrical undertakings, lock, stock and
barrel, for £IOS,OOO or thereabouts. It
looks a good thing- for the Gast Co., for the
total assets are valued at only £185,000, and
the gross receipts from the electrical side are
no more than about £20,000 per year out of a
total of £60,000. On the other hand, the
electric business is swelling visibly, having-
grown last year by 18 per cent., and when
the Government gets its cheap. water-power
the business ought to boom if it is well
managed.
B. H. Junction North has 180.000 issued
shares and 70,000 in reserve. It is proposed
to increase the capital from 20s, to 30s. per
share. Shareholders are to discuss matters
io Sydney on the 16th, and the formal reso-
lution will be put in Melbourne on the
23rd. A two-thirds majority is required to
carry the scheme through. The market is
very dispirited. The co. on September 30,
1914, had assets (cash, stores, concentrates,
slime, etc.) totalling £160,478, against lia-
bilities of £61.215, so on that date, the,
nominal surplus was £99,263. Since then
the nominal surplus is understood to have
been reduced to something like £BO,OOO. But
a slime dump is no good to pay the store-
keeper with, and something has to be done.
In desperation Great Cobar asked the
N.S.W. Government for £40,000 to keep
the mine going. "The request being of course
turned down, all the men. have been put
off. From this it is clear that the effort to
squeeze more money out of the _ English
debenture-holders has thus far failed. It:
has been a ghastly business altogether, and
a fine instance of what some people call
bringing capital into the country. In other
words, the property was sold for an utterly
ridiculous -sum to an Anglo-French co., which
did most things wrong, largely because the
reports on which it bought were absurd.
While the old Cobar Syndicate worked the
mines they were a source of great profit to
the owners and the co. Then the properties
were sold and “foreign capital was intro-
duced,” and the country promptly began to
suffer.

Adelong Estates (N.S.W.) pay a long-
expected shilling dividend. No. 2 dredge
has had some fine runs, and now that No.
1 has also come along there are hopeful
prospects.
“Blue Geebung” ; As the mine-owners are
not likely to concede the Northern (New-
castle) coal miners demand for a -0 per
cent, increase in wages, the matter may pro-
bably be argued in the Arbitration Court.
If the owners have the common sense to put
in a return of the number of shifts lost by
the men every fortnight, it will require very
forcible argument to convince an unbiased
mind that workers who can afford to take
so many holidays are not receiving sufficient
remuneration for their labor. Increased pay,
which will allow him to miss even more
shifts than at present and still uraw the
same money on pay Saturday, is not likely
to induce the miner to reverse his present
motto —“Pleasure before business —which
is such a serious handicap to this Slate in
building up the very large trade that the
excellence of our coal should secure for it.
Associated (W.A.) treated 127,05 < tons
last financial year for £135,917, and made a
profit of £1960. This surplus was increased
by the balance brought forward to £16,022,
but a 6d. dividend reduced it by £12,384, and
£11,430 written off for development swal-
lowed up the balance, and a few other
charges left a debit of £8659. Work-
ing costs (exclusive of development)
were 19s. Bd., or a fraction of a penny
less than the previous year. The broken ore
in the stopes is estimated at 32,479 tons, as
against 33,306 tons 12 months ago.
Yuanmi (W.A.) has issued an ore re-
serves statement which shows that develop-
ments in the Yuanmi leases are more than
keeping pace with the mill. Only sulphide
ore, however, now remains, the quantity in
sight being 40,713 tons, worth 40s. 7d., com-
pared with 29,635 tons, valued at 40s. 5d., a
year ago. The co.’s other show, the Oroya-
Black Range, is worked out.
