Illuminating extract from a letter sent by
manager Bob Nicolson to the Ivanhoe
(W.A.) directors :
Re recent diamond-drilling from the 3320 ft. level.
The porphyry has come over 19ft. since last bore-
hole, and the ore body 404 ft., which is a favorable
development. If the ore body continues at a similar
angle it should be out by the porphyry on the west-
ern side in another 50ft. or so. I would recommend
to sink to 3470 ft., and put in another drill-hole
from bottom of shaft.
Nicolson’s recommendation has been adopted.
North Kalgurli (W.A.) has decided to
economise. Expenses are to be cut down
at the mine, and there may be a change of
managers.
* * * *
A well-attended funeral service was held
in London last month over the discredited
remains of Chaffer’s (W.A.). The chair-
man mournfully announced that the plant
had cost £39,000, as against an estimate of
£25,000. Also, as foretold on this page some
time before that plant was completed, there
was not enough payable ore developed to
keep it going. A disappointed shareholder
said there had been enough of “mad dog”
management, and he wanted to know what
arrangements had been made for working
the property if fresh capital was put up.
One of the new members of the Board said
that if the reconstruction scheme was
adopted a firm of mining engineers of high
repute would be prepared to take charge.
Reconstruction resolutions were unani-
mously adopted, but Kalgoorlie is dubious
about the success of the scheme, and the
underwriters are likely to be landed with
much of the scrip.
Guy Andrew, a man with American oil
experience, says he has got hold of a big
thing in oil on Bruni Island (Tas.). After
putting in four months wandering round
Port Davey and other remote parts of the
State, he has turned; those districts down
and gone nap on the present proposition,
which is less than 30 miles from Hobart.
He says that near Cape Frederick Henry
there are “tar-wells” formed by the viscous
oily substance exuding from the rocks ; and
he thinks this a dead-sure sign that oil is
there. How much there is of it, he admits,
remains to be proved. About 4000 acres
have been pegged out on behalf of two
syndicates.
* * * #
When John Craze threw down the pick
at the British Montana, Zeehan (Tas.). to
follow the plough, it looked as if he had
quitted mining for ever. But now he is on
with the old love again, and has gone to
handle the Round Hill, near the Forth
River (Tas.). The district was once known
as the Claude, and had more downs than
ups; but recent work has changed every-
thing. The workings show a width of up
to 50ft. of ore, and loud whoops that the
Round Hill is the biggest lead mine in
Tasmania can now be heard in Collins-
street, Melbourne. A little of the ore is
hot stuff, going 80 p.c. lead and 600 oz. sil-
ver ; but in the bulk it is a concentrating
proposition, and Craze is ‘Scheming out a
plant and a plan of mining work to allow
of an output of 150 tons per shift. So sure
are the knowing ones that Round Hill has
a future that several lots of shares in re-
serve have been rushed at Bs. each by one
or two of Hobart’s fatmen. John Craze
consequently starts off with a full purse,
an unusual experience for West Coast shows.
* ■* * *
11. P. Cecil still runs the South Mount
Cameron, Bradshaw’s Creek (Tas.). He
finds it nearly as tough a job to wash profits
into the tin sluice-boxes as it was to make
a do of the old South Long Tunnel. He
walked into Bradshaw’s Creek after the
Chinese had been living on the drifts, and
managed to pay a few dividends till he got
stuck up by the last droughty seasons. Now
it is like old times, when he could run his
nozzles three shifts. But the fogs that settle
down on the Ringarooma Valley are as dense .
as the best products of London, so from
March to June he could handle only 35,500
cub. yards of drift for llj tons stream tin
and a sweetening of gold. This pays the
way, and as the sun strengthens his output
lengthens. There is, however, a little yap-
ping crowd who fail to appreciate Cecil’s
work. They ought to plod round through the
midwinter muds and mists on the. Ringa-
rooma, and they would soon recognise that
this is no soft snap.
* * * *
Of all Australian mining towns which
were flourishing three or four years ago the
saddest to-day is Zeehan (Tas.). Four-
roomed cottages, with quarter-acre freeholds,
have been sold there lately for £25, and an
eight-roomed house has been leased for ss. a
week.
Andy Kelly’s successor at Coronet Hill.
Northern Territory, is Callanan by name,
and well commended for his copper work.
He has ideas, and goes for a new shaft on
the hanging-wall side. This is to save put-
ting in more pumping power, and, incident-
ally, to prospect another outcrop. The
Commonwealth foots a share of the _ cost,
which, with wages at 15s. a day for miners,
must be high. The co. had only £l3OO in
hand on June 30, so it looks as if it will
have to let out some of its reserve £lO stock
if the claim is to have decent prospecting.
* * * *
“Alarnon” : It is time someone took N.T.
mineowners by the ear and impressed a few
salient facts upon them. Recently a mine-
owner began, with Government assistance,
to re-work an abandoned copper show. For
over 15 years that mine had been idle, and it
was now full of water. The owner pumped
it dry, but allowed all the water to run to
waste, either not knowing or not caring that
it was impregnated with copper. All of the
metal could have been saved, for scrap iron
can be obtained practically for the asking,
and there is always ample timber at hand.
Similarly, half the mines in the N.T. are
littered up with useless machinery that has
been left to wind and weather until it is
ruined. It is an exception to find a mine
in good condition, or with machinery that
has received any intelligent treatment. One-
half the money spent on N.T. mining pro-
positions is wasted, the result of the in-
competence of mineowners and managers.
Devotion to their fetich of Chinese “cheap”
labor is responsible for very poor value
being got for the other half.
* * * *
An “all in” pegging race took place at
Sandstone (W.A.) the other day. Two
prospectors who applied to the Warden for
the same area, and were refused on account
of faulty pegging, rushed out of court and
headed, one on a mule and the other on a
bicycle, towards the coveted claim. The
betting was odds on the bicycle; but Balaam
won out of sight. A Bulletin man was a
starter on a camel in a similar sprint from
Coolgardie in 1894, and had as competitors a
man on a brumby and another driving a pair
in a buekboard buggy. The camel dropped
dead while leading, the brumby fell and
seriously injured its rider, and the driver of
the conveyance went on and afterwards gave
liis opponents a lift home.
“Quidam” on some gold that can t be
taken out again :
' Ararat (Vic.) did some wonderful things in its
day. At White and Blackman’s lead, Chamberlain
and two mates took 400 oz. from a shallow 33ft.
square claim. A pound of gold to the load was
common on the lead, the sinking being from 20ft. to
40ft., and the washdirt 6in. to a foot thick. Later
on, a lot of the headings were taken out profitably.
Rumpling and party got 60oz. to the load in their
claim on Blackman'L On the White Lead “Liver-
pool Jack” got loz. to the load, taking 6ft. deep of
wash. It took him six months to work out his
33ft. claim. The average yield from the White
Lead claims was 251 b. of gold per claim (33ft.).
Ararat, by the way, wasn’t Ararat then, but Mount
William. Later, another field was worked out on the
Grampians, also called Mt. William, but it wasn’t like
the original. Ararat was found by Chinamen from
Adelaide, and worked by them for some time before
the white man got on to the discovery. Most of
the gold went to Adelaide, the S.A. people forming
a road for the police-escorts so as to get a share
of Victoria’s golden harvest. The old track can
still be seen out on the Grampians. There were
many flour-gold leads about Ararat, and thousands
of Chows lived near the Hopkins River for years,
carting flour-gold tailings from the puddlers, and
re-treating them on their big canvas cradles. A few
years ago a Victorian cyanider, rather more than
usually unfinancial, was meditating on some scheme
for making a rise. He suddenly remembered that
when a boy in Ararat he had seen Chows cradling
for flour gold, and any hint of flour gold draws a
Vic. cyanider like a magnet draws iron. He imme-
diately fled to Ararat, and discoverd a flat, hundreds
of acres in extent, composed of alluvial tailings, from
2ft. to 10ft. deep. Shortly afterwards, several plants
were at work on different portions of it, and he got
the rise all right. No newcomer would ever have
suspected that the grass-grown flat on the Hopkins
was composed of “made” auriferous material. As for
the flour gold that must have gone down the Bet Bet
River between Maryborough and Dunolly, from the
old Timor and surrounding diggings, it makes any
cyanide man unhappy to think of it.
Fate of Associated (W.A.) is still in the
balance. During July a working profit of
£lOB7 was made on a turnover of £12,072;
but this surplus was just £l5 short of the
sum expended on so-called development.
Some good patches of ore are occasionally un-
earthed in new ground as well as in parts
of the old workings, that are now being gone
over again, but the margin of profit on the
grade treated is small and sometimes vanishes
altogether. At depth the property is done
for.
Westralia’s “Great Gold Hunt” originated
by “Bullant” Mclntyre, of Southern Cross,
started on September 1. On that day the
hunters, headed by Mac and the town band,
moved out from the Cross and began the
chase. All will be well until Marvel Loch
is passed, and then the expedition is planned
to search the lone, publess land to the south.
Everyone wishes the enterprise success, even
though many think it lacks organisation.

Sydney, September 6, 1915.
My Dear Abini, —
Tile Cafe Chan-taut in the Town Hall was
the Amateur Sportsmen’s effort for the
wounded. Nobly supported by their female
relatives, they opened proceedings with the
vice-regal presence and a brass band on
Saturday, and closed it without outside
assistance on Monday. lion, organiser
Hsiick led the Strickland party round the
Elizabethan and Assyrian show after the
official speech had taken the first chill off
ihe ceremony. 11. Y. Brad don, chairman of
*he committee, thanked Sir Gerald "Fitz-
gerald’ for his presence. That slip amended,
and the few remarks hastily concluded, the
band broke loose and split the welkin with its
din, and the lady pirates—who were like the
sands of Manly for number —rushed to busi-
ness, robbery, etc. The Strickland party
then retired to a tea-table, where they got a
few words in edgeways while the concert
proceeded.
Mabel Batchelor; the trio Iris Edgar,
\S yune and Black; and Dorothy Brunton
and C. 11. Workman, of the J.C.W. Co., were
good on the vocal side. The flapper discov-
ered that Workman, who is usually described
through a mouthful of chocolates as “a dear,”
is very bald. This shock over, the Young
Person, who surged round the adjacent tea-
tables, loudly applauded her idol’s frisks
across the platform.
Two shillings entrance for the afternoon’s
show kept down the attendance. The public
only jibs sometimes, and the variation from
the usual “bob” fee —a recognised charge at
these affairs —hurt the daytime rush of vic-
tims. _ Mrs. Corbett, wife of the Sun’s
sporting writer, and Mrs. Wright were look-
ing after sales at one of the most extensive
Queen Bess stalls. A Mena stall (Mrs.
Greatorex in command) was fortified by
damsels dressed in the wriggly-striped, pat-
terned stuff which we suppose Cleopatra
wore at her matin prayers to Osiris. Mrs.
James Shaw and her friends had a cubby-
house that was thick with haggis, tartan
ribbon and Scotch accent. Mrs. Roger Fitz-
hardinge, a lively young matron clad in
rustic brown leather drapery, was responsible
for the safe keeping of the live stock. At
one crisis in the afternoon, two shepherds
arrived to claim a very young lamb, and a
monkey made hideous grimaces at the rest of
the company.
A fancy-dress carnival in the evening-
brought a satisfactory eliuk to the
treasury. Mrs. Smithers, a pretty young
woman dressed like a china-ornament shep-
herdess, was elected Queen of the Carnival.
She made a wobbly progress round the ball-
room in a (-hair carried shoulder high by
shepherds in swallow-tails. The usual in-
stalments of kings, queens and historic
knaves were present in suitable attire. As
her prize Cleopatra (Miss Foley) got a set
of fish knives —in delicate allusion, no doubt,
to Antonio’s Dago origin.
Wattle Day came and went without any
casualties. East year’s free-planting cere-
mony at the Athol Zoo was not repeated.
Perhaps this was because in the interval the
plans of the Zoo have been completely
changed, and trees that were dug in when
the Board of Trustees had the gardens in
hand have been dug out again since the
Public Works Department took over the big
job. The wattle ladies gave away great
sprays of the golden plumage of the bush
at the Soldiers’ Club. Other enthusiasts
whisked up to Liverpool and left their cards
in the same form.
In the thick of Saturday’s dust storm the
St. John Ambulance Society handed over'
two big cars for the Military Hospital’s use.
David Storey (whose son has just been
wounded at Gallipoli) and Surgeon-Colonel
Lane Mullins made suitable remarks on
behalf of Brother Kangaroo.
Sir Thomas Lees, the boyish-looking
baronet who was on Chelmsford’s staff for a
while, was killed in action the other day.
Young Lees found the Society mill a bore,
and preferred to dig out a well-informed Aus-
tralian and get something solid into his listen-
ing ear. He intended to have put up later
for a seat in the House of Commons. In a
letter written in London a few weeks before
the war he said:—
1 am still a Tory in spite of m.v Australian
experiences, because I think that parte stands for
the Empire in the wider sense. But it’s a grand
thing to have had a bit of experience of Greater
Britain —some English people’s ideas of what Aus-
tralia is makes one stare! Australia has opened my
own eyes.
lie looked forward to coming this way again
to complete his education. But a Turkish
bullet found him first.
The bearing of the I)untroon Military Col-
lege youths was one of the features of
General Bridges’ funeral at Canberra last
week. No wonder. The boys had done a
whole month’s steady rehearsal of the de-
pressing ceremonial drill, so that the old
chief might go to his long rest in style. But
they were glad when it was all over. When
you have had a funeral on your mind every
day for a month you’re apt to get tired of
that funeral.
Until September 15 the public can con-
finue to absorb the story of the Trade Fair
at Circular Quay. The Chamber of Com-
merce fathers this show of British, Austra-
lian and Allies’ goods. The china exhibit,
bearing the names of English makers, is a
revelation to those who fancied that Dresden
was the last word in decorative ware. Aus-
tralian goods—notably a line display of
woollens from the backs of our own edible
lambs —are in a conspicuous ascendancy.
►Some of the inedible variety present tlie
other day were so liberally sprayed with
Australian scent that there was an atmo-
sphere of bush flowers. The perfume was
chiefly Eau-de-Sydney—though still, of
course, labelled Eau-de-Cologne.
In artificial flowers the Australian is going
to hold his own with the most exquisite
blooms from Paris workshops. Already there
seems to be nothing lacking but a wider
range of delicate coloring in the pastel
shades. For the rest, the boxes of silky
flowers are lovely enough to break down the
Thrift campaign—to the extent, anvhow, of
a new spring hat. Governor Strickland made
a brief christening speech on the first day of
the Fair, after which the company hung over
the machine that folds and gums envelopes
or buried itself amongst the contents of a
carpenter’s shed, according to its bent,
t aimed music is served up every day, and
afternoon tea is yours for the usual coin.
Captain Dumaresq, who used to be on ex-
Governor Chelmsford’s staff, is now
D.A.Q.G. at Gibraltar. He writes that,
though he was turned down for trench
work, he has the consolation of feeling he is
releasing a better man to go to the Front.
Mr. D’Apice, the Hunter’s Hill moun-
taineer, has been stricken with illness, so
his place on Governor Strickland’s staff is
temporarily filled by the youthful Geoffrey
Hughes. Geoff, is the younger hope of the
Tom Hughes’ family of two sons. Elder
brother -Roger is just about to leave the
Sydney Uni. to go to the war. Now Geof-
frey, in his teens, is vigorously attacking
the theory and practice of aviation, in order
to presently join a flying corps. This study
allows him a few leisure hours a day on
solid earth. So lie’ll “attend” his Ex', till
duty calls him to his angel trade.
Mr. R. E. N. Twopeny, who died in
England the other day, was an easy-going,
gonial half of the Pastora lists’ Review.
The much-travelled, excitable little Cornish
man, skipper A. W. Pearse, is the surviving
partner of the Review founders. Like our
late visitor, Louise Mack, Twopeny was
born in a country parsonage in S. Aus-
tralia; and the Register taught him the
first things he knew about printers’ ink.
Later in life he was mixed up with many
Exhibitions, but returned to his old love
nearly 25 years ago.
The Red Cross Society had a meeting that
filled the Government House ballroom and
overflowed. Lady Edeline took the chair.
Official secretary Budge assisted in waving
the company into its chairs; and the new
hon. aide Hughes, making himself generally
useful, showed no symptoms of feeling his
position more acutely than any other young-
ster who “attended” for tlie first * time.
It. Y. Braddon added a forcible postscript to
speeches by James Ashton and Hanbury
Davies. The latter had to serve out the
meat of the ceremony. Slowly and without
the visible flicker of an eyelash, the legal
man threaded his way through the lanes and
technical by-ways that lead to a Constitu-
tion. “Perhaps you didn’t know it, but
you’ve been working without a Constitution
all this time,” the horrified lawyer whis-
pered. Subdued giggles from the feminine
phalanx indicated cheerful indifference to a
trifle of that kind. Luckily one thing was
certain. No party could grab the Red Cross
and make political capital out of its great
wt u'k.
James Ashton had a quiet undercut at
Prof. Andy Stuart, whose soul was plagued
with a recent outlay on linoleum for a mili-
tary hospital. “After the war we shall be
better able to more sharply define the limi-
tations of Red Cross expenditure. Just now
we cannot always decide whether the money
should come from the cheerful giver or the
reluctant taxpayer. But we do know that
whether our men want comforts, here, in
Egypt, or elsewhere, we must give what we
can.” Marcus Burldtt, the blind boy-
pianist of Mosman, obliged the company with
some music, and then the word went round
that the frail artist would give a concert to
benefit the Sheepskin Vest Fund in St.
James’s on the 13th. AYhereupon those pre-
sent fell upon the refreshments with great
slaughter.
With a lmrried “Good-bye all!” the un-
obtrusive Dr. Halcomb ran up the gang-
way of a P. and O. liner. For months he
has been quietly looking for an opening in
inlanders. When the authorities cabled
Come!” he was off before his patients had
tune to get a word in his ear. Amongst the
lean kine on his books there will be many
a grateful thought for a medico who con-
tinually “forgot” to send in his bill.
Prof. Andy Stuart’s slim lady is presid-
ing over the meetings that are arranging
to send Christmas boxes to the Kangaroos
m the Lemnos Hospital. The “War Chest”
organisation has promised to attend to the
actual delivery of the plum duff, cigarettes,
etc., and will see that they go to whomsoever
they may be addressed. This hospital is
manned by some of our leading surgeons,
with Surgeon-Col. Fiaschi in charge.
The dancing man is either wilting in the
background or pulling on liis boots for the
Front, if he isn't there already. So the
charity ball committee has to face a difficult
problem. Prices and fancy-dress costumes
are the only lure. St. Martha’s Home com-
mittee recently made a double bid for public
support. Visitors played euchre in the
Town Hall vestibule, or scraped together a
thin supply of dancing men—mostly of an
age that carried an exemption certificate on
its face. The first arrival in the ballroom,
by the way, was a lean tortoiseshell cat,
lured by the lights and the smell of cooked
food.
Gallery attractions before the dance in-
cluded infant prodigies who squeaked out
patriotic tunes —to the accompaniment of
a collection-box rattle —and whirlwind
dancers. The lady whirlwind had a hand-
some mane of tawny hair, which her partner
soon shook out of its pins. A wonderful
Catherine wheel, wrought of “a rag, a bone
and a hank of hair,” was wildly encored.
Mark Higgins stage-managed the platform
side of the entertainment. The Tom Hughes
lady, handsomely upholstered in filmy white
and black, was early on the scene! Mrs.
John Toohey trailed raven draperies into
the card-room. Other committee dames in-
cluded Mrs. Chandler, Mrs. W. Macleod,
and Mrs. W. Porter.
A few busybodies on various women’s
societies have been kicking the ball out of
bounds lately. A fierce desire to round up
their own sex (which is responding to the
war cry without any lash) and “organise”
its efforts is the latest obsession. The
National Council of Women has this noisy
bee in its bonnet; and the hum of the insect
is deafening the platform ladies to such an
extent that they can hardly hear their own
oratory. It buzzed at Government House the
other day, with I)r. Arthur preaching from
his favorite text. “Thrift.” Miss Rose Scott,
though not always a safe guide when the
enemy’s guns are booming, has since brought
the Councillors up with a round turn. Their
business, she reminds them incisively, is to
help the weak and tend the wounded and
keep clear of controversy.
Artist Lionel Lindsay is taking in hand
the design for the programmes for Mrs.
Yvonne Charvin’s concert on October 1).
The lady has been whipping up recruits for
a kind of All Nations’ fancy sets. This
spectacle _is likely to include a wide range
of feminine apparel, from the drapery of
Boadicea to the present flounces of Potts
Point.
The Duffy family, of Mosman, who used
to belong to Bundaberg, had a wedding the
other day. Duffy pere is the thoughtful kind
of parent who gives his daughter a new
house, in addition to his blessing, when the
right young man comes along. I fancy the
last bud has now gone to the altar and has
been Mrs. Gray for a week. The church was
so heavily trimmed with white blooms that it
looked like a study in snowdrifts, and the
wedding breakfast flowed with medical com-
forts. The nice-looking bride was disguised
in more than the usual plumage, and only an
expert could tell just where the satin shim-
mer supported the foam of frothy white top-
terraces.
This week sees National Park used for
the first time by Expeditionaries. Years
ago the field artillery used to have their
annual camp there. Now, Holdsworthy
being otherwise engaged, the batteries go
out to the old, picturesque training-ground.
Waverley’s youngest alderman, Jack
Playfair, is on his way home after two
severe bouts with the enemy. Like other
young soldiers, he is over-sensitive about
the damaged appearance he thinks he is
bringing back. But the scars that show are
the symbols of the first wild rush up Galli-
poli, and the Australian girl simply dotes
on heroes who carry such Distinguished
Conduct Marks.
The local musician is possibly “up
against” the war more acutely than any
other professional. The artists earn a bit
of butter (not 'Auntie Sam’s churning,
Please!) by digressing in poster and other
commercial work. But the luckless musi-
cian finds that the public denies itself his
classic goods as its first sacrifice to the war
god. Hence the welcome to a promised
series of Sunday-afternoon “pops.” Maybe
tbe unco’ guid will attempt to pull J.
Brownlow’s hair for starting the notion.
But Paling’s young mall grows a thick
thatch, so he can spare a lock or two.
The young daughters of Captain Lloyd, of
Bose Bay, gave a thrilling sock and ’bacca
party for the 12tli Light Horse on Sept. 3.
They lured their pals to come in great force,
bringing their socks with them. Father is
Assistant Embarkation Officer, with Sapper
-I- H. A. Lee, formerly of the Engineers, as
his chief.
The Lewis family, whose trade-mark (in
conjunction with Whitty’s) is known in every
kitchen and laundry in Australia, had the
name of a representative written on the Roll
of Honor the other day, young Dudley Lewis
having, succumbed at sea while on his way to
a base hospital. His mother lives at Bur-
wood.
The Fresh Air League had a whirling
day for its raid on the affable public. The
grounds of Crow’s Nest, lent by the Hay
dame, had a dressing of khaki to add to
the glory of the occasion. A group of
returned soldiers were the lions of the
party, and Silva Waugh and other damsels
assiduously a ttended to their wants. The vet-
eran Fresh Air Leaguers, Mrs. Kellick, Mrs.
Ernest Broughton, Mrs. Julius Caro, Mrs.
Adamson. Miss Ford and other lady-pirates
wheedled the elusive shillings out of all
comers. The affair was opened by the wife
of Joseph Carruthers. M.L.C.
Mrs. Harry Finn, who evidently fears no
foe, has undertaken to act as referee in a
sock-knitting competition which closes about
the end of October.
The “Gallipoli” bazaar was uncommonly
lucky to worm its way into a Saturday
afternoon when the crowd came into town.
In spite of heat and dust, it threatens to
stand out as a money-making concern. The
stacks of flowers in Dalget.v's premises were
probably from the bosses’ gardens. Manager
Macrae opened the show, which was
arranged by the feminine .portion of the
firm’s staff.
As race week —in a subdued tone, suitable
to the times —brings a modest revival of
spring fashions, who can be blind to the new
chintz muslins? Our girls will look like
bunches of mixed flowers when they billow
round Randwick lawn next month. Posies
of bloom flourish on most of the new season's
goods, and the hats will put forth
young green tendrils on a lattice-work of
azure and blush-rose ribbon. But you.
mustn’t mention this to I)r. Arthur, who is
expounding the doctrine of No New Clothes.
Verbrugghen—who, by the way, looks as
though he is going to succeed in his wicked
scheme to import his teachers —is timing his
big orchestral concert for the fore part of
October. As the Town Hall will house the
debut, one hopes the new Director will watch
the race dates. Hundreds who love a Horse
woidd also like to be present at this festival
of Fiddle.
The Wentworth Hotel winter garden was
lent the other afternoon for the benefit of the
Wentworth Free Kindergarten. This is
growing into shape, chiefly owing to the per-
sistent clamor of Mrs. Francis Anderson.
Up to June last the infant was ministered to
in a local hall. But the premises were so
violently unsuitable that the lease was not
renewed. Meanwhile, the committee boldly
rushed into bricks and mortar. Now the
hunt for cash is fierce.
The Polish Relief Committee (8 Spring-
street) is again on the trail of your half-
crown. Allies’ Day is a long way ahead for
the starving youngsters of ruined Poland.
Miss Boyle O’Reilly, who saw them, calls
them “the war-mad children who have lost
their parents,” and are going blind and deaf
under their mountain of woe.
Violinist Ivy Wigmore, of Perth, passed
this way the other day. After eight years
in England the Westralian girl is returning
to her native town. She has been most of
the time a student at the Royal College of
Music, where Sir Hubert Parry serves out
the thunder. Ivy could write A.It.C.M.
twice after her name if the world wasn’t so
crowded. She took her associateship for
the fiddle, and then got a second helping for
her voice. Two scholarships, besides, are
to her credit. After the social depression
in England and tho nightly gloom of
shrouded London, Ivy feels as if sunny Aus-
tralia was outside the world of the war.
Oswald Anderson, a local song writer,
gave an evening party at the Repertory to
welcome Peter Dawson and buxom Mrs.
Peter. Anderson, like others of the frater-
nity, did well on the other side till war
broke up the harmony. When the Sydney
man arrived in London a few years' ago.
Peter Dawson extended the hand of friend-
ship to some purpose. He introduced the
man who wrote songs to publishers who pro-
duced them. He sang all the said ballads,
and also gave Anderson the bread-and-butter
billet of accompanist on his provincial
tours. So, as several good turns deserve at
least one good return, the Repertory party
greeted the baritone with loud applause.
Yours affectionately.
VANDORIAN.

MELBOURNE CHATTER
Melbourne, September G, 1915.
My Dear Yarrie, —
The remains of Brigadier-General
Bridges were borne up Collins-street to
► pencer-street station on their way to Can-
berra. Khaki boys with purple in their hats
lined the streets from cathedral to station,
ana more of them marched slowly in the
procession to the strains, of a band. Gov-
ernor-General Ferguson, in scarlet, with
white feathers waving from his cocked hat,
looked more gorgeous than Governor Stan-
ley who walked alongside him in subdued
khaki and a flat field,cap. A few paces
behind the Governors and near the gun-
carriage stalked a tall, portly person in
dingy black and prominent white cuffs, with
a dejected high hat on his head and a pol-
ished . gold watch-chain spread over a
capacious black waistcoat. It was the
undertaker. He eyed the Governors in a
sidelong, ruminative manner, as though he
\\ ere, thinking: “You too shall be mine one
day. And yet he may only have been mus-
ing about his corns or his census card.
A posse of politicians, who marched in
ours, had their physique and deportment
shown up witheringly by the splendid
khaki lads _ who followed them. A
tailor watching the procession must have
wept_ tears of mortification to see such
di essing, and a stage manager would have
£ air to see mere supers shuffle so
untidily. Dady Helen fled along side streets
in a purple and silver limousine, and .joined
the Procession at the train. Mrs. Pearce,
the YV ar Minister’s wife, was another who
went the whole, sad journey.
T be Gh-G. turned up at the Brussels Con-
ce't larty at the Auditorium on Saturday
night. He looked ruddier and cheerier than
er ei, and had with him Lady Doris, who was
wrapped in a delicious white fluffy cloak
AVlth a Toby frill round her throat and ears
and prettv Miss Mandeville, from Hobart
who; was cherry-colored about the cheeks and
pale blue about the clothes. Lady Helen
did not appear.
.AH the latest Belgian arrivals of the
higher plane performed. De Bourguignon, at
the piano, played Chopin in a dreamv way.
Ihe chief soprano 0 f the Brussels * Opera
lheatre, Miss Viceroy (in private life
Mrs. Goossens) sang. She Avore tiny bunches
ot Pink, white and blue roses all over.her
decollete gown; they were dotted round the
tunic; also they appeared blushingly from
under transparencies of ninon like pom-pons.
-Lying all the fluffy gown together was a wide
sash of startlingly-blue ribbon, and a big
pmk cabbage-rose was disposed carefully
?™y. slll P s - It was an imposing creation.
Gellist Baroen performs in a soulful manner,
and looks like King Albert the Good. He
played a Love Song composed by Benburg.
Benburg is the composer of the Brahmin
song that Melba turned into a furious
success. When Baroen got going full-soul
ahead on this Benburg Love Song the audi-
ence made a fuss. They dragged him back
AAuth approving noises, and got tAvo encores
out of him. Florent Hoogstoel played his
violin. He broke a string, ana bis hair stood
up more stiffly than ever and his face grew
even more solemn than usual. These Belgians
seem to have forgotten how to smile; they
are so solemn that they might be adherents
and admirers, of that English monarch who
lost his son in a boating* accident, and was
enveloped in gloom for ever after.
The tall Stewart-Richardson man who
"as here as A.D.C. to Lord Denman has
Avntten to Melbourne friends to say that he
is uoav a major. This is still further proof
that he is not dead, as was erroneously
stated by someone the other day. His
brother absorbed no less than eight bullets
last month, but is doing well. They are
both fighting in Gallipoli.
Young Jacob Jona, Avho lately joined the
doctors’ camp at Seymour, after having had
some suburban practice in Richmond and
I ootscray, is now adjutant to Dr. Morton,
medical O.C. Dr. Jona, whose brother Leon,
or Malvern, is an old hand in the A.M.C.. is
a, burly person with the working capacity
of a Clydesdale.
Though women doctors are not encouraged
at the actual Front, there is now a soldiers’
hospital in London at which the medical
staff consists entirely of women. The
Prahran medicine lady who Avas Mary
Henderson, and is noAv Mary Bell, is on the
spot, waiting to lend a hand.
The last of the ancestral shearing-pens
having been booked to such elderly and
grizzled fleece-removers as still remain in
Victoria because they are too old to fight.
Ernest Austin has handed over his share of
tlie sheep-work to the old man, and left for
the war. The Austin souattage is in the
beautiful Mount Emu Creek country.
Mrs. Slawecka. who lives with her
daughter in a quiet street on the South
Yarr'a hill, has two sons on service. When
she came from Paris a decade ago to help
brother Paul Bibron, the waltz-expert, in
his noble work among- the heavy-steppers of
loorak, the handsome woman brought a
couple of young lads with her. One of
these, France Slawecki, went to Egypt with
a field ambulance, and reached the Darda-
nelles early in the fighting; The other son,
Boleslas, has just gone away as sergeant
with a medical outfit. Slawecki the elder
left Bussian Poland to live in Paris when
the revolution made matters uncomfortable
for him and freedom in his own country.
As the Red Cross funds cannot be drawn
on for the purpose, the Willie Smith and
Bob Blackwood matrons have been officially
advised to start a subscription list in Vic-
toria to aid a charitable enterprise of their
own. These, ladies, with Dr. Helen Sexton,
went to Paris to open a convalescent home
at their own expense, and were allotted half!
of a French going concern to work on. Ap-
parently the costs exceeded expectations.
Hence this new hat.
. Lawyer Sidney Stephen, of Brighton, who
is a brother-in-law of John Madden, is repre-
sented at the Front by son Clive, a young
doctor lately out of the Melbourne Univer-
sity. At latest advices lie was working at a
base-hospital near Boulogne.
Major G. PI. Knox has become a Lieut.-
Colonel, and his wife is going to Egypt soon
to congratulate him. The children are to be
left meanwhile at Coonac.
Sep Nichols, who was motoring in Eng-
land with his daughters when the war
started, has turned the conveyance into an
ambulance. It is working in France at pre-
sent, while the daughters sit in London and
sew shirts for soldiers.
The handsome young Baron von Bissing
has not gone to. Germany to fight, after all.
A friend met him on the Block last month.
Let us hope that this statement will dispose
of that other rumor as to his having been
given a commission in an Australian regi-
ment at Gallipoli.
Mrs.. Purves, widow of the K.C., has been
hard hit by the war. Her son Walter was
reported wounded and missing some months
since, and in the last charge of the Victorian
Light Horse another boy, Godfrey, was
killed. : ?
Molly Carew, singer and parlor-vaudevil-
lette, left by the Omrah. This is her second;
voyage.to England. During the previous visit;;
she plighted her troth to a bright young
man who is now a medical officer with an*
R.A.M.C. job in a military hospital. They
are to be married shortly, bar accidents.
Mrs. Fred Hughes, whose Colonel husband
had command of the Light Horse Brigade
which was said to be practically swept
away, endured an anxious few days after
the. great battle. Then came a glad cable
saying that both her husband and son (the
Colonel’s orderly) were safe.
Dr. Ramsay Smith, who seems to have
got things tangled up in Egypt, used to be
the coroner in Adelaide. He is a singularly
abrupt man. One could almost picture him
speaking sharply to the corpse. He never
tempered the curse for the;-negligent official,
or sugared the piil of rebuke in a case of
proven incompetence. Wherefore there are
likely to be plenty who, will gloat over his
evil luck. On the other nail#. be has worked
hard for the under-dog. and has doled out
much kindness under cover of an abrupt
manner. Of recent years he has drawn his
salary as President of the Board of Health.
He has a charming wife and some children,
who are sure to be anxious to know what
all the fuss is about.
From my Adelaide correspondent:—
The Government has decided to purchase the
Mount Crawford estate for £35,000—a bit over £6
an acre. Mr. Murray remains in possession .for three
years, and then takes the whole of fh‘e a purchase
money in Government bonds at 4t per cent.
The Cheer-Up Society is pushing- ahead with its
Soldiers’. Club. All that remains is the question of
a free site. A free plan and free labor for building
the place have already been acquired.
The first annual meeting of the South Aus. Branch
of the Red Cross Society has been arranged for the
Wednesday afternoon of the G.-G.’s visit, when Lady
Helen Munro Ferguson will be one of the speakers.
Others who will say a word or two are Ladv Gal-
way, the Premier, Dr. Stirling, Josiah Syition and
the Rev. D. McNicol. By the way, Dr. Brummit
has given up the cash-box, and Howard Davenport is
treasurer in his stead.
The Master of the Supreme Court, Major W. L.
Stuart, has got leave of absence and gone into train-
ing. Colonel Lovely’s third son is also at the Exhibi-
tion Camp. He was at Mackunda Downs in Northern
Queensland when he heard of his brother’s death
from meningitis. At once settling his business affairs
he came down to fill up the gap in the South Aus.
contingent. The Josiah Symons’ eldest son tem-
porarily forsakes law for soldiering next week, and
departs by the Maloja. He went from St. Peter’s
College to Oxford, and is a junior partner in his
father’s firm.
The hills districts in and about Aldgate and
Crafers couldn’t arrange for their Australia Day
celebrations on July 30, so they ran a carnival on
Saturday, and netted about £OOO for the Wounded
Soldiers’ Fund.
K. L. Duffield, who went from Adelaide to join the
North Devon Hussars, has been appointed transport
officer of his regiment.
We are to have no Eight Hours Procession this
3 f ai , but instead an Anzac Day Procession and Car-
niral on behalf of the Wounded Soldiers’ Fund.
_ Died at Evandale on Monday It. C. Mitton, a one-
time headmaster'of the Pultney-street School. He
was one 1 of the founders of the Preceptors’ Associa-
tion of the Educational Journal, a publica-
tion Which was edited by the late Dean Russell
and which nearly half a century ago advocated an
educational system much on the lines of the one
maintained by the State to-day.
Captain George Landale, who has been
training “Terriers” in England since his re-
turn ]to the army list, is awaiting his call to
the trenches. Walter is already there, and
now there only remains Algy, a tall, fair and
somewhat vacuous young man. And he
expects to be off shortly.
George Chirnside and his daughter Aud-
rey are going to Egypt to help as best they
can. Mrs. George, who is not too robust of
late, will stay on at Werribee, the family’s
bouth Yarra house, Airlie, having been sold
and turned into a private hospital.

Is it a subtle; sneer at the costume supplied

Captain Jimmy Smeal, of the Melbourne
xiosprtal, left the other day with a contingent
ot doctors for service abroad. The committee
gloaned on first hearing of Smeal’s decision
to go, but presently remembered its prime

turn The new, young man at the hospital is

Herewith a few tips for military shirt-
makers :—-
Don’t use flannel collars; they shrink.
Don't have collar higher than fin. It shows above
the tunic.
Don’t forget to put on a cuff that buttons.
Don’t have the front opening shorter than 14in.
Don’t have the yoke (from shoulder to shoulder)
wider than 19in. .
Don't use linen buttons ; they get dirty, and break.
Don’t herringbone; or leave raw edges; or make
pleats ; or put seaihs on the inside. All’these devices
make harbors for vermin.
. Et’orn Rosna. who is staying so long
in London that she is probably rooted there
by now:—
Hymen’s latest Anglo-Australian job was at the
wedding of Harold Ellison, of the High Commis-
sioner’s Office, with Kathleen Gorst, and it happened
at St Mary Abbot's, Kensington. The benedict is
the eldest son of the late Dr. Ellison, of Glenelg
(S.A.). The bride belongs to a scribbling family
and is a grand-daughter of Sir John Gorst, who has
a castle in Wilts. Her father, who lately dropped
his pen for the less mighty weapon, is now a lieu-
tenant in a Territorial regiment, . and came home
wounded from France in time to give the damsel
away. A younger Ellison brother, who is likewise a
lieutenant, upheld the bridegroom, and the Sydney
organist, Arthur Mason, played the Wedding March.
Part of the British Army ran over from France for
the event.
Elizabeth Newbold, the young Victorian siiwer
sends a eooee from Noo Yark. The tuneful girl was
in Italy when the European combustion started and
threatened to spoil her career. However, grand-
opera managers in U.S.A. hearkened unto her with
such good results that at the moment of writing she
had two five-year contracts to choose from.

A dear old Dutch lady, the widow of the
late Colonel Lethbridge—an Anglo-Indian—
has just died. She was a daughter of one
Gustav d'Albedyll, an Important in the East
India Company, and had lived in India and
m the East more than half her life. She was
with her son, the Rev. E. Lethbridge, of All
Saints’ Church, Terang, -when the end came.
The Macoboy girls appear at the Audi-
torium on September 14. John Maher,
assistant Town Clerk, is managing the show
for them, and Mrs. Trentham Fryer is at-
tending to the details. They are said to be
glorious, and as they are popular in Sassiety
the concert is expected to boom. Their only
brother, a corporal, attached to the 14th
Battalion, was killed in the landing at Galli-
poli, and the Expeditionaries are anxious to
do the family honor.
“Toddy” Waller, wTo was in Australia
with his father’s company last year, has
been divorced. As Ethel Warwick the wife
played in “The Flag Lieutenant” and other
genteel bellowdramatic productions. Toddy
is a sound actor, with a fine sense of humor
and immense feet. His wife that w 7 as is al
very good-looking Jewmss, but her sense of'
the ridiculous is defective. According to
the law reports of the case, “on leaving the
witness-box she was completely overcome
with emotion, and had to be assisted out of
court by friends in a swooning condition.”
Possibly the sudden shock of seeing the face
of her spouse, with whom she has not been
playing speaks for years, brought the oldi
love back with a rush.
An artistic Australian variety of the little
“Maxim” booklet which flourishes in New
Lcrk and London has been brought out by
Edith Willard, a Melbourne presswoman.
It is clothed in violet cardboard, tied with
mauve ribbon, and titled “Maxims of the
Corset.” The Keystone Printing Co. did
the fixing and Charlie Nuttall put in the
illustrations. The maxims are much as
usual, if not more so. “Anyone can make
a success of modesty—only a‘few succeed
with impudence” is a fair sample. Remarks
on figures, and corsets range from Cleopatra
to the Josephine period, and the advts. are
as. lull of appeal to women-readers as Mrs.
Willard’s advice.
Julius Knight dropped off the express at
Seymour the other week and gave a show.
The house consisted of something like two
men and a boy—not that the recruits were
indifferent to . Superbus, but because menin-
gitis is causing wholesale curtailment of
night leave at the camp just now.
Carlyle Smythe, son of the “Much Trav-
elled,” is providing weekly entertainment sv
for the Australian wounded at Harefield.
Una Bourne, Mrs. Benda, Mona McCaughey '
and Carrie Haase supplied the first per-
formance ; then Alexander Watson gave a
one-man show; and Ada Crosslev. Elsa '
Stralia (who used to be Elsa Fischer) and
others were to follow when the mail left.
After giving shrill voice to two of the
latest shrieks of fashion in “Under Cover,”
at the Royal, Muriel Starr appears, so to
speak, in a perfect dream of a nightie. The
famous garment which Henry James James
married for the purposes of “The Double
Bed Dialogues” could not compare with this
complication of silk and foamy lace, festooned
with pink ribbon. Muriel reveals herself in
this in a bedroom scene, and in the act that
follows, when a burglar alarm has aroused
the house-party, she comes downstairs with-
out putting on anything in the way of a
kimono, but wearing in addition to her
nightie an elaborate cap that looks like an
edition de luxe of the sunbonnet associated
with musical-comedy dairymaids.
The Sandringham goffers made £5OO for
the Red Cross as a result of one day’s
special work. Each round brought the cause
half a sovereign. Lunch was costly, and
every member had a host of women guests.
The weather was straight from the South
Pole, but the girls didn’t much mind having
their skirts, blown. about, since sports boots
are very tricky this season—being high and
laced at. the. side in fine ballet style. Club-
house flirtations, coffee and fur coats w'ere
in high favor throughout the blizzardish day.
When Lady Northcote gave a party at
Government House years ago, her passion
for detail carried her to the tasting of the
soup or coffee she always ordered for the
chauffeurs and coachmen in waiting. >Now T
she is probably overlooking the flavoring
which the tetrarch in her kitchen drops into
the broth for wounded Australians. Thev’re
going in relays to her home, Eastwell Park.

Nerve Wracked
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
JADY, give heed !
ithin this Land of Nervous Dread,
Where Strange Things dying and
Strange Things dead
Slink by with noiseless, furtive tread ;
Where is no healing light of the sun,
Nor the dark of night when the day is done.
Lady, my sister, in this my need,
Lady, I pray you, give heed, give heed !
Lady, give heed !
There must be somewhere some holy place,
wit ( d by Love and the light of your face,
\\ here one might dally a nleasant space
And be as the green things growing there,
I houghtless as they and as free of care.
It there be so, be it yours to lead ;
Lady, my sister, give heed, give heed!
Lady, give heed !
touc h of your tender hand would lay
Ihese bodiless fears at their cruel play :
Lei ter ant whisperings, where were they,
Did you bid them cease from their teasiim
din *
The blood-starved rooms of my brain within?
I crave of your kindliness in my need,
Lady, my sister, give heed, give heed !
Lady, give heed !
For you, so gentle, are surely kind,
And the utter peace of a quiet mind
Is yours to yield, were you so inclined ;
Or (maddening thought!) are yourself as
well
Some Fata Morgana the fiends in Hell
As a last refinement of pain, concede?
Lady, my sister, give heed, give heed !
Lady, give heed!
If overwrought and my thoughts awry,
I have judged you wrongly, it is not I
But the brood of my need’s extremity:
I call on you now as a man unmanned.
As a very child, for a press of your hand ■
If not of your perfect lips indeed ;
I pray you, give heed, give heed !
Lady, give heed !
JLhe tense brain giddies ; a grisly rout
Dr sick foreshadowings gad about
Above and beneath, within and without.
Like a saraband in a madman’s dream :
Sister, my sister, or else I scream,
How can I move you, how can I plead?
Lady, my sister, will you not heed !
S.A.
HANNIFORD G. RUSSELL.
Mahupawa's Curse.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Walter hamlin blew into
Poneke in the early days, before
the earthquake had razed part of
the town and provided subject matter for
a latter-day army of hoary-headed violators
of the truth. Knocking round had become
a habit with Walter. But he kept a pair
of sharp eyes on the look-out for the tide
which was to lead on to fortune. At
Poneke something told him that the tide
was due to flow. So he resigned his menial
position on the barque William ,T. Cooker
and started out for the back country. His
resignation was effected by means of a
small iron bar. It was not till two hours
later that the watchman knew what had
struck him, and by that time Walter was
scaling the precipitous sides of the Rimu-
takas and cursing the slop chest of the Wil-
liam J. Cocker for providing him with
brown-paper boots.
On the far side of the mountains he
walked into a Maori kianga and was haled
before Mahupawa. Mahupawa was not
only the chief of the Ngatirunas and boss
tohunga of the whole of Wairarapa, but a
man of discernment. In Walter he recog-
nised a kindred spirit. They became close
friends. But the lotus life was not for Mr.
Hamlin. He saw a local need and deter-
mined to remedy it.
There was a gorgeous spree when the
first fruits of the still were available for
consumption. Walter was acclaimed the
savior of the brown people. As a supreme
honor Mahupawa presented the pakeha with
his daughter. \\ alter had already provided
himself with a bride, so the lady was some-
thing of a superfluity in his menage. But
the dusky property brought appreciable
prestige.
When the high spirits engendered by the
first brew had died down, and the dead had
been buried, and the smouldering feuds
stowed away until the next time, Walter
decided that the time had come to look after
his own interests. He had a heart to heart
talk with Mahupawa. For a small quantity
of waipiro he purchased a large tract of
land. There was plenty of land and not
too much waipiro. Mahupawa intimated he
was always open to do business on the same
terms. Satisfied that the title was all right,
Walter spent two days examining his pro-
perty. Then he returned to the kainga and
called a big korero. Afterwards as many
natives as could be supplied with axes fell
to on the virgin bush. The work continued
for many months, the workers being paid
ia waipiro. The shirkers fell foul of Mahu-
pawa, whose mana as a tohunga exceeded
that of all others, and affliction seized them.
Some died, others merely developed leprosy
and were ostracised by the tribe.
When the Government road at last
arrived from Poneke, and after halting a day
meandered off into the bush again, Walter
utilised it to put his waipiro on the market.
He organised a transport service and a
system of bush telegraphy, and in the
course of years became a wealthy man. He
fenced his property, stocked it, and built a
large house. Then, for the first tisy“ Vr he
went down to Poneke. aiawn
He returned with a pakeha brii 1 / a 1 1 ? d
an escort of armed men. The nat n<
eluding Mahupawa’s daughter, wer? ' I pj'n
half an hour in which to get off tl V t A<am-
lin Estate. To prevent their retu£ e , xooun-
dary riders were stationed along the fence.
A CASE IN POINT.
THE MORALIST: “Strange how different causes produce similar results.”
THE CYNIC : "Quite true! Here's Jones won't go to the war because his wife don’t want
him to; and Smith won't go because his wife does want him to.”
For a week Mahupawa spoke to no man.
Then he called the tribe together, and
uttered his curse. It was a terrible and
comprehensive malediction, but it did not
extend to the fourth generation. There
was to be no fourth generation. By way
of inauguration Mahupawa slipped past the
watchers one night, and gaining access to
the homestead blew the greater part of Wal-
ter Hamlin’s head off. Then he went away
and quietly died.
* * * *
The humidity of the night had converted
the fragrance of the bush into a stench that
drifted across the hundred yards of lawn and
garden to the homestead verandah. Perhaps
it was the night which had unbalanced
young Bycroft’s brain and changed the futile
soul-agony into a blood lust, but his pulse
quickened as he lay back in the deep wicker
chair and watched the place in Hamlin’s
bull neck where the knife would sink—if —
if Of course, he knew she loved him and
was only marrying Hamlin because she had
promised him years before. This Bycroft, who
had known her for ten days, had learned in
the rose garden the night before, and by no
word of mouth. At one moment their souls
had almost fused. Only her promise to
Hamlin had made her flee into the house.
God, how he loathed that man! Over the
verandah rails there swung the vision of a
girl with coils and coils of shimmering chest-
nut hair and great grey eyes. Black hate
swamped the boy’s soul, and the vision was
blotted out.
The mutter of talk died down for an
instant. Then the third man spoke. He
was asking about the curse. Only Hender-
son, thought Bycroft, would have had the
bad taste to speak openly of the thing to
Walter Hamlin’s grandson. But it was
balm to Bycroft’s soul to see the red ebb
from Hamlin’s face. There was a pause,
and then the big man spoke from the depths
of his chair.
“Well, yes, old man, there was some talk
of the Curse when the nater made such a
mess of things, but it was only old wives’
talk, I guess. There were good, solid rea-
sons for the old man making an end of it.
Anyhow, I’ve lived for thirty-five years
without being affected by any particular
curse, and as I’m going to be married next
n Sionth there’s not a big chance of the Ham-
lins dying out with the third generation.”
Hamlin got up and stretched himself, and
to put an end to the situation Henderson
did likewise. Then they moved towards the
door. But Bycroft sat very still and
straight in his chair, and his face was white
as death.
Three miles from the homestead was bush-
clad Mahupawa Hill. When Mahupawa’s
body was found on the summit of the hill
the Maoris declared the place tapu, and
it became the habitat of wild pigs and the
breeding-place of many birds. When
Bycroft came up from the south in search
of a station the Hill became an important
asset in Hamlin’s mind. Bycroft had loads
of cash and sporting proclivities. Hamlin
was marrying money—some said for money
—but he wanted all the gold he could lay
hands on. The old man had indeed made a
mess of things.
Bycroft was obsessed by an idea. At
night it took shape as an ever-recurring
nightmare which left him in the morning
a nervous wreck. At breakfast, when Ham-
lin showed in a dozen ways his proprietor-
ship of Letty Gordon, it became a stinging
desire. In the daytime Bycroft generally
rode alone, ostensibly to inspect the pro-
perty. But once away from the homestead
the pasture land received little attention.
His idea drove him to the hill where the
natives said the spirit of Mahupawa roamed
never to be appeased until the last of the
Hamlins was underground. In between
whiles he played tennis with the half-dozen
girl guests, or dallied with Letty Gordon
in the rose garden. It was Letty who
brought him to a decision. The ladies had
begun to talk, and, woman-like, they took
care that Letty should know it. It was not
her nature to evade responsibilities, and she
spoke to Bycroft in level tones and told
him. without sign of feeling, that this was
the end of things. That night Bycroft
announced his desire for some sport on
Mahupawa Hill.
Hamlin was in great spirits while waiting
for the dogcart to come round. Bycroft had
intimated he would buy the place if the hill
proved as food for sporting purposes as its
reputation. With the run sold and the
mother settled comfortably in Wellington
with her sister, Hamlin would be free to
take his long-projected honeymoon trip to
Europe. But Hamlin hardly felt “the thing”
this morning, though he showed his usual
outward aspect of coarse jollity. Standing
apart from the bevy of women who fluttered
around, Bycroft inwardly cursed Henderson
for a clumsy ass. For at the last minute
Henderson had decided to go with them.
Transient compunction gripped Bycroft
when Hamlin’s mother came out to see them
off. Grief and incessant anxiety for her
son’s safety had infirmed but not over-
whelmed Francis Hamlin’s widow. She
spoke cheerfully to Bycroft and wished him
luck in his day’s sport. Then the dogcart
appeared and Hamlin’s leave taking of Letty
Gordon brought a red mist before Bycroft’s
eyes.
At the foot of the hill they tethered the
horse and tumbled the dogs out of the cart.
Hamlin’s boisterousness had continued all
the way, and now he broke into a song
which brought to Bycroft’s mind a vision of
Letty Gordon at the piano and her lover
bending over her. When Henderson joined
the vision faded.
For an hour they climbed behind the mute
dogs through dense underbush. Then Hender-
son lied about a sprained wrist, and started
back for the road. At least, he started back,
and said he was making for the road. As
the sound of his progress through the bush
died down the dogs gave the full-throated
cry of the pig-canine when it has found and
the quarry is not far distant. Hamlin and
Bycroft struggled on. After a while the
dogs’ cry changed to the yapping bark which
denotes that the pig is bailed up. The sound
came from the top of the hill.
Hamlin pushed through the ferns and
entered the clearing, with Bycroft at his
heels. On the far side, a dozen yards away,
with his tail against a huge totara, was an
old-man boar. In front of him the dogs
were doing a song-and-dance turn. For the
dogs the old fellow displayed contempt,
broken by short bursts of senile rage at the
futility of their noisy performance. The
appearance of the men changed his feelings
and tactics. He seemed to suddenly recollect
an appointment made for another place. As
the pig sprang forward with a toss of his
head which opened a dog’s side from flank to
shoulder, Hamlin swung his rifle up. Then
something caught bis foot. He stumbled
and went down. The crack of a rifle hung
in the air, then echoed from gully to gully.
Hamlin lay where he had fallen.
Bycroft never knew how he got down the
hill, and found Henderson and the cart. As
a matter of fact, Henderson found him. He
was incoherent then, and still more so at the
homestead. But he received a lot of sym-
pathy.
* * * *
Six months after Hamlin’s death Bycroft
returned from a tour of the world. On tha
second night after he arrived in Wellington
he proposed to Letty Gordon, and was re-
fused. The lady had just accepted Hender-
son.
Then Bycroft went away and buried him-
self in the bush. But Henderson found him
out, and dragged him back to civilisation.
Henderson was a human vampire, and threat-
ened to suck him dry.

THE QUESTION.
HIS WORSHIP (to plaintiff): “Do you mean
to infer that the defendant made over-
tures to you of a financial character
prior to the theft?” (Question repeated,
and no response from stupid witness.)
"Constable, see if you can make the
man understand.”
CONSTABLE: “Tell his Washup did the
prisoner put the acid on yell afore lie
lunched per dough?”
Sugna” : The vegetable’s birthplace has a
good deal to do with the warpableness of timber.
But the district to which it is transplanted has
still more to do with it. I have been all my life
at the game, yet until I went to Canberra I didn’t
know what wood could do in the contortion line. I
knew a squatter who when he shifted to the locality
of Canberra (then Acton) from another part of N.
S. Wales, brought a valuable old fiddle with him'.
It had been in the family over 100 years, he said,
yet it had not been long in its new home when it
began to show signs of warp. The same thing hap-
pened in the case of an old flute. What price, then,
Oregon and N.S.W. hardwood, which have been
mostly used for building at Canberra? If it comes
to handing out first prize for wriggling I’ll nominate
tallow-wood against “Boronia’s” (B. 19/8/’l5)
jarrah. If the contest is held at Canberra, even a
rib of the Ark could be guaranteed to get out of
plumb.
‘’Outcast”: You don’t expect to be hard
pressed for water in a country like Papua (the
general complaint is that most spots there get too
much of it), but as a matter of fact there is a
huge area where fresh Adam’s ale is as hard to find
as a non-borrowing Australian Treasurer. From
Orokolo to the Dutch boundary I reckon you can’t
find decent fresh water anywhere along the coast.
F.ven the nigs., and there are a good many thousand
of them in this area, have to depend mostly on
either cocoanuts or the rain-water collected in their
canoes. If you want to get drinking-water anywhere
between the bight of the Papuan Gulf and the mouth
of the Bensbach you have to go from 20 to 80 miles
up one or other of the rivers. In the mountain
country things are not quite as bad, but if you climb
up high the chances are you won’t get any water
along the ranges. Have had much personal experi-
ence of having to spread canvas to catch rain at night
to get enough to cook grub for self and the boys. In
the country back of the Papuan Gulf the formation
is mostly limestone, and the country carries no sur-
face water. It doesn’t even carry beer.
* * * ' *
“The Wanderer” : On the Upper Manning
(N.S.W.) there is a place called “The Cells.” To
thal title hangs a tale. In the spring of 1847, in the
“bad old days of Port Macquarie,” Governor and
Lady £ itzroy visited that settlement. Major A. C.
Innes met the party at what was then the Major’s
cattle station, Glen Innes, now a thriving town.
From there their Exes, passed on to Yarrowitch, an-
ot lei of Innes’s stations, thence to a wayside camp.
Here the Major had had a large wooden hut erected
tor their accommodation. The bedrooms were so
small that they were more like ordinary convict cells
than anything else—so much so, in fact, that it is
recorded the Major, when the bed-hour came, asked
Ins guests whether they were ready “to retire to their
cells. The pleasantry is perpetuated in the name.
* * *
“Jaques”: “Viking” (B. 19/8/T5) asks
if foxes have been spotted further north than Nanango
(Q.). Yes, they have been cursed at settlements on
the Daly River. I dunno whether it’s necessary to
mention that the Daly is 800 miles nearer the North
Pole than Nanango.
* * * *
“Leo Lear”: A word with “Pick” (B.
12/8/ 15). After groping in much obscurity on the
subject of eel-birth, scientists with rare
exceptions now acknowledge that the
young elvers come to life in the depths
of the sea. The chief objections to
this theory are (1) that young eels
an inch or so long are found at the
head of streams miles from the sea,
and (2) on the top side of waterfalls.
But shoals of infantile wrigglers have
often been observed entering the mouths
of rivers and getting a gait on up-
stream. Coming to waterfalls, obser-
vation again butts in. Hopkins Palls,
near Warrnambool (Vic.), would at
first glance appear an impassable bar-
rier to the small worms. Yet repeat-
edly they have been seen working their
passage up the wet rocks there as in
similar places. Anyone with time and
patience can verify these two facts for
himself. To refute the sea-breeding
theory a given number of eels would
have to be put under observation in
perfectly natural surroundings, except-
ing that there would be no exit or
entrance for them. If young were then
found it would be a starting-poiut for
assailing the established theory. The
trouble is we too readily accept any
old yarn that “bush naturalists'’ put
up on us, such, for instance, as that
eels have their origin in horsehair, that
snakes won’t relinquish the ghost till
sunset, that young ’roos are born in
the pouch, that politicians feather their
nests, and that jackeroos swallow their
young. Wherefore, no eel-breeding
scheme will collect my credence till
that of the scientists hits been publicly poisoned.
* * * *
Sarto”: Cheers for another good Aus-
tralian the she-oak. During the recently-deceased
drought many cattle were not only kept alive but
fattened on its leaves. While I’m handing out
references I may as well pass one to the quandong
or wild cherry, whose fruit in the bush has cheered
up many a man and made many a pot of jam for
Mrs. Mulga. When Nature invented the quandong
she was off her rocker, because she placed the stone
on the outside of the fruit.

Baicoo : I quoted to a canny engineer
“Mulga’s” argument (B. 12/8/’l5) that gidya was
the timber for stoking. “Aye,” he said, “if ye use
the other fellow’s boiler.” The heat from gidya, he
explained, was too fierce and burnt out the “coffee-
pot ’ too quickly. “Next to coal,” said this expert,
is coolibali. It gives a steady, regular heat, last-
ing, and with little ash. But, ye ken, if I were in
Mest or Nor’-West Queensland I’d be speering for
wilga or vinetree.”
*‘ * *
“Pick”: With the solitary exception of
Queensland teak “Boronia” hasn’t a hope of finding
any Australian timber which will not, in a greater
or lesser degree, warp and shrink, just like Westra-
uan jarrah (B. 19/8/’l5). Further, jarrah does not
hold the warp championship. That is rightly claimed
V the silky oak of N. S. Wales. Even though it be
tremendously weighted down and kept in the dark
until absolutely dead, it invariably springs when
worked. Its beautiful grain and color make it a
suitable wood for fancy parts in the construction of
vehicle bodies, but it must be iron-braced on all sides
or on the first run it will twist. Wood shrinkage is
ue to sap, and may be overcome by immersing the
gieen timber m water or oil. The soakage drives
out the sap, and thereafter, when dry, the stuff may
o relied upon to keep its measurements.

Goody : Gaols are not what they were in
the golden days of the West. At Niagara once Con-
stable Tim Halloran, having adhered Paddy the Dog
to “the log,” went off for a convivial glass with the
landlord of The Dryblowers’ Friend. As they were
filling them up again in the boss’s sanctum a commo-
tion in the attracted their attention. It was
Paddy balancing . the log against the counter and
pouring a pannikin of rum into his interior. The last
I recollect of that night was Halloran and his
prisoner staggering away into the darkness lugging
the gaol between them.

THE SNAKE THAT BIT O'REILLY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
O Reilly cooked at Grundell ere he stood out on the
grass;
The shearer chaps esteemed him somewhat highly,
I'or though he lathered up a treat, and pubs he
couldn’t pass,
And did his silver in with blacks and folk of little
class,
Of all the Murrumbidgee cooks from Mungadale to
Yass
There was none could hold a candle to O’Reilly.
His duffs were just the glassy, and his rock-me dols
a treat;
liis soup had been acclaimed by “Booser” Smiley ;
His sea-pie was a luxury that aldermen might eat,
His brownie like a wedding cake; his bread you
couldn’t beat.
A lazy, fat and surly bloke, but, chef from head to
feet,
The shearers voted solid for O’Reilly.
He donned his cap and apron, and he cooked for
Outback Joe.
“I’ll fatten up the brutes,” said Patrick, dryly.
He snatched his cheque at Cut-Out, and he ducked
for Pongelo,
And into awful Carathool The simpleton must go,
Where Johns are swift and sudden, and the town is
Holy Joe.
On a roaring razzle-dazzle went O’Reilly.
He put the peg in later, when the town was painted
red.
His face and beak were likewise tinted highly.
The welkin shook next morning when he tumbled
from his bed
With roars of “Strike me bandy!” and appeals to
“Strike me dead!”—
Upon a tiger-snake he lit, a foot below its head,
And thrice it bit the leg of P. O’Reilly.
When Patrick finished cursing in his loud, emphatic
way,
111 bring you, said the Boss, “to Doctor Keilly ;
Put on your hat and coat, man, and I’ll run you into
Hay.”
Said Pat: “Run out some whisky, for ’tis here I
mean to stay.
I’ll see you burnt and roasted ere I leave the hut
to-day.
A fig for all your snakebites!” said O’Reilly.
“Gorstrike me pink and purple! Do you take me for
a fool?
I’m ’noculated, Boss,” said Paddy, slyly;
“I carry in my carcase of the grog of Carathool
An antidote for all-the snakes from here to Mumble-
dool.”
O'Reilly went on cooking. ’Twixt the barracks and
the school
We found it, dead—the snake that bit O’Reilly.

Beauty Doctor Gives Simple Recipe to
Darken Grey Hair and Promote Its
Growth.
Miss Alice Whitney, a well-known beauty
doctor, recently gave out the following
statement: “Anyone can prepare a simple
mixture at home, at very little cost, that
will darken grey hair, promote its growth
and make it soft and glossy. To a half-pint
of water add loz. of bay rum, a small box
of Orlex Compound and }oz. of glycerine.
These ingredients can be bought at any
chemist’s at very little cost. Apply to the
hair twice a week until the desired shade is
obtained. This will make a grey-haired per-
son look 20 years younger. It is also fine
to promote the growth of the hair, relieve
itching and scalp disease, and is excellent
for dandruff and falling hair.” [G

"1\ .C.M.” : In the limber section I intend
entering what in the Braidwood (N.S.W.) district is
called mountain-ash. It is as hard as a Hun's heart,
will turn the edge of any axe if hit across the grain,
and is used for mauls. It is also the best fuel I ever
’•aised steam with. From the leaves is distilled an
essential oil, which ranks high in the making of
perfumes. The vegetable is one of the tall variety ;
shafts of SOft. to the first branch are common. The
leaves, grow in bunches at the end of the branches.
The under side of the leaf is red, and seen on a
windy day the trees are as handsome as any other.
The wood polishes l etter than blackwood, having
much the same color and grain, but its hairiness keeps
it out of the cabinet-maker’s shop.
“F.11.R. : lie keeping mice from hay-
stacks, grain, flour, etc. Use sulphur. Sprinkle it
over the bags or about the stack, and you will find
the rodents give it as wide a berth as the teetotaller
gives “colonial wallop.”
GARDENING FOR AMATEURS.
“Rag” : They’re pretty casual about their
duffing in the Gulf country. I was a passenger once
per buckboard between tiie Einasleigh and Kidston,
when we happened on to a mob of springers grazing
unattended. We pulled up, and I was handed the
reins while the proprietor of the vehicle got close to
the beasts with his rifle. When he beckoned me to
drive up 2cwt. of young bullock lay dead. The
brigand cut the brand out and shredded it to bits,
skinned the carcase, cut out what meat he wanted,
rubbed salt and sugar into it, shoved it into coarse
sacks, and resumed the voyage.

“McKais”: If “Sammy” lias found no good
in any of the recipes offered him for his acroplaning
horse let him take a piece of sapling about an inch
shorter than the hobble-chain and hitch it along the
chain. That 11 stop any kangarooing-, because a horse
when about to spring places his front paws together,
and he can't do it when the piece of timber is in
the way.
“To Babarantiko”: “Lagani-Namo” (B.
2-///’ln) helps to throw some unnecessary mud in
connection with the murder of Mae Kay,
of Lindenhafen, New Britain. Mac know
well enough the danger of touching at the
village where he was killed. In addition,
he had been warned. Why lie anchored
nobody but himself will ever know. Any-
how, three of the niggers implicated
have paid the penalty for the job—one
of them the leader. The reason for the
killing seems to have been just “general
principles.” The nigger lives up to his
convictions, as a rule. He knows what
he wants and goes after it with charming-
directness. Usually he pays for his fun,
too. To do him justice, he doesn’t mind.
A lady of New Guinea, arrested in con-
nection with the killing- of Mae Kay, gave
three different reasons for it. (1) Some
white man once upon a time stole a pig
frotn the village, and it seemed a fair
thing that it should be paid for. (2)
They did not like white men. (3) They
had never “had” a white man, so could
not afford to miss the opportunity. Reasons enough,
you see. Each one, from the niggers’ point of view,
a casus belli.

“Cross Roads” : Any experienced horseman
who tames the wild brumby uses the smallest enclo-
sure .possible. Harry Dempsey, father of Dempsey
brothers, mentioned by “Barrier” (B. 19/S/’10), has
had the brumby in the open without a bridle, but I’ll
wager if his advice was asked in the matter he would
say: “The small yard, sonny, every time.” If any-
body knows more about the game than Dempsey, it's
his next talk.
“Flinclerino”: “Rag’s” encounter with a
native cat in a vermin trap (B. 12/8/’l5) reminds
me of Jimmy Ah Ling, licensed fisherman, of Trinity
Inlet (N.Q.). One evening Ah Ling navigated his
flatty up to his fish-trap near Blackfellows’ Creek, and
made fast to one of the ti-tree uprights. The fall-
ing tide was still 3ft. deep in the trap, so the
Chinkie discarded his slacks and climbed into the
enclosure to gather the fish. Then the excitement
commenced. Ah Ling trod on something which
straightway threw a back handspring and snapped at
him like a steam bulldog. Simultaneously Ah Ling
let out a Foochoo yell and turned a double-somer-
sault. The cause of his indisposition was an ener-
getic crocodile, and although it was less than a yard
long it made strenuous efforts to swallow the China-
man whole. In the rough-and-tumble, the trap partly
collapsed and the flatty broke adrift. Then the
crocogator and the other live stock made their escape.
Ah Ling, trouserless and boatless, was left hanging
on to the top ends of the trap sticks, and was nearly
eaten by mosquitoes and sandflies before he was found
and rescued. Since then he takes extreme precautions
with a long pole before entering a submerged trap.

“Umnma”: Meandering round the Louis-
indes Archipelago 1 met the Dead Finish in beach-
combers. We talked about the war, of course, and
•he gave me some news about the invasion of England
by J. Caesar. What I couldn’t make out was his re-
peated reference to Clara Patrick. “Wot!” lie ex-
claimed in astonishment when I confessed my ignor-
ance. “Nivver ’card of Clara Patrick ! Wlioi, they’ve
got a moniment to her needle in London if the
blarsted Germans haven’t bust it up.” Which recalls
a well-known Australian sport who had trouble with
his sons. “Gorstruth,” he exclaimed to his solicitor,
“it’s enough to make Mary McDillon turn in her
grave.” “Mary McDillon?” said the attorney, “Mary
McDillon?” “Yes, yes,” said the sport; “you know
—the'woman in the Bible.”
“Moana” : The sparrow pest is well and
hearty in Maoriland. On a farm at Selwyn recently
seven guns were fired simultaneously into ' a mass
meeting, and the casualties numbered 228. Unfor-
tunately the name of the niutton-head who imported
the sparrow into M.L. was not in the list of killed.

HENARE PURETAPI.
“Devilfish” : The sassafras is one of those
Australian plants whose economic value is practically
unknown except to a few bushmen. There are people
in the backblocks of Tasmania, who use sassafras
“tea” and thrive upon it. A decoction made from
the bark is a fine tonic, and is said to cure toothache
and neuralgia. If the sassafras grew in Peru or
Cochin-China instead of right at our doors we would
import the bark in vast quantities for medicinal
purposes.
A FALSE ALARM.
Recognising the fodder value of kurrajong,
eorob, saltbush and several other indigenous vege-
tables in droughty places, Ashford, Minister for Lands
(N.S.W.), will supply landholders with seedlings of
these fodder plants, gratis. To induce the man on
the land to avail himself of this assistance £l5O will
be distributed among those obtaining the best results
with the seedlings ; and portions of the State farms
are to be set aside for the experimental cultivation of
these native herbs.

M‘GEE and the giraffe.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
My friend M'Gee, from Bungaree,
I trotted to the Zoo,
The lumbering elephant to see,
The growling tiger, too ;
The lion of gigantic size
He looked upon with small surprise.
The buffalo he seemed to know;
For deer he didn’t care;
And no concern he cared to show
Before the Polar bear ;
But how he snorted through his nose
When high the wild giraffe arose!
A weird surmise was in his eyes.
“Begor!” I heard him say.
(His wonder he did not disguise—•
He would not come away.)
“I’ll wait to see how the galoot
Climbs up that high to feed the brute!”
VICTORIA.
