A right up-to-the-minute request was
made by a juryman during a murder trial
in Hobart recently. Being informed that
as the case could not finish they would be
locked up for the night, one arose and
asked if his Honor would allow them to go
to “the pictures” under strong escort! His
’Onali said he’d be dashed if he would, or
something like that.
The “smalls” of Australia are to have a
round of calls from an entertaining co.
headed by Australia’s old friends Spada and
Philip Newbury, the Ilaymans (late of
London), and the Waldrons. N. S. Wales
first, starting in the West this month;
Queensland to follow.
“C. Ockney” : It is unfortunately true, as
“Ho Ho” points out (B. 19/8/T5), that
“the English language is rarely heard in
Australian playhouses now.” But I don’t
think the managements are blamable. The
English drama was dead long before the
war, and by latest reports it is still dead
and likely to remain so. Hence there seems
no prospect of any new British plays being-
staged here. During the last London season
few productions lasted longer than a fort-
night—or three weeks at the outside. Even
well-equipped play-concoctors like Knob-
lauch, Macdonald Hastings, James M.
Barrie and Stephen Phillips “fell down.”
Here are a few of the recent runs at
West End theatres: “Wild Thyme,” at the
Comedy, with Seymour Hicks in the lead,
three weeks; the piece which succeeded it,
“Mr. and Mrs. Ponsonby,” only totalled
eight performances; “The Laughter of
Fools” (Prince of Wales’), 25 perform-
ances; “The Road to Raebury” (Criterion),
11 performances; “Marie Odile” (Her
Majesty’s). 30 performances. Although
Beerbohm Tree produced this last in his best
manner, and in spite of his not appearing
in it himself, it failed dismally. Tree is
now in “the halls” doing a condensed version
of “Trilby” twice nightly. He finds these
starring engagements at a fat salary very
useful. The two biggest successes in Lon-
don are “Peg o' My Heart” and “Potash and
Perlmutter.” The former has been running
since last October and the latter since last
April twelvemonth! Both are American
plays.
“De Oriel”: Sorry to call “Barcoo” fB.
12/8/T5) a German plenipotentiary, but
Earl’s Court, Rockhampton, is not the larg-
est open-air picture-show place in Australia,
nor even Bananaland’s biggest. Olympia, at
Ipswich (Q.), fills the bill. It can hold the
whole local population and half their sins
at once.
Clara Butt is accumulating new experi-
ences. In last month’s production of “Peter
Ibbetson” —dramatised from Du Manner's
Anglo-Parisian story by J. N. Raphael, an
Ar.glo-Parisian himself —she appeared on the
stage for the first time in a speaking part.
Just before, she had gone on to sing in the
big “Henry VIII.” patriotic performance.
Naturally, she had to dress for the period ;
and that was her first appearance on any
stage in “costume.”
“Snell’': Consider tlie morals of “The Law
of the Land.” The hero and heroine are
firstly adulterers, secondly murderers, and
the ethical aim, so far as Broadliurst has
any aim beyond dollars, is to screen this
precious pair from the consequences of their
iniquity. The husband in the case is the
abandoned villain, because, being a deceived
husband with another man’s child foisted
upon him, he does not preserve the amenities,
curse him! The wife is a wanton and a
murderess without compunction, but she is
quiet with it, and wears pretty frocks,
whereas the husband is a plain man with a
nasty temper. The moral is that a plain
husband deserves all he gets if he doesn’t
take it kindly. It serves him right for being
a heroine’s husband, anyway.
“Floater” : They tried a Jap soprano for
the first time in the name part of “Madame
Butterfly” on the recent revival at London
Opera House. The lady’s name is Tamaki
Miura, and one critic gushes that “her act-
ing—a delightful blend of naivete and
tragedy —and her voice, with its quaint,
Occidental quality, quite won over her audi-
ence.”
“Norbell”: The late Dan Barry always
wore black gloves when playing Uncle Tom.
to save the bother of dirtying his hands with
burnt cork. One night in Bendigo he
whipped out between scenes to swallow an
inspiration, and forgot to put his gloves on
when he came back. lie was just in time
for Little Eva’s death, and kneeling rever-
ently at her bedside he raised his hands to
Heaven in pious grief, whilst all the as-
sembled niggers wailed “Sweet By-an’-Bye.”
Presently Dan wondered what the house
was giggling about, and then he caught sight
of his hands. Quite equal to the occasion
Dan exclaimed : “Praise de good Lord ! A
miracle ! A miracle ! Bress de Lord ! Ali’m
turnin’ white !”
“S.T.S.”: The overpowdered stage lady
(B. 22/7/’l5) is no new vision. I remem-
ber Toole long ago burlesquing “Fedora” in
a thing labelled “Stage-Doora.” After the
passionate love-scene, the old comedian used
to withdraw from the heroine’s embrace, and
silently but emphatically use a clothes-brush
all over his shoulders and breast.
“Moana”: “Floater’s” remarks (P>.
5/8/T5) regarding Pqul Hester’s new com-
edy. “Beverley’s Balance,” now beginning
to look' like a real success in the U.S.A.,
reminds me that the writer of this rather
slight plav owes a lot to Australia’s old
friend Margaret Anglin. Margaret usually
sniffs at mere comedy, but she was tempted
into the cast on this occasion by the witty
and distinctly clever dialogue which propels
the piece. The play is really a satirical
farce, very different from some of the
American comedies dished up in these parts
lately.
“Archdeacon Bluegum”: The swamping
of Australia with cheap-jack U.S.A. canned
drama has driven me and a large part of my
congregation out of the picture-shows. For
a couple of years or more cino.-men have
protested to me that the public demands the
kind of photo-drama that Uncle Flam pro-
duces. and that “scenic” films will not go.
I’m busy now hunting for those alleged
authorities. Last week the long scenic film.
“London by Day and Night,” .was unwound
at Sydney Lyceum, and ran at least twice
daily. It was advertised as the attrac-
tion, and it drew exhilarating business.
I saw it from various parts of the
house on divers occasions, believing, as
I do, that the Hun has still some-
thing up his Zeppelin, and that there
may be violent alterations in the old historic
town. Each time I was in the theatre people
before me, behind me, and on each side of
me gave the extra-glad hand to that “scenic,”
and any back-number showman who tells me
again that “that sort of thing is dead” does
so at the risk of being invited to try his
hand at the bottle-o business.
“Ami”: Harrie Ireland’s rheumatic
affliction reminds me of Rosalie Duvalli,
similarly punished and very likely from a
similar cause. Rosalie had to give up as a
dancer through rheumatism contracted by
performing in a British theatre which was
too new and damp-. Managerial greed thus
spoilt a graceful performer. But Australia
gained in one way, because the lady settled
down in Melbourne and taught other people
how to be less like their great-grandfather
apes.
“Ivaniva” : Mention of Pierre Bellew 18.
12/8/T5) as representing Napoleon in the
French Day pageant at Melbourne recalls
that his mother was Eugenie Legrand, lead-
ing lady to Boothroyd Fairclough, that more
than passable tragedian who opened at the
Theatre Royal (Melb.) in 1573 in Hamlet.
Eugenie was Ophelia, but her French tongue
tripped over many an English word.
“Monkbarns”: “More study!” moans the
despairing actor nowadays, if he lias one
new part in a month. Experience is sup-
posed to be gained when the tyro plays a
part of a few lines in a run of a whole year.
Harwood and Cathcart compared the enor-
mous number of characters they had acted.
Each did not drop far short of a thousand.
Harwood had acted every part in “Hamlet”
except Hamlet, and Cathcart every one
except the two clowns. Irving and Ban-
croft each acted over 400 parts before
emerging into much notice. A coun-
try stock actor might have half a dozen new
parts to master in a week. Heavy work
every day with double tides on Sunday. Then
look at what work meant even for those well
up the ladder. In London the performances
lasted five hours, from seven till 12, and
for a benefit it might be one a.m.! Walter
Lacy relates how that for 00 nights running,
at the London Princess’s, under Charles
Kean, he acted Rouble, an important char-
acter in the two-act comedy of “The Prima
Donna,” followed by Chateau Renaud, chief
villain in the three-act “Corsican Brothers,”
ending with Highflier, principal light
comedian in the two-act comedy, “A Roland
for an Oliver.”
“S.S.”: This is the story told to me by
the barman. “He was in here yesterdee.
He ain’t actin’ nowhere now. He was takin’
a stout, when a shiclter sez to him, ‘ ’Ellow.
ole long-wool!’ he says, ‘ain’t you goin’ to
the Front?’ Me noble the act-or man looks
him over. ‘Coin’ to the Front?’ sez he. ‘My
son, I came to the front before you were
born.’ ”
THE VOICE OF BROWN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Brown gets the foolish fancy
When we with wine rejoice,
And many drinks have vanished,
That he has got a Voice.
He’s likely then to christen,
While scoffing strangers smile,
A fat-and-forty barmaid
His Mary of Argyle.
We do not mention singing
Before him, as a rule;
For that is simple folly.
Blit Smithers is a fool.
He mentioned Tipperary,
And promptly Brown upsprang
And, in a lusty manner,
The famous ditty sang.
The publican said bluntly,
That he admired the voice,
But there could be no singing—
The Law allowed no choice.
That settled Brown—he vanished,
But presently was seen
To a policeman singing
The “Wearin’ of the Green.”
The Robert urged us, mildly,
To lead him out of view.
“Oi cud,” said he, “arrist him;
But Oi am Oirish, too.”
We did not wait to question •
The Irish blood of Brown,
But seized the raging singer
And trammed him out of town.
He sang the wild conductor
A song of the unfed,
In plaintive notes, requesting
A penny for some bread.
Then other songs he sampled
Till people made a fuss.
But, as we argued matters,
He left the tram and us.
We found him on a corner
Still singing this and that,
While some sly, ragged loafer
Was taking round the hat.
He swore Brown was his partner
With* angry oaths and shrill ;
But in a cab we bundled
Old Brown; and, singing still,
We drove him home. Together
We bore him to his door ;
But there he struggled from us
And burst in song once more.
It was a song of parting:
He called his wife the Maid
Of Athens. ’Twas intended
As a last serenade.
’Twas then we parted from him;
In haste we fled for town,
And only heard the water
That shortly fell on Brown!

The cable says that Frank, a U.S.A.
factory-owner who was accused of murder-
ing one of his girl employees, has been
dragged by a howling mob from the hands
•of the Law and suspended from a tree.
So Uncle Sam, who is too proud to fight,
proves that he still isn’t too stuck up to
lynch.
The Asquith Government has been reduced
to the humiliating necessity of explaining
that the Baron Schroeder job was a fraud
on the public. Schroeder was “naturalised”
in London as soon as the war broke out. not
because he had pretensions to loyalty, but
because his internment would have caused
financial trouble in the city. He practically
forced the Government to let him go through
the “naturalisation” farce, though he was
notoriously anti-British and his son was a
German officer. Moral: Don’t let an un-
naturalised alien get into a position where
the Government can’t afford to treat him as
the enemy he is when the Government de-
clares war against his fatherland.
“Understudy” has a suggestion:—
If Pearce will hand me his job for two or three
minutes I’ll make an order to the effect that every
volunteer who presents himself shall be forthwith
■examined, lock, stock and barrel. I volunteered and
passed the doctor in Ballarat. Arrived in Melbourne,
after burning my boats, I was there promptly chucked
•out. I wanted an inch of chest, and spent a month
getting it. Then the Department lowered the stan-
dard two inches, so I passed with two to spare and
was chucked out a minute later for having heart
trouble. Leaving out the Ballarat examination, on
the face of it farcical, I wasted a month, and was
examined twice. Had the first test been thorough I
need not have wasted the month, or as much of the
time of doctors, dentists, clerks, etc., as the second
exam, entailed.
Nothing particular happened at the muni-
cipal elections in Victoria. The Mayor of
Prahran, one Heyward, was pushed out in
favor of solicitor D. S. Abraham, a Toorak-
dweller of some distinction. Lately Hey-
ward made a few brusque remarks about
the unemployed, choosing a patriotic meet-
ing for the utterance, and those remarks
came back and slew him. Prahran also lost
T. G. L. Scott, one of the military censors,
beaten by Alfred Woodfull, a solicitor.
“Tommy” Carroll, a Board of Works Com-
missioner and 22 years in the Malvern
Council, was assisted out of his seat by
draper Darroch; and the ancient Edward
Dillon, who had been 48 years in the Cam-
berwell collection, fell before the attack of
one Arthur Willison. They say it will kill
Dillon.
“Carmine’s” art note : —-
McCubbin has been lecturing in Melbourne on Aus-
tralian Art. The early painters strove for the
grand rather than the homely. Witness Chevalier’s
“Buffalo Mountains” and Guerard’s “Kosciusko” and
“Mitta Mitta,” alien to Austral environment. Buvelot
was a forerunner of the present, with typical bush
scenes like his “Summer Eve at Templestowe,”
wherein one can find hints of the charm of Corot
and the Barbizon school. Native-boni artists worked
in the open, instead of in the studio. Withers settled
in Heidelberg. Note the result in his Tranquil
Winter.” Buvelot revelled in the hot summer even-
ing ; Davies in the subtle twilight mystery; Streeton
in the startling brilliance of summer noon. Of course
McCubbin made generous reference to Longstaff, Fox,
Paterson, Mather and others, and equally of course
did not refer at all to his own eminently creditable
output.
A noise has been made in IVestralia over
what is styled “the Wyndham secret con-
tract.” Some time ago Scaddan made a
very sudden bargain with a firm to build
freezing and water-supply works at Wynd-
ham, in the far nor’-west, for £155,150, or
about £45,000 less than the official estimate.
The contractors brought material from
Europe and elsewhere and sent a gang of
men up with a boss. On arrival the boss
decided that the work couldn’t be done for
the money, and threw up his job. A little
later the Government began to feel uneasy,
and finally induced the firm to cancel the
contract and accept £7844 for commission
on purchased material and cost of plans.
The Opposition twitted the Ministry about
the deal when Parliament met, and “Mulga”
Taylor, who breaks out occasionally, gave
tile Government a shock by moving from the
Ministerial side what was tantamount to
a no-confidence motion. Caucus then got
t 0 W ork, and when the House next met the
mo t; on lapsed. But the Opposition is now
taking a hand, and if it gets the undivided
support of the Country party (which is
doubtful ) it may push the Government out.
gcaddan says he took the tiling over because
there was a grave doubt about the firm’s
ability to complete the contract in anywhere
near the specified time; also there were
legal difficulties. The real reason, however,
is said to have been labor umons objection
the contract sys tem and the importation
men bo do the work when there were
plenty unemployed in the State. Compen-
sation was given because there was a legi-

Messrs. Finney, Isles and Co., Limited, “The
Drapers of Queensland,” have issued their new season
fashion book, and it is now being' posted to their out-
of-town friends. This authentic fashion book will
probably be of greater value this season than any of
its predecessors, as during the winter great changes
have taken place in the fashion world. Styles that
were perfectly correct a few months back no longer
reign. The pretty feminine frilly frocks of 1850 are
all showing in this very complete book. The compil-
ing has been very carefully planned and carried out
with the sole object of making mail-order shopping-
simple and easy. The book is an encyclopaedia of all the
newest in millinery, costumes, coats, underwear, chi I
dren’s wear, dress goods, furnishing, men’s and boys’
wants, furniture and household goods generally.
Fashion Guide will be posted free to anyone out of
town taking the trouble to send their" name and
address.*
POLITICAL
POINTS
What absurdities the voluntary Defence
system flogs us into! So eager are we to
push and bribe men to do their duty that the
other day the N. S. Wales Full Court allowed
the application of a lawyer’s articled clerk
that service as a member of the Expedition-
ary Force should be counted as service under
his articles of clerkship! On the same prin-
ciple, supposing the war lasted five years
and a man served in it all the time, he ought
on his return to have the right to say what
trade or profession he chose by virtue of his
service. Five years’ soldiering would thus
qualify a man to practise as undertaker, doc-
tor, lawyer or plumber.
THE UNREWARDED CHAPE.
THE HUN: “Vot vos the better cef I chase
him lint I don’t catch him?”
“Leo I.cars'’ tale of another permanent
and reproductive job :
The drought has left one peculiar monument in
Western Victoria in the shape of a windmill whose
head now stands or floats about four feet above water
in Lake Condah. This lake was formerly one of the
Cabbage Garden’s few fresh-water inland seas, but
being regarded as an incubus —though it covered less
land than many stations would fence for a weaning
paddock—some of Bull’s superfluous cash was used
to bail and drain it, and its dry bed was leased for
a sheep-run. The mill was then erected to pump
water. Since the drought collapsed this part of Vic-
toria has been deluged with torrents unequalled for
decades. Consequently Condah defied the Government
and its puny drains, rose to its old level, swamped
Bull’s £ s. d., and left the mill pathetically waving
its arm for help above the flood.
The Germans are “experimenting with a
giant aviatili” which is “even noisier than
the Zeppelin.” This is a characteristic tri-
umph. Modern German science is all noises
and stinks, and the Hun who couples a
horrifying noise and a suffocating stink will
be acclaimed the inventor of a masterpiece.
The annoying part of it is that, when the
world is quiet again, their own infernal
noises will have made them so deaf that
they will be unable to hear the blistering
comments of posterity.
“Mujik": How can the “Never Again”
brigade expect to be taken seriously when
they talk of their fierce determination to
encourage Australian industry and simul-
taneously turn it down? At a recent official
feast in Sydney foreign wine helped the
tucker along, while 17 speakers blithered
that “every pound kept in Australia is a
golden prop to our national solvency.”
Meanwhile it is announced by the Common-
wealth’s commercial agent at. Capetown that
a good market is offering for Australian
champagne in South Africa. So things may
be evened up on the lines dear to the foreign-
trader’s soul by Australia dragging bottles
from the other end of the globe and sending
other bottles on a long, wet journey.
The Tasmanian Government is mortally
afraid that the recruiting movement may
end in some of its departments being manned
only by the old ladies at the top of the tree
and the young ladies who do the typing.
Consequently all State servants have been
forbidden to enlist without special permis-
sion, and a good many who have asked for
leave to go have been refused it. One war-
rior-clerk in the Health Department reached
camp in spite of all precautions, and had
been there over a week before he was missed
and hauled back to his stool. It is so much
more important that his stool should be kepi
warm than that the Empire should stand up.
The \\ estralian Labor Government has
introduced a Bill which will not do Mr.
Bung any good. The measure divides the
State into four districts —Metropolitan.
Goldfields, Agricultural and Nor’-West-
and provides for a referendum to be taken
in each. Each district will decide the hour
at which its hotels and clubs shall shut
down on liquor. Ballot papers xvill afford
an opportunity of voting for closure at any
hour between six and 11 p.m. If six o’clock
closing is ruled out the votes for that
hour will be transferred to seven, and so
on, as in the recent S. Australian poll.
Lessees of hotels and members of clubs are
to be entitled to the return from their land-
lords of an amount of rent proportioned to
the number of hours by which their trading
time is curtailed; and when in the case of
pubs a premium has been paid by the tenant,
a similar proportion must be refunded. The
Bill is certain to pass the Assembly, but it is
probable that clubs will be exempted by the
Council. The only other remote possibility
is that the Bill may be rejected by the Coun-
cil on the ground that if a referendum is taken
at all the State should vote as one and not
by districts. Meanwhile the Government is
being righteously taunted by the Opposition
with having introduced the Bill in order to
rid itself of the responsibility of enforcing
the Act of 1914.. which provides for the shut-
ting of pubs and clubs at 9 p.m. during war-
time.
No man interprets the good gospel
that the laborer is worthy of his hire quite
so clearly as the Australian.
Arrangements were made re-
cently to billet return wounded
on the hospitality of private
Melbourne homes. But when
the scheme was explained to
Gallipoli Bill, he replied defin-
itely that he couldn’t consider it
unless the Government contrib-
uted the cost of his keep. If
the Government wouldn’t do
that, then he preferred to stay
in the hospital or rest-home. It
meant, as clearly as Bill could
say it. that he didn’t believe in
voluntary service—that con-
scription was the only fair
thing, everybody paying his whack towards
the maintenance of the crippled Kangaroo.
N.S.W. Attorney-General Hall got his nose
badly pinched in the door when lie dashed off
those frantic orders of his for Yankee butter.
Uncle Sam, who at the best is a wretched
butter maker, unloaded on him thousands of
boxes of grease so vile that even a butter-
starved Sydney turned up its nose at it.
Hall is now warily sending 4000 boxes of the
awful stuff on another sea-trip, in the hope
that John Bull or Senegal or somebody won’t
be so particular. He reckons the loss may
be kept within reasonable limits if the con-
signment brings £lO a ton more than the
choicest Danish. But as it will certainly go
at job-lot rates, a sadder and chastened
Hall is busy changing the conversation.
What with his wheat losses and butter
calamities and disturbances over the Aus-
tralian pig, the Attorney-General is having
strenuous times. It is wonderful what a
man can miss knowing about wheat and but-
ter and bacon deals by studying the law.
Tories all over Australia have swal-
lowed their principles pretty effectively
as regards the Maternity Bonus. When
Fisher brought the idea forward mem-
bers of the Women’s Gnashonal League,
“Mother of Ten,” and others con-
demned it as an Insult to True Woman-
hood, and announced that they would sooner
die than accept the shameful “liver.” Offi-
cial figures, however, show that practically
every woman in the Commonwealth, no
matter how lofty her principles or how
bloated her banking account, who gave birth
to a child last year, went to the. further
trouble of filling in the forms necessary to
obtain the bonus. In 1913 the number of
claims allowed (135,667) was only 47
below the births (1.31,714) ; while last year
THE CRUELTY OF BRITISH SEA POWER.
HERR DOCTOR : "Your Majesty is much run down. You need a sea voyage.”
THE KAISER (bitterly): "I ere can I go—He Aquarium?”
the claims allowed (139.119) actually ex-
ceeded the births (137.983) by 1176. This
anomaly is accounted for by the fact that
about 3000 allowances are paid annually
in respect to still-born infants not included
in the total number of birth registrations;
while against this is to be set the fact that
only one bonus is paid for twins or triplets,
of which multiple births there were, for
instance. 1350 in 1912. Put the main fact
which official figures establish beyond dis-
pute is that claims are made in respect to
practically 100 per cent, of the little immi-
grants.
From an entirely untrustworthy source : —•
Thank Heaven, the sovereign authority reserved to
the Australian State* is not limited to prohibiting
pig and butter and cliickwheat going over the
borders; they can also prohibit things coming in-
wards ! Consequently the X. S. Wales Government,
in exercise of its powers of eminent domain, is ready
with a proclamation that no boots, clothing or
machinery shall be allowed to cross the Murray north-
wards. This will, fix those Federal Parliamentarians
who think they have authority to do anything more
than borrow money.
When Vic. Opposition-leader Elmslie
ousted Watt two years ago, and held office
for a week with the grudging aid of the
Corner, he got in ahead of Corner-leader
McLeod by about 10 minutes. Last week
he took less risk, firing a shot immediately
the House assembled, while Oman, McLeod
and Johnston were hurrying up Bourke-
street and McKenzie was reading in the
library. Of course the whole thing is farce.
Under the ridiculous party system Elmslie
cannot beat the Government without the aid
of the Corner, and if he got the Corner’s
aid he could not rely on it for five minutes.
Meanwhile, when you consider the mass of
useful work waiting to be done—which an
elective Government, not bound by party
ties, would probably make an effort to do —-
compared with the tons of work which the
Peacock team has energetically left undone,
there seems niucli excuse for John
Weary’s plea that Parliament might as well
shut up till the war is over. But when you
come to think of it, what a shocking admis-
sion it is for a Minister to make!
Thus N.S.W. Governor Strickland, at the
Sydney “Fair” which is intended to show
the way to “capture the enemy’s trade”: —
It was a pleasant coincidence that the president
of the Chamber of Commerce (G. A. Parkes) bore the
name of a great Australian statesman, who was r
markable for his foresight.
A great Australian statesman named Parkes
who was remarkable for his foresight? There
certainly was a Premier named Parkes; but
lie was the boss Foreign-trader of his time;
and his lack of foresight helped to make this
“Trade Fair” necessary. Surely Strickland
wasn’t referring to that Parkes as a plea-
sant coincidence! If he was, then we could
get equal misinformation on important sub-
jects for much less money than his Ex. costs
us.

The Bulletin piclm-c was, if 1 may put it
that way, literally truer perhaps than the
artist thought. The writer and a hundred
other people have seen cows gazing at
half an inch of water in the Molonglo River
many a time, whilst the small sons of the
Acton dairyman were lopping the willow
trees by the side of the alleged river to pro-
vide sustenance for the beasts.
.Mr. Public House in Vie. wants the Act
under which he trades altered to put him in
line with his neighbors who sell soft tack.
They can deal out ginger-pop all night long
if they like, but he must stop at 9.30 p.m.
Nevertheless he would be well advised to.
leave things as they are. If the bars are
allowed to remain open after that hour for
the serving of raspberry vinegar only, the
temptation to oblige a customer with a
“stick” to put into it will be so -frequently
succumbed to that there will be a rare old
tearing-up of scraps of paper in the form of
publicans’ licenses.

“He seized this chance. He”
“I. throw that back in your teeth.”
“You can throw it back in"
“Untrue! Untrue!”
“\ ou are making' a flank attack ; we will meet it
and"-
“Despite all this display my conscience does not
accuse me.”
No, not at a meeting of the Bungalunga
Progress Association, but in Australia's
National Parliament, where a truce has been
called, and every one is bending his energies
to the conduct of the war. This is how
Fisher and Cook bend. The rest do it in
assorted shouts and snorts.
WANTED—THE TRUTH.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
How few were we? How many were they?
IVhat is tlie price that we have to pay?
What is the task before us set
Where the shrapnel bursts and the ranks are met?
These are the things that none may know,
But the battle calls and the death-lists grow.
The voice of the mingled tumult swells
From the shell-swept ridge at the Dardanelles,
And the months go by and the strong men fall.
Is the end in sight? Have we gained at all?
By the censored word and the boast of pride
Must Australia's faith to the end lie tried?
We are not weaklings to flinch or fail
At the last black word of a dreadful tale ;
But our hearts grow cold at the grim surmise
That the half-told truths may be whole-told lies.
If we stand for truth, let the truth be said,
For the right to hear is our best blood shed.
What foe is here that should point his gun
With a straighten aim for the word ye shun?
Shall the spring wind’s treason the tale take back
Of the loss we suffer, the things we lack?
Dr is it your timid hearts that fear
If we knew we should count the cost too dear?
Into the dark our strength must go,
Blindfold to face a wide-eyed foe,
While tales of victory find us still
Flinging like death to the first-won bill ;
And we need more men, and ever more,
To do what ye said we had done before!
We are children of light. In the strong, bright sun
Dur lives are lived and our deeds are done.
'Tell us the truth, if ye fain would fire
Dur blood to the triumph your hearts desire,
Nor fear that the whole of the stark truth told
Will make one foot in Australia cold.
But this secret war, where suspicion steals
Through the throb that the patriot soldier feels,
May end in a scorn to all men known
Of those who knew not to trust their own.
Black shame were yours if history tells
How the Censor lost us the Dardanelles.
N.S.W.
GEORGE STREET.

SPORTING
NOTIONS
Uncle Sam’s form, assuming that Sam is
fit and seasoned for his passages, will he an interest-
ing revelation at the A.J.C. Spring Meeting. After
his last-year victory in the Caulfield Cup the big
chestnut horse was put to the stud, and was under-
stood to have retired from racing pursuits. Subse-
quently he came back to town (as Trafalgar did for
a while), and at time of writing he is nominally a
favorite for the coming Metropolitan. Bigger than
ever, Sam has lasted through the preliminaries of a
preparation for his engagements, and, all going well,
his work on the Randwick track will be the “winding
up’,’ which was never a very severe ordeal in the
case of this exceptional gee. Owner Reid reckons
that Sam is “better than ever,” and will win the
Metropolitan. Precedents are against him. Horses
'that have been studded don’t recover their best
form, as a rule. Sam may prove an exception. The
weight (9st. 41b.) would hardly stop the giant if he
were his old self, and as a 7-year-old he is still
fresher for racing purposes than many younger-horses
that have been more knocked about. All the same,
nobody who knows the game will back Uncle Sam for
the Metropolitan until he has been fairly tested on
the track or in a w.f.a. race.
* * * «
The Spring-Summer meetings at Rand-
wick, Caulfield and Flemington will test the relative
merits of imported and native-born horseflesh as thev
never were tested before. Last season three of the
big handicaps—Metropolitan, Futurity Stakes and
Australian Cup—went to foreigners, one of them,
Lempriere, being an American, and none of them
carrying a first-class burden. A lot of others were in
training, and, all things considered, the imported
houses didn’t make as good a show as in the two
previous seasons when they were less numerous. This
season a German bomb dropped on any important
handicap field would be likely to hit an English-bred
animal. Most of the importations have been let in
lightly enough'. Ben has got only 7st. 41b. for the
Caulfield Cup, and can afford to earn a penalty in the
meantime. Hash of Steel is kindly treated with
7st. 121 b. for the Epsom, seeing that he was good to
win the Futurity Stakes last February, when, being
bred to English time, he was younger than our three-
year-olds. William the Silent, well backed for the
Metropolitan, is asked to carry no more than 6st.
121 b. Evidently the liandicapper thought none the
better of William for his having been silently born
in the Cold Country. In fact, handicappers must have
good reason for thinking that most of the imported
horses were poor things from the outset, or that they
are apt to deteriorate in the warmer climate. The
case of the rather weedy Almissa, who won three good
Cups in Victoria, is regarded as exceptional. All the
same, there have been some notable instances of im-
provement in imported horses, and if there are going
to be any very dead birds for the Spring gamble,s they
will probably be among the little crowd of lightly-
weighted newchums.

“Whip and Shoes”: Horse-sinners who
struggle over weight problems and their possibilities
have something to consider in the two Epsom candi.
dates Machine Bov (Machine Gun—Flower Girl) and
La- Balfour (Balfour —La Belle). Machine Boy is
thjjv champion Northern N.S.W. performer, and the
other day won the handicap at Wallsend
9st. 31b., and ran second with 9st. 111 b. at
Newcastle on Saturday. In the Epsom Machine Boy
will carry only fist. 121 b drop of 331 b. on the
Maitland win. La Balfour’s best success was at
Canterbury Park (Sydney) on Saturday with Bst.
151 b. more than the Epsom weight. Now, you
take your choice and back your fancy.
Quiraing, Dr. Ewan Frazer’s big Sydney
house, was sold last week, so it may be assumed that
this horse-owner has left the country for good. He
began racing in a small way a dozen years ago at
Bong Bong and other picnic meetings in N. S.
Wales. Becoming infected with the love of the
game, he bought at generous figures, with the re-
sult that his colors figured at the front in big events
both at Randwick and Flemington. He was an
enthusiastic believer in the “figure system” of breed-
ing, and could talk it with considerable fluency.
For one who went into the racing business knowing
nothing whatever about horses, he picked up a
wonderful amount of knowledge in a short time. He
was a member of the committee of the A.J.C. and a
non-bettor.
Trouble in Bananaland since the Govern-
ment decided to take from the racing, clubs certain
dividend fractions hitherto unpaid to investors has
assumed a new aspect. Assistant-Minister Fihellv re
cently gave a broad hint to the Q.T.C. that if the clubs
were overcome with the difficulties of working under
the new conditions, the Government would be pre-
pared to take over the totalisators and do the job
itself. There were more people suddenly interested in
that highly-commendable remark than the racing
clubs; there were some members of the Government
Caucus who arose and heartily shook hands with the
proposal, and swore they would never desert it until
the machines were State-run. To give an idea of what
the clubs were scooping in fractions, I'ihelly says
that up to the end of August £602 had been paid
into consolidated revenue, representing fractions of a
penny, since the new rule came in force on July 1
That averages £3OO a month. Before the altered
conditions most of the clubs held back fractions of a
shilling I

Is.S.W. s most elaborate recent embezzler
m connection with the grand old industry of trying
to keep pace with fast and not-so-fast horses turned
up the other day at West Maitland Quarter Sessions.
He had been a bank manager at Wallsend, and had
dipped for over £3600. The police said there was
no doubt that, excepting the cost of a motor-car,
practically all the money had gone on overdue
racing nags. “Three years’ hard labor,” said the
Judge, and another well-meaning enthusiast went
■along the Mugs’ Corridor.

Some idea of the grip that trotting has
got on Maoriland can be got from the fact that
during last season the governing association handed
out no less than 2397 riders’, drivers’ and trainers’
licenses. The biggest scrap on earth doesn’t seem
to be affecting the sport. At the Metropolitan meet-
ing at Christchurch in November the stake-money to
be scattered amounts to £lo,ooo—.the same as'last
year.

Otago University’s first fifteen of last year
deserves the bakery. Thirteen of these footballers
made a rush to the Dardanelles; two were killed
in action; two died from fever following upon
wounds; and two were wounded. The rest at
latest information were fracturing the sixth com-
mandment with the enthusiasm the occasion
demands.

Such games as linger on in Adelaide have
taken air interesting turn. At lacrosse, Kast Tor-
rens, top on the season—largely through the goai-
throv/ingj power of three Oke brothers—lost a semi-
final to University, who then went down before
Sturt; and Sturt finally beat Kast Torrens in a
challenge match. Football has got to the “last
four” stage ; and the afternoon that saw the list
settled saw also West Torrens (which even so couldn’t
get into tfie four) beat Port Adelaide (which is still
comfortably top on the season). Tlius there are
possibilities about the Finals.
* * * *
S. It- Grant (Balmain) is another top-
notch Sydney oarsman who leaves a big reputation
behind to hunt for the Turk’s scalp. He had a
seat in the N.S.W. eight-oar boat in 1912-13-14, and
a long array of performances which compare with
those of the mighty Rodrick. Balmain’s new eight-
oar bogt has been immortalised by being named the
“S. R. Grant”-—-the best tribute the club could think
of to pay to a fine member.
* * * *
Rodney Heath, formerly Victorian lawn-
tenuis champion, is in England, and will be given a
commission in the Royal Flying Corps if his vol-
planes prove satisfactory to the authorities. He
is a son jpf jeweller Frederick Heath, who takes the
official records for the V.R.C.
Of the eight players who represented
Sydney Polo Club’s B team last season against Glen
Innes in the Countess of Dudley’s Cup Competition,
three went to the Front. The first to lay down his
life is Jack Merivale, secretary of the Sydney Polo
Club, who enlisted as a trooper in the Light Horse,
and subsequently got a commission. Major J. F.
White, who played against him on the Glen Innes
side, is lying wounded in a Cairo hospital. He was
also in the Light Horse.
*~* * *
“Ranji” has lost an eye, one of his two
greatest -physical assets. Those astonishing leg
glides ofhis, and the late cuts which sent the ball
banging to the fence between the wicket-keeper and
the finest 1 of slips, were due to his eye. The same
marvellous organ made him a beautiful game-shot, a
first-rate. billiard player, and an adept at most
other kindred sports. It seems absurd to get badly
wounded on a Scotch grouse moor after fighting in
Flanders for six months, but such experiences are
common. “Bay” Middleton, one of the best and
most daring riders to hounds that England ever pro-
duced, broke his neck jumping a three-foot drain, and
Roddy Owen, who won the Grand National on Father
O’Flynn, and fought hard in Egypt and on the
Indian Frontier, was finally settled at Dongola by
cholera.
-*■#■**
“Ivaniva”: Young Tom Hayward, a
nephew of, the famous Surry cricketer, was recently
killed by accident while riding a motor-cycle near
Cambridge (Eng.). He was an able, all-round player,
and was engaged with the ground staff of the classic
Fenner ground. He was the third Tom Hayward'..
The first visited Australia with the first English
Eleven hi 1861. The second visited Australia on
several occasions, and was the strong man of his side..
“Hermes” : One of the results of the war is
an attempt to start the motor-manufacturing industry
in Australia. Already, for some months past, motor-
bicycles have been made in Sj’dney, good Australian!
from engine to handle-bar, except the magneto..
Another ■ firm is now laying down a plant to build!
self-charging electric cars. Hitherto electric cars
have been good for city use only, owing t®.. tftfc ,
difficulty of re-charging. The average electric ite
good for a run of not more than 75 miles. Then-
the batteries have to bo replenished. The self-
charging car carries with it a small dynamo that
can be hitched on to any driving machinery, and
tbis will re-charge the batteries in very brief time..
As the war is teaching us to do things like this,.
I’m trying to have a kind thought about it.
The “Homing Tyre,” patented all over
the world by the wealthy.squattocratic syndicate that
has taken it up, is the invention of Edwin Jones, of
Melbourne, _ who instructs the workmen in how to
make it with cocoamit fibre. Jones is an Ancient
Mariner, holding a master’s certificate, and his tyre
was a nautical inspiration, so to speak. It is claimed
to be the thing for rough travelling. Whereas an
ordinary rubber tyre carried for j emergency fise
costs from £7 upwards, the “Homing”—which can-
not blow out, neither can broken bottles cut it to
pieces—costs only 555. It holds its own with, the
.rubbers in a run on a smooth road,; afid has proved
equal to 35 miles an hour. A -great character
is ex-mariner Jones. The Lusitania and Arabic
piracies (he formerly served on the; Arabic) moved
him to offer himself as a recruit. Each time he
passed the examination up to the point where one of
his legs failed in the bending test. Now he has a
scheme for forming an independent band of war
workers, who will pay their own passages to Egypt
and give their services as * able-bodied veteran handy-
men. Lots of old sailors and others are asking for a
chance to do something. Jones proposes to charier
a ship and take the handy-men to Alexandria.

HIS SYSTEM.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
George Arthur lodged with us at Kay's.
He was a slim and natty youth,
Gentle and nice in all his ways.
It shocked us much to learn the truth-
He made his living backing horses,
Or picking winners on the courses.
He picked a winner every day,
And sometimes hit on four or five,
So specialised in going gay,
And found it easy to contrive
To have two stalls for every show,
And blow the champagne bubbles-o.
He owned he paid for little dinners,
And motor-cars could hire all day
H.v going out and picking winners;
But nothing further would he say.
His system we believed to be
A copper-bottomed certainty.
George Arthur lives no more with Kay—
The peelers have curtailed his fun.
He found three winners Caulfield way,
And someone caught him picking one.
He watched the bookies paying out,
Then picked the winners in the rout!
VICTORIA.

WHY SUFFER TORTURE BY THE CRUEL
ELECTRIC NEEDLE?
ELECTRICITY ALWAYS STIMULATES HAIR GROWTH.
HOW TO KIL*L AND DISSOLVE OUT ROOTS AND ALL.
For the benefit of Sydney “Bulletin” readers, Lady explains how she accidentally discovered
a New Absorption Process which Permanently Removed her Hairy Mask, after Electricity,
Tweezers, Caustic Pastes, Potions, Powders, and all other Depilatories and Advertised
Remedies had absolutely failed to do anything but harm.
By following simple directions given below any woman now lias the means of easily
preparing and using in her own home this wonderful process, which has hitherto been a
carefully-guarded secret known only to a few high-priced specialists. Pull directions are
now made public for the first time.
All who are afflicted with superfluous hair
will be interested to learn of the amazing
discovery made by Miss Kathryn B. Firmin,
who until recently was deeply humiliated
by these repulsive growths upon her face,
neck and arms. As the hair constantly
became more thick and hideous she tried
every process and remedy advertised or
recommended, but found
to her sorrow that if
any of these removed
hair at all, the effects
were only temporary,
and new growths soon
appeared stronger than
ever. Even hours of
torture under the cruel
electric needle simply
meant great pain, a
sore and blemished
skin, and the inevit-
able disappointment.
After spending huge
sums in efforts to be
rid of her terrible and
beauty-d estroying
affliction, Miss Firmin
was about to give up
in despair, when by
chance she learned of
a means by which the
beauties of Ancient
Rome are said to have
permanently banished
superfl nous hair.
With only a very slight clue as to the nature
of this remarkable process used in bygone
ages, Miss Firmin tells how she set to work
experimenting in her tireless effort to wrest
the lost secret from the past. From the
accounts of Miss Firmin’s discovery, which
have recently aroused so much *' interest
among women with superfluous hair, there
seems to be no doubt that at last there has
been found a way, most radically different
from anything hitherto known, by which
any woman can now rid herself permanently,
harmlessly and painlessly of all superfluous
hair-growths by dissolving them out of ex-
istence, root and all. One part of the pro-
cess consists of a solution easily obtained
After she had endured for ten years the humilia-
tion of having a heard and moustache like a man.
and after electrolysis, pastes, ‘‘lotions.” and all
other methods had failed absolutely, Miss Firmin
killed all her superfluous hair in a single night
by the harmless new absorption process fully
explained in this article.
and prepared by anyone, which possesses
the remarkable quality of being readily
absorbed by the hair, so that it creeps
down to the root, dissolving as it goes,
just as oil creeps up a lamp-wick. It is,
perhaps, needless to caution any who may
use this process, which has so deadly an
effect upon the hair, that it must never by
any chance be per-
mitted to touch hair
which is not to be
destroyed. In ex-
plaining the process
Miss Firmin mentions
that it is perfectly
neutral and ineffective
to the skin, as anyone
can quickly prove by
experiment, but she
disclaims all responsi-
bility for permanent
loss of desirable hair,
such as eyebrows, hair
of the head, etc., to
which the process is
applied. Even though
the accidental appli-
cation be insufficient
to dissolve the hair at
once, it will eventually
die and fall out, and
there exists no known
means for restoring
life to hair roots thus
affected.
For the benefit of any readers who may
be interested, and who wish to be rid of
their superfluous hair by this remarkable
process, we are authorised to announce that
Miss Firmin has agreed to send all neces-
sary particulars regarding its preparation
and use to any reader sufficiently interested
to send her two penny stamps for return
postage. Simply address Miss Kathryn B.
Firmin (Suite 631. P.), 133 Oxford Street,
London, W. (Eng.), and full information
will be sent by return post in plain, sealed
envelope. On account of the great demands
upon Miss Firmin’s time, she has stipu-
lated that this offer must be announced to
positively expire at the end of 60 days.

Business. Bobbery, etc.
“Q.K.” : Chemists and all other buyers of
perfumes, soaps and things for the “trade” are
laughing at the story of how Julius Blau
talked the Commonwealth Ministers into
letting him get away to America on what
is alleged to have been a bogus mission. Of
course he satisfied Hughes and the others of
his bona fides, after his friend and admirer
Carr, had introduced him to them.
Blau and liis agency for the “4711'’ brand
of eau-de-Cologne have always been a mat-
ter of interest to the trade, and to inquisi-
tive outsiders who had wondered how the
“4711” brand came to Hood the market. There
are at least two better brands, but “4711"
fairly got this country by the nose. It was
all in the personality of Blau, the "natu-
ralised Hungarian” partner in a German
firm. Julius Blau was far and away the
most persuasive salesman in the business.
Years ago -when I sought an explanation of
the “4711” mystery I was told that Julius
could whistle a bird off a tree. “All
the travellers in the trade take their
hats off to Julius,” it was said.
Evidently Hughes has had to take
his hat off to the plausible Hungarian-
German gentleman, apd will swear into it
whenever henceforth the name of the in-
gratiating foreigner is mentioned.
“Croson”: I observe that, through the
shire councils, the Peacock Government is
inquiring of the man on the land what labor
he is likely to require during the coming
harvest, with a view to organising the sup-
ply. Heaven knows farm labor needs organ-
ising badly enough ; liut 1 am not sure that
the Government’s idea of “organisation”
coincides with mine. The other day Henry
Chaplin, farm laborer, sued a Bass Valley
farmer for £l7 short paid on .'!4 weeks’ labor.
It appeared that Chaplin and his grown-up son
and daughter had toiled at the monotonous
job of teat-pulling for 34 weeks; and then
trouble started. Whereas the boss averred
the stipulated wages—for the three adults —-
was £3 per week and “find” themselves,
Chaplin swore it was £3 10s. The hours
were from 7 a.in. to 3 p.m. in winter, and
from 4 a.in. to S p.m. in summer. Even
the honorariums shivered in front of the
icicle who was prepared to defend such soul-
less sweating, and allowed the claim.
“Billybunger” : It isn't all cakes and fizz
with the wool cultivator these days. A big
Queensland station took off its clip in July,
1014. Along came the war. knocking local
wool sales on the head, and the clip was
stored at Brisbane for seven months, waiting
for the buyers to open their mouths. The
charges for storage and insurance were no
soft snap. Eventually the wool was sent to
England for sale, and bumped heavy freight,
insurance, “bomb tax” of 2s. (id. a bale. etc.
It cost £1 a bale to sell that clip after land-
ing it at St. Katherine’s dock, London. To
date, nearly 12 months after the shearing.no
payment has been received, and shearing is
about to commence again. That means stiff
interest for the wherewithal to finance the
harboring. Then there are the drought losses
and the stoppage of mutton export to be con-
sidered. It's nnly a few weeks ago that Pre-
mier Ryan induced the British Government
to take Banahalffnd carcases under the con-
ditions which N. S. Wales enjoys. That
relieved the situation a little, and allowed
part of the squattocracy to indulge once more
in tobacco and toothpicks. In tlie matter of
fire-water they’ve been following the King.
* * * *
The war has made the Yankee motor-car
buzz as it never buzzed before. During the
11 months from August of last year to June
of this, 21,000 cars were sent from N’Yark
to Europe, their value, plus the value of
motor parts and tyres, being £8,200,000. And
the business is still jumping like an in-
furiated wild-cat. In the same period how
many cars has Australia not exported?

* B. 19/8/T5) of the thrifty man who 'grew

Forth (TasA who grew a black wood tree
until, there being sufficient timber in it for

to England. There it: was made into the
shell of it grand piano, which was shipped
back to him.
A RHYME OF ASSORTED MEAT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The butcher waited beside his block in an odor of
ancient fat,
And to the dowager prowl and prim he amiably
a nswered : “ Thu 11
Why, that is the primest of dairy-beef, and a very
nice piece of meat!
Yes, mum —four pounds with the bone in it, and the
value is hard to beat.
Ten bob for the lot!” With a bunted look tlw
dowager murmured: “Oh!
It really is dear!'’ But tlie butcher took
'The elderly meat from its shiny hook,
And said, as he parcelled it up: “Oh, yes —hut y'
gotter have meat, y’ Know !”
A year went by, and tlie last, lone cow for butcher’s
meat had died,
And the same old butcher stood and served the same
old block beside,
And the same old dowager sought his shop for a
trifle whereon to dine;
And “Rabbits Are Cheap To-day”—outside she saw
ids simple sign.
“Yes, mum, a dollar a pair,” he said. “Fat ’tins —
tlie price is low.”
“Dear me, how dear!" the dowager said ;
But the butcher waggled his greasy head:
“V" gotter have meat-—or as near to it as yer able
to get, y" know.”
A few years later and trawler-tack was Australia’s
daily dish,
And the butcher waited beside his block in an odor
of frozen fish ;
Then the dowager, bent and exceeding old, and palsied
of hand and tongue,
iloved slowly, with an appraising eye, the deep-sea
dead among.
She made her choice of a sea-meat steak from the
glittering, frozen show,
And sadly a sov. she placed upon
The butcher’s palm, and her smile was wan
As she breathed: “Ah, fish! In tlie (food old days we
used to have meat, you know!”

“Front Page” in the history of brandy production
in our Australian Commonwealth.
As a stimulant it is a safe, reliable, honest spirit,
as pure as the crystal spring, being produced wholly
from the fresh, ripe grape. It is guaranteed so by
the Government of the Commonwealth.
As a beverage, it has no depressant action, and you
can taste the grapes in it.
Twenty-six years’ experience have taught us how to
produce the finest brandy in the world trom the
Australian grape.

BEFORE THE MIRROR.
By “JEANNETTE.”
“Jeannette,” the well-known writer on Beauty Culture and author of “The Book of Beautv,”
will be pleased to help and advise any reader on matters of the toilet. Queries should be accom-
panied by a stamped addressed envelope, arid directed to “Jeannette/’ c/o “Before' the Mirror,”
43 Gray’s Inn lioad, London, W.C., England.
These are, undoubtedly, days of exceptional nervous strain for one and all of
us. Anxiety, with its beauty-destroying influence, is casting its shadow over the
lives of many of us women, and while, perhaps, a few—the more stoical amongst
"s—can avoid worrying over past and prospective events, we may all, with a little
care and foresight, succeed in preventing the results of worry from being notice-
able in our appearance. To these fortunate few this little chat will, however, be
as interesting and instructive as to those who stand in real need of help in toilet
matters, and may be the means of bringing to their notice some hitherto unknown
method of retaining, or regaining, that beauty of face and figure which is the birth-
right of every woman. Some of the ingredients mentioned below are, at present,
not generally known to the public, but any good chemist will usually be found to
have a small quantity in stock.
Wrinkles, and How to Remove them. —Worry,
late hours, or the passing of old Father Time
may cause those ugly little lines which so
detract from the freshness and charm of a
woman’s face; but the cure is always the
same. Feed the tissues beneath the skin, and
massage them with a reliable skin food,
which, while nourishing the fatty tissues,
will not coarsen the skin nor grow hairs upon
the face, as so many inferior creams do. To
remove the wrinkles bathe the face with hot
water, wipe it dry; then, while it is still
warm, smear a little mercolised wax over the
whole face, carrying it well down on the neck,
and, with the tips of the fingers, rub it gently
into the pores, working in an upward and
outward direction. Wipe off any superfluous
wax, leaving a little on the skin all night.
In the morning wash off with warm water
and Pilenta soap. The use of the wax not
only feeds the skin but removes the dead,
outer cuticle, giving the fresh, young- skin
beneath an opportunity to breathe and show
itself.
lieautifui Hair—How to Shampoo.—Whether
the hair is luxuriant and glossy, or thin and
lanky, depends very largely upon the care
bestowed upon it. To keep the hair in good
condition it should be shampooed once in
every two or three weeks. Before shampooing
massage a little pure olive oil or almond oil
into the scalp, then mix one teaspoonful of
stallax granules in a cup of hot water, fill a
basin with warm water, into which tlie ends
of the hair can fall, and shampoo the stallax
mixture into the scalp in the usual way.
Rinse, and dry by fanning with a palm fan,
or in the open air, if the weather permits.
Should the hair be naturally Very greasy, the
oil massage should be omitted. Stallax can
be bought at any chemist’s in quarter-pound
sealed packages: it keeps indefinitely, and one
one package contains sufficient for twenty-five
or thirty shampoos:
The Question of Powder. —Powder should
never be used by anyone with a tendency to
wrinkle, for it fills up the lines on the face,
and tends to deepen and accentuate the wrin-
kles. In the place of powder, get about one
ounce of cleminite from any chemist, and dis-
solve it in four tablespoons of warm water.
Place it in a bottle, and apply to the skin with'
the finger tips, allowing it to dry on the face.
This gives the skin a soft, peacli-like bloom,
and prevents that unbecoming, greasy appear-
ance which is the bane of many a woman’s
life.
A Tonic for the Hair. —During the spring and
early summer, the hair generally needs a little
stimulant to its growth, in. the form of a
tonic. To make a simple and inexpensive one.
at home, take four ounces of bay rum, and
add to it one ounce of borannim. Shake thor-
oughly, and leave for thirty minutes, then
strain, and add Sufficient water to make half
a pint. At night, massage the scalp for ten
minutes, then dab the mixture into the roots
with a soft sponge. Five minutes’ brushing
in the-morning will he found beneficial in giv-
ing an added gloss to tlie hair
To Wave the Hair. —llot irons, when employed
to wave the hair, not only make it harsh and
brittle, spoiling color of fair or auburn hair,
biit induce a tendency towards greyness. To
give a wave to straight hair, damp it with
silmerine and either plait, tying the ends, or
arrange in waving pins for a short time. A
couple of ounces will last quite a long time.
A Greasy Skin and Hlaekheads. — Blackheads
are absolutely fatal to beauty, for they give a
coarse, dirty look to the face which no cos-
metics can ever hide. The way to remove
blackheads quickly and effectively has only
recently been discovered. For many years
those who suffered in this way were restricted
in their diet, denied sweets, and had to suffer
other privations. Modern science has, how-
ever, provided a simple, effective and quick
remedy. Dissolve one stymol tablet in a glass
of hot water, and when the effervescence has
subsided, dip a small, soft sponge into the
liquid, and bathe the face. Leave for a few
minutes, then dry with a towel, and the black-
heads will come off. For a greasy skin, bathe
' the face three times a week with stymol. and
spray with cold water every morning, using a
vulcanite throat spray.
To Reduce the Figure. —With the present
fashions, even more than with the tight skirts,
it is imperative that the-woman who wishes to
look smart should be slim. To reduce one’s
weight without injuring one’s health is no
easy matter, and can only be done by taking-
some specific which will counteract the natural
tendency towards putting on flesh. A few
clynol berries, eaten regularly, will speedily
transform the fat, ungainly figure into one of
slim and graceful proportions, without any in-
convenience whatever, and in a very short
time. Clynol berries are quite pleasant to the
taste, having a marked peppermint flavor.
To Restore Grey Hair to its Original Color. —r
Streaks of grey, whether in fair or dark hair,
are always unbecoming, and prove a cruel tell-
tale of age. It is not generally known that
an ounce of tammalite, which can be bought
at any chemist’s, mixed with four ounces of
bay rum, makes a most satisfactory lotion for
restoring grey hair to its natural color. Apply
the lotion night and morning with a soft,
toothbrush, and, after a few days, the hair
will be found to be gradually returning to its
original shade.
Superfluous Hair—its Cause and Cure. —Super-
fluous hair is invariably the result of using
inferior soap or bad face creams. To remove
the hairs, apply a little powdered pheminol
mixed to a paste with cold water and applied
to the skin with a bone knife. The skin
should be dusted with powdered alum before
applying the pheminol. and the paste removed
after being left on for two minutes; This
treatment is recommended not only to remove
the growth temporarily, but to eventually kill
the roots. [2

S.” : Ssme years ago the ferryboat
Manly got into trouble about Middle Head.
She was filled with passengers, and Sydney
was nearer to the long-expected catastrophe
than it was aware of. Panic-stricken offi-
cialdom then decided on thorough-going
measures with regard to life-saving appli-
ances, and every ferry proceeding beyond
George’s Head has since then had to be
equipped with anchors, chains and distress
signals, the same as coasting vessels.
Through a combination of good luck and
good managing there had not arisen a single
occasion to use these things—until two
youths lately boarded the’ Cygnet, lying up
for overhaul. Finding the signal bombs
they started to experiment with a hammer.
There was a deafening report (these things
are designed to attract notice within a 10-
miles’ radius) and a very dead boy. Curi-
osity kills more than a cat when it gets a
fair chance.
“Canuck” : Lately I observed in the door-
way of a Richmond (Melb.) grocery several
sets of blacking brushes, marked “Is. the
set of three.” The goods- being of fairly
good make the price was obviously absurd,
so I investigated and found that certain
importers were selling the sets wholesale at
10s. Od. per doz. They also had a line of
combined polishing and blacking brushes at
4s Od. per doz. Both lines came from
Japan. Going further I found that 'he
lowest quotations of Australian-made brushes
were: polishing. 95.: blacking, Bs.; and dirt
7s. Od. per dozen —245. per doz. for sets of
three. I had just recollected that these
10s. Od. and 4s. Od. Japanese lines had
already paid a duty of 05 per cent, ad vat.
when I was called away to attend a non-
political meeting to “organise for the pur-
pose of capturing Germany’s trade.” '
A Sydney paper, discussing the hole-and-
corner “selection” of members of Parliament
by Liberal and Labor Leagues, demands to
know “what is to stop any wirepuller, acting
for a candidate or against him. offering in-
ducements to a member of the league to vote
in one direction or abstain from voting in
anotherV” Nothing, of course. On the
other hand, there will be a large inducement
to pull wires so long as the capture of a
small party machine is equivalent, under the
party system, to the capture of a whole con-
stituency.
TO A MISERABLE YELPER.
Stop it, pale, piffling poet!
Curses upon your head!
You are a sham, and you know it !
Stop it, you babbling poet!
Go and yelp under the bed !
Into the dustbox your rubbish cram !
Back" to the bush you infernal sham !
BLAST all your blitherings ! BLAST AND DAMN ! !

BRISBANE.
Townsville.
The Birmingham Small Arms Company Limited
has subscribed £lOO,OOO to the War Loan. About
£30,000 worth of the stock is taken up to cover
applications from- their employees, to whom special
facilities have been given for the purchase of the
scrip over extended periods by weekly instalments.
The Company will give their work-people just double
the maximum time for the acquisition of the stock
which the Government scheme affords, and the privi-
lege has been very widely taken advantage of.*

IN BANANA LAND
A beautiful memorial church has just been
erected at Tamrookum to the memory of the
late R. M. Collins, pastoralist and good
fellow. But Collins’s real memorial will
be the conserving of the noble Laming-
ton Plateau as a State park—a step which
he advocated and assisted with real public-
spirit on all occasions.
That Captain Colin Austin whose death
at Gallipoli was reported last week was a
soi. of Robert Austin, an old Westralian
exnlorer who- finished his days as Sergeant-
al-Arms in the Queensland Assembly.
He took Kitchener’s eve during the Boer
discord, and it was at the big fellow’s iuvita-;
tion that, yohrig Austin stayed behind after
the war ended.
One Pooran Dabee Singh, a dweller in
Brisbane for some 40 years, raises an uncom-
fortable point for the Ryan Government ;o
perch upon: Owing to a recent decision
of the Queensland Cabinet. ; Asiatics may
not work in the canefields. But- Ger-
mans may. Pooran Dabee Singh wants
to know from the Chief Secretary' if this
is the reward his countrymen receive _ for
battling against the Gerhog in Belgium,
France and Gallipoli. In the same specious
manner somebody might now raise an equally
uncomfortable point for'Pooran Dabee Singh
to perch upon. .It is only necessary to ask
him whether it was for rewards, then, that
the brave Indian troops-have been battling
in Europe.
“E.N.M.” on a scheme that Ned Kelly
overlooked :—•
As a money-maker in a good cause —or any old
cayise —give me a “mock court” such as was held
at Brisbane’s Patriotic day shivoo. The proceedings
were at once decorous, liquorbus, hilarious and
meritorious. The judges (Frank Boucher, C.J. ;
Tom Welsby and W. Smythe) took part in the pro-
cession in wigs, red robes, etc. Pat Ranson was
Grown Prosecutor. J. Cahill (a nephew of Queens-
land’s Police Commissioner)- and W. Webb were coun-
sel for defendants; but when it was found, that they
charged £1 fees, and invariably ..begged the .Bench
to double all fines imposed, most of their clients
just curled up. It cost a bob for any of the
public to enter the court—and 2s. 6d. to get out.
Every now and then all hands were fined for “loiter-
ing,” and every then and now the Court adjourned
for a drink —whereupon Tom Welsby would fine
himself 2s. 6d for adjourning the Court and an-
other 2s. 6d. for having the drink. Innocent by-
standers who drank with the Bench had to pay up
for doing it. Argument meant more fines ; one cul-
prit debated the point for 25 seconds, and was finally
'counted out for 25 half-crowns, plus his original
fine. Whittingham, M.L.C., was fined £5 for being-
known to keep and feed old racehorses that had been
dead for years. Arthur Morgan, Lieutenant-Governor,
got a £5 punch for having deserted his political
jbb, and “Jim” Clarke (Brisbane’s “Pearl King”)
was hit to leg for the midnight murdering of oysters
which if allowed to mature might have fun to seed
pearls. A leading bank-manager was bled for refus-
ing an overdraft, and the entire team of .footballers
were brought in and compelled to part. Theodore,
the Treasurer, gave up £1 for failing to turn a
deficit into a credit balance, and “Bishop” Barnes,
of the late Denham Ministry, was fined for flirting-
in the political sense. W. J. Cpakes,- a director of
Finney’s, was neatly caught for a quid. He was
charged with being on the premises without his
“Block.” The Court gathered about £250 between
1.30 and 5.30 p.m., an average, roughly, of £1 per
minute. The C.T.A. has been asked to repeat the
“Court” at an early date, and with a little more
preparation and a wider advertisement £5OO should
be netted for the wounded. In S. Australia the
Travellers did so well in the city that bands of
them are now invited up to country towns to repeat
their crime; and the Queensland Association hopes
for equal success.
“Zechariah Banana” : One of Queensland’s
upside-down laws deals with the accommo-
dation of shearers. The pastoralist is
obliged to provide a decent hut for the bar-
bers who while away their time On his run
for a few weeks in the year; but th&*men
who work for him the calendar round he
can house in any old pig-kennel. Also, the
land-holder employing less than nine men is
untouched by existing legislation, though
every bush-worker knows that it is the
small selector, and not the squatter, who
expects his hands to sleep in tanks and
spend their time between drinks beneath a
gum tree or in the shelter of an upturne'd
kerosene tin. The Assembly now threatens
to overhaul these incongruities.
“Mulga”: The rec-asing of artesian bores
is by no means a new experiment in Banana-
land. I know of several places in the War-
rego district where the plan has been man-
aged successfully, with the result that a
diminished flow has immediately returned
to its former dimensions. Boring contractors
have advocated this way of increasing flows
for years. The Canadians have seen it suc-
cessfully carried out on oil bores at home;
but as the cost is almost as much as the
original bore —from £l to 255. per foot —
THE INSOLENCE OE THESE FELLOWS!
DEPARTING BOOKKEEPER (sacked by the Boss): “Well, good lye. Sorry I have to leave
you. I'd have made a man of you if I'd stayed a little longer."
the squatter thinks hard before he chances
it. What"makes the flows fluctuate it is
hard to say. I know of two bores within
a quarter of a mile of one another, and as
the flow increases in due it decreases in the
other, and vwe versa. The temperature of
the water varies in the same manner. A fair
average flow is a million gallons daily.
O’Connor’s bore at Coongoola siding, near
Cunnamulla, yielded a daily five million gal-
lons when first struck, but was regulated to
run a far smaller quantity afterwards. Talk-
ing about regulating artesian supplies, the
waste of artesian water in Queensland was
scandalous until the Government assumed
control of the bores. Incidentally, if a per-
son is subject to rheumatism a week’s -work
on an artesian bore, screwing up , casing
after the flow has been struck, will cure
him. as a rule.
The day of the youthful Under-Secretary
has arrived in Bananaland, the latest of
the genus being T. W. McCawley, who
becomes permanent head of his Department
(Justice) at the age of 34. McCawley is a
Toowoomba boy who served as a clerk in a
solicitor’s office in that town, whence he
drifted t 0... Brisbane and joined the Public
Service. He is now a barrister of some
tonnage and has been eminently successful
in piloting Crown law business into a happy
haven. Industrious, capable and methodical,
he has few superiors in the Queensland
Public Service.
Cornin’ Into Work.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
You a-comin’ in,
Me a-goin’ out,
Passin’ ev’ry day
In life’s roundabout.
Passin’ by to work,
Not a word to say ;
Only smilin’-like,
As we met each day.
Winter cornin’ on.
Rain a-fallin’ fast;
Courage in me hands.
Passed the word at last.
Held me gingham up—-
“Come in from the wet”;
Then you smiled an’ spoke.
An’—l’m glad we met.
QUEENSI AND.
C. B. ASTLEY.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, September 4, 1915.
My Dear Thargomindaii,—
Brisbane had another day-out yesterday
when the actors and actresses, the stage-
hands and the call-boy united their talents
towards raising money for the Patriotic
funds. There was a procession and a matinee,
the latter at H. M. Theatre. The show-people
.with J. C. Williamson, the King’s Royal
Dramatic co., the Dandies, the Empire co.,
the Tivoli co. and Harry Plimmer (holiday-
ing) lent a helping hand, or a voice, or an
ecstatic kick, or a ragtime extract from
Shakspeare ; and even our blasd aristocracy
sat up and entered into the simple amuse-
ments of the village-folk. Benefit to funds,
about £3OO.
The National Council of Women has
opened a bureau in the Town Hall, and is
busy enrolling females for National Ser-
viee. Eve wants to know how many of her
sisters there are to help Adam (helping in
this case doesn’t merely mean looking after
his wigwam or smoothing his nerves when
he comes home with his. load of cares and
other accumulations, but Really Doing
Something while he looks after his own
wigwam and smooths his own nerves). The
questions to be answered are related to the
Australian War Census. For instance,
“What sort of work can you do?” “Will
you do regular work for payment, or will
you work voluntarily?” “How many hours
a day are you prepared to give to National
Service?” Lady Goold-Adams was the pre-
siding Eve at the N official opening of the
bureau on Tuesday/.
The Women’s- Mutual Service Club has
arranged a series of lectures on cookery,
and a number of demonstrations on cutting-
out and making-up children’s frocks. The
organisation is going aMad in fine style,
and her Ex. of the Commonwealth, admiring
it 'from afar, is forming an exactly similar
one in Melbourne.
Miss Myra Philp, daughter of Mr. and
Mrs. “Jim” Philp, the well-known Aus-,
tralian writer, journeyed to Townsville this,
week, and collected Mr. Spooner, son of a
Church of England clergyman. Mrs. Philp
gave a tea-party at Rowe’s to wish good
luck to the marriage.
Two engagements, which will particularly
interest Central Queenslanders, have lately
been announced. The first is between Joan
Archer, only daughter of the Archers of
Gracemere, and Alister Archer, who is chas-
ing the Unspeakable with his mates of the
25th Battalion. The prospective bride-
groom’s parents live in Norway. The
other promise binds Miss Grace Reade, of
Capella, and young Lewis Walker, son of
Mr. and Mrs.. A. C. Walker, of Bingera
cattle station.
Yet another engagement, this time of
interest to many Sydneysiders and Brisbane
folk. The bride-to-be is Kathleen Murray,
daughter of Mr. Murray, of Gartness,
Manly, Queensland; and the groom—who
also wears the universal khaki —Gimme W.
Watson, of Maoriland. Miss Murray has
been on the stage .for the last year, and had
good prospects of> “making a name.” But
why waste time manufacturing an article
that may not satisfy you when one that
fills the bill is waiting on the spot?
The prospective groom has three brothers
with the army, and the other party to the
contract has a brother, five cousins and
three uncles at the Front. The brother is
Lt.-Surgeon A. Murray, with our boys at
the Dardanelles.
• Two University lads who are doing well
in the locality are Norman Lloyd and Fred
Small. Both left here with the sappers.
Lloyd has been made a sergeant with
the Fourth field company of engineers, at-
tached to the second division. Young Small,
who is a grandson of the late Capt.
Trouton, of Sydney, has been appointed
quartermaster-sergeant of the Fifth field
company of engineers, with the second divi-
sion of the Australian Forces.
One of the members of the Assembly, Mr.
Grayson, was thrown out of a motor-car the
other day while on a recruiting expedition.
The vehicle in which he travelled collided
with another car, out of which two children
were hurled in the same untidy manner as
the legislator. It was- the party’s lucky day,
for it was all more hurtled than hurt.
A factory for manufacturing sheepskin
vests for Tommy Bananaland has been
started. Several generous donations of
skins have already been made, but some
thousands more are urgently wanted. Three
of the jumbuck’s wrapper make two vests,
so if any good Queenslander wishes to do
something more for the boys in the trenches
—it will be mighty cold soon in Gallipoli—
all he or , she has to do is to kill the fatted
lamb and forward its pelt to the lied Cross
Society.
Godfrey Rivers, who has guided the
stumbling pencils of' at least two genera-
tions of Brisbane boys and girls, has re-
signed his position as head of the art depart-
ment of the Technical College, and shortly
will pack up his canvas and paints and flit
to the Apple Isle. Thence he goes to Eng-
land. Many a budding artist will remember
the kindness of Godfrey Rivers when times
were hard. At least one Brisbane artist
received an offer of the cash necessary to
pay for his studies abroad, but he was not
able to accept the generous offer, and since
lias made good in the South.
Wyndliam Pike, son of the managing
director of Pike Bros., has joined the motor
transport section of the Kangaroos, with
his cousin, E.. Hayes,
Dr. Costello Shaw received a cable this
week saying that his. son, Roy., who went
to England to efilisf. had been given a
commission in the Artillery.
Yours affectionately,
AUSTRALIA JANE.
The death of Patrick Robertson Gordon,
ior many years Queensland’s Chief Inspec-
tor of Stock, removes one of the best-known
men in the State. Gordon was not only an
official of weight; he . was also a _ writer
whose pen on all matters pertaining to
sheep, cattle and land always commanded
attention and respect: Among other things
he has the credit of having introduced the
present brands system of Bananaland, which
is generally admitted as an improvement on
that of almost every other country. In
private life Gordon was known as an ardent
lover of music, Death snapped his major
chord in his 85th. year. .. : c .
After many years’ urging, the_ Depart-
ment of Public Instruction has decided that
attendance at German schools is not to be
regarded as valid excuse for absence from
the State schools. Teachers are now' wait-
ing to see whether Minister Hardacre is
vertebrate or invertebrate. Past adniinis-
f ration of the compulsory clauses of the
Education Act merely excited public snickers
and pedagogic snorts. " ..,
“Outback”: In doing its bit in the
Empire’s need, Longreach, in the wild and
woolly West, has a better record than even
Charleville. Since the big argument started
360 men from there have left for the Front,
and others are moving. This does not count
the men who have enlisted from other places.
As Longreach lias subscribed £7OOO to the
Patriotic Funds, and on Queensland Day
weighed in with .over £2600 as well, it is
emphatically keeping its end up.

Look to Your Teeth.
There are no teeth like those furnished by
Nature. For service, comfort, and good
health, artificial teeth cannot be com-
pared with a natural set.
Sensible people will accordingly try to pre-
serve their teeth as long us possible by
keeping them clean. Cold water and soap
and water are useful, but are somewhat
ineffectual. Rough powders, such as cuttle-
fish, crabseyes, crude chalks and charcoal
are too severe on the enamel.
EUDENTA TOOTH PASTE is scientifi
cally prepared to clean the teeth, sweeten
the breath, harden the gums and disinfect
the mouth (thus preventing decay).
EUDENTA is therefore regarded as “the
perfection of dental luxuries.” 1/- tube every-
where. Post free 3/3. Free sample posted.

HAVE YOU GOT
CATARRH ?
If you have a dropping; of phlegm into the throat,
If your nose is stopped up,
If crusts form in your nose,
If you are easily susceptible to colds,
If you suffer from frontal headaches,
If you have noises in the head,
If your hearing is affected,
If you have to clear your throat and expectorate often,
if a change in the weather affec is you,
If you are a victim to shortness of breath,
If you sneeze frequently,
If your sense of smell is affected,
y° u have a heavy, tired feeling on awaking,
If you suffer from a husky throat,
If you are affected with any of the above symptoms,
the chances are that you are suffering from
It is an exceedingly dangerous com-
plaint, and is so insidious that the sufferer hardly
realises how serious is his or her condition until
matters have reached an almost chronic stage.
Catarrh. often begins with a cold in the bead, hut it never stops there.
1 ie simple cold becomes a protracted one, while the discharge from the
nose grows more piofuse and uncomfortable.
Ihe inflammation extends to the throat and bronchial tubes, causing
hoarseness, a tickling sensation, and an aggravating cough. The matter
that is continually dropping hack into the throat finds its way into the
Etomach, resulting in a distressing form of dyspepsia, nausea, and loss of
appetite and strength.
The catarihal poisons are absorbed into the blood, and all the memhianes
of the body become infected. Wliat was supposed to he a purely local
uisease has become constitutional, deep-seated, and chronic, and may
eventually result in Deafness, Bronchitis, Asthma, Neuritis, Catarrh of
the Stomach, or that dread scourge of the'numan race, Consumption.
