Captain Vallange, the long, lean Madden
son-in-law, is in charge of a receiving-station
for aeroplane news on the English coast.
When a fagged airman hops in, grunting
something about a Zeppelin being on its way
back to the Eatherland with a broken tail,
Vallange hastily puts the glad tidings into
publishable English and bustles it over the
wire to the War Office. You will remember
him as an A.D.C. here in the misty past.
Stout Guy Madden is employed for the
present at transport work. For years he
clothed his three-ply chest in a Highland
uniform (I suppose because the family is
Irish), but he has not yet been accepted for
service abroad. Like many other would-be
Gallipolites who were in the soldiering busi-
ness before 1914, he has been found to be
too useful here. Still, he hopes to be some-
where near Constantinople before the end of
the year.
A masterpiece of strategy in ticket opera-
tions stands to the credit of the three
matrons who are pledged to run the
“Oriental” monthly song teas. Instead of
bring six ss. tickets at Mrs. Suburbia by
post, the trio sent along the 25 bobs’ worth
with a kindly letter inviting the recipients
to decorate the committee. Work, nominal;
admittance to each show (and presumably
to the committee itself), ss. a month. P.S.
—Contributions to be sold or raffled tor the
cause thankfully received. Mrs. Suburbia
swallowed the bait very nicely, and she
swore to stump up, even if it entailed butter-
less bread, as a thanksgiving offering for
being asked to mix in such high circles.
1 rovidence, if it takes any interest what-
ever in high circles, must have intended
them for that sort of work.
Percy Rowan, killed in action at Gallipoli,
was a son of the late Andrew Rowan, Vic-
torian wine-cellarman. He went to Wes-
tralia some time ago to take up land, and
started for the war with the W.A. Light
Horse. His mother still lives at the old
Brighton-road home, but most of the family'
is scattered. The fallen soldier was a
nephew of the John Maddens, the C.J. and
Andrew Rowan having each married a
Stephen sister.
Rear Canon Snodgrass, vicar of St.
Paul’s, Geelong (Vic.), has for long been
recognised as an expert creator of a refined
drawing-room atmosphere. His bachelor
days-at-home at Geelong are a rosy dream,
and his afternoon-tea manner most soothing
and benign. He dotes on cats, and has a
tribe of the quadrupedal variety about the
vicarage, always prepared to supply a tor-
toiseshell kitten to any parishioner who may
need something by w T hich to remember her
dear adviser. The late Janet Clarke was a
Snodgrass, and the William Clarke connec-
tion at least did Evelyn Snodgrass no harm
in his upward climb to the perfect peace of
a cat-encompassed canonry.
The John Langs, w T ho some time ago
struck a rich financial patch and went to
inhabit a five-acre pleasaunce in the most
heavily-gilded portion of South Yarra, are
in England, where they are taking a rest
after a strenuous period of honorary labor
at a military hospital. John Lang, who
married a daughter of the late Colonel
Umphelby, is a Melbourne solictor, best
known as secretary of the annual Henley-on-
the-Yarra diversion. Pie is about six inches
under the military height, which partly
accounts for his not departing with the Aus-
tralian troops; but at the hospital the
authorities made him a captain, in spite of
his stature. Dr. W. H. Lang, V.A.T.C.
handicapper, is John’s uncle, and the late
Andrew Lang, of England (he of the
brindled hair”) was another.
War isn’t all—well, what General Sher-
man said it was. I know of one army nurse
who got. herself engaged to a nice officer
in S’Africa by allowing him to brush her
hair for her. And now here’s Philip Gibbs
describing how he. tried to be guide and
chaperone to a fugitive hosnital staff during
the heaviest fighting in Belgium, and how he
had some trouble to round up the nurses
who were “getting out of hand and indulging
in private adventures.” One of them, a buxom
and jolly soul, who “didn’t care a damn,”
had established friendly relations with a
naval lieutenant, and “I had great trouble in
dragging her away from his engaging conver-
sation. Others had discovered a shop where
coffee was being served to British
soldiers, who were willing to share it with
attractive ladies.” It might interest you to
know that he finally got the lot—including
some frightened nuns—into a beer tavern, the
nuns breasting the bar with the reverend
mother in the centre, wearing, as Gibbs says,
“a little aureole round her head from the
glamor of the pewter pots.”
Katie Turner is a young metal worker
who, with ordinary luck, should wrest a well-
buttered crust from the world. Architect
Pladdon, being impressed with her skill, is
giving her a shove along; and the Arts and
Crafts are showing some of her stuff, includ-
ing settings for enamels and jewels, as well
as large, useful things.
. One rest-house for our returned wounded
is run by a member of the Toorak peerage
who lacks insight or something. Tea is
served there in the evenings by his express
command. Apropos of this he explained
loftily to a friend : “I have chosen nice, sen-
sible servants, from my own staff and from
the houses of friends, to wait on the soldiers
women of their own class, y’know, so that
they will not feel embarrassed.” And then
the Duke, having made his inferiors com-
fortable, went and had another roll in his
money.
Miss Armytage, who shared the vice-
presidency of the absurd little Gollywogs
with Melba, has opened a shop in the new
Elizabeth II ouse. It is a Gollywog emporium
and a continuous bazaar stall for gifts.' The
attendants are all leisured women who are
playing at shop-girl gratis, and the results
are to help invalided Australians. Small
boy scouts and some small girls from the
Training Home help as messengers and kids-
©f-all-work. A sixpenny tea-room lurks
behind the bazaar, and musical entertain-
ments supplied by amateurs attract restless
khaki men with no engagements on hand.
Yours sincerely.
JOHANNA.

AUDITOR-GENERAL’S REPORT.
In accordance with the provisions of Section 20 of the Commonwealth Bank Act, 1911, I have to
report that the Aggregate Balance-Sheet of the Commonwealth Bank of Australia for the ended
the 30th June, 1915, has been examined by officers under my direction, as well as by thyself. The
several items of the account, as above set .forth, have been verified with the books and vouchers, with the
securities held by the Bank, and with Statements certified by the respective Managers and Accountants
of the Bank. "
I have obtained all the information and explanations required bv me. The said Balance,-Sheet has
been prepared in the form prescribed by the Regulations under the Act, and, in my opinion, it exhibits
a correct view of the affairs of the Bank. ,
I have also to report that the sum of £5,700,623 18s. 2d. above set forth was held by. the' Bank in
Gold Coin and Bullion for the Treasurer of the Commonw r ealth.
J. W. ISRAEL,
- Auditor-General for the Commonwealth,
31st August, 19.15.

Auditors’ Certificate and Report.
In accordance with the provisions of section 113 of the Companies (Consolidation) Act 1908, we
report to the shareholders as follows: —-
We have examined the above balance-sheet with the books at head office, and have had access to the
branch accounts transmitted to the head office from Australia and New Zealand. We have verified the
correctness of the cash, money at call and short notice, Stock Exchange loans, investments, and the bills
of exchange held in London, and'having obtained all the information and explanations we have required,
we afe of opinion tlr t such balahce-sheet is properly drawn up so as to exhibit a true and correct view of
sthe state of the company’s affairs, according to the best of our information and the explanations given to
'us, and as shown by the books-of the company and the said branch accounts.
WHINNEY, SMITH and WHINNEY, Chartered Accountants, Auditors.

RHEUMATISM AGAIN.
.Many people who had rheumatism last
winter are beginning to feel the aches and
pams of the complaint again. It is typical
of rheumatism that it usually lies dormant
during the summer, only to appear with
renewed vigor in winter. The dregs of the
complaint have been in the system all the
time. Victims will continue to suffer year
after year unless the cause is completely
eliminated from the system. Acid in the
blood is believed to be the cause of rheu-
matism, and medical men have noted that
there is a marked and rapid thinning of the
blood during the attacks. The system is
thus deprived of its natural resistance to
the disease. Much success has followed
treatment of rheumatism with Dr. Wil-
liams’ Pink Pills, and sufferers who have
treated themselves with this medicine have
found that, rheumatism has been perma-
nently banished and did not return with
the next cold, damp weather. These pills
have a specific action on the blood, increas-
ing and enriching it, and they tone up the
whole system, enabling it to throw off the
poisonous impurities that cause rheumatism.
•n Ur °wn chemist can supply you with these
pills, {sufferers from rheumatism can obtain
a handy booklet on “Diseases of the Blood”
by'sending their names and addresses to the
Dr. Williams’ Medicine Co., Sydney.*
Troublesome Constipation.
Sufferers from constipation know from ex-
perience that after temporary relief has been
obtained by the use of a purgative, the con-
oition . often becomes worse. Most strong
purgatives have a tendency to cause con
stipation after their immediate effects are
over. Pinkettes, the new laxative, are a real
help m correcting the. irregularity. Tli-y
do not wear out in their effects, and taken
according to directions the patient will find
that they soon help to create a healthy regu-
lanty. They are. a safe family medicine
because they contain no liabit-creating drug •
they are daintily got up ; are easily given to
children, and they act thoroughly without
causing sickness and griping. Send for a
free trial sample to the Dr. Williams’ Medi-
cine Co., Sydney, mentioning The Bulletin.
linkettes are Is. a. bottle at all medicine
dealers.*

Good all Through
Look Big Ben
square in the face-
lie’s good all round
and good all through
—faithful, loyal and
punctual.
Big Ben lias great,
strong keys that make
his wind-up easy. His
broad, deep-toned bell
makes your get-up easy
and pleasant.
He rings on the dot at
any time lie’s told—with
a straight five-minute call,
or a gentle nudge every
few seconds.
He will stop short in the
middle of a tap during either
call if you want to shut him off.
Big Ben is made in La Salle,
Illinois, U.S.A by Westclox. His
price is 12/6, postage 1/- extra. If
you cannot find him in your town,
send a money order to the makers’
Australian office, Westclox, cr. King
and Kent Sts., Sydney. They will
have one mailed to you by the
nearest jeweller.

The Inky Way.
“Gus Gosh”: The first Australian Inky
Wayfarer to return home damaged from
Turkey was Blake Young, of the Adelaide
Register. A bullet, which entereu one side
of the throat and emerged from the other,
paved the way for pneumonia. Altogether
eight of the Register staff went to sub-edit
the Turk. Young is a son of A. 11., of the
'Tiber, and of Mrs. .Jeanne F., who for-
merly divided her spare time between con-
tributing to the Register and carrying on
the late Catherine Helen Spence’s propor-
tional representation work. Lately she has
been more busy in seeing that the Holy
City does its fair share in supplying ambu-
lances.
* * * *
The S. M. Herald discovered an anony-
mous bard the other day, and printed on its
leader page some verses entitled “The Roll
Call,” “sent in by a soldier, who wishes his
name to be secret, from Egypt.” The fur-
ther information was offered that the self-
effacing soldier had stopped one or two bul-
lets. Alas, the verses had appeared in the
Melbourne Argus three months before, con-
tributed by “Oriel.” Sad to reflect that a
hero should be villain enough to take down
a simple-minded journal like the S. M.
Herald.
* * * *
“Jink”: Newspaperdom in Christchurch
(M.L.) is gasping hard for breath. The
Star, which has the habit of making itself
heard every evening, got on to a story about
a soldier’s widow who was in financial
trouble, and it sent a reporter to
talk to her. The reporter copied a nasty
letter sent her by the local Patriotic Com-
mittee and published it with an article urging
her claims. This started other letters, and
finally the Patriotic Committee discussed the
matter. The talk was duly reported, the
committee’s remarks being decidedly unfav-
orable to the widow. The widow’s lawyer
immediately fired a writ at the Star claim-
ing £5OO for libel, using the first article as
the basis of her action. After a two days'
sitting, in which plaintiff’s counsel with
great spirit asked the jury to “order these
newsmongers to keep their hands off soldiers'
widows w r hen their husbands were lying on
the slopes of Gallipoli with Turkish bullets
in ’em,” the stolid 12 awarded her £250, in
spite of a summing-up by the Judge in favor
of the newspaper. The Star has solemnly
sworn that it will now give up the vicious
habit of helping lame dogs and poor lone
widows, and will stick to giving the Kaiser
beans.
* # * *
One German industry that will be killed
by the war is the “Tauchnitz edition” of
English books. Australia does not see much
of them, because they cannot be brought
here except as smuggled goods, but all over
the Continent of Europe they appeal to the
British and American tourists. Old Baron
Tauchnitz, who started the idea about 1840,
was so fair-minded that he paid the authors
whose work lie appropriated, though copy-
right law then did not compel it; but he is
only a name now, and the name is going to
be fatal to the trade. So Louis Conard, a
Parisian publisher, lias jumped in, and pro-
poses to annex the whole business.
“Austin Biff”: Re skirts controlling
printeries. Mortlake (Vic.) Despatch is
complied, edited and etceteraed by two
women. In their establishment the usual
indescribable chaos is conspicuous by its
non-existence. Up till the time I met that
office I thought it was the First Command-
ment, handed down by the first father of the
first chapel, that all printeries should be in
an everlasting state of “pie.” Probably it
was; but Eve naturally found the Forbidden
much more pleasant. And, as in the Begin-
ning, I agree with her.
* * * *
“Foe”: What a cursed substance is wood-
pulp ! The cable says that some escaped
Italians who were forcibly employed by
the Germans in an explosive factory in the
Duchy of Baden have brought the news
that the Huns, being short of cotton, are
making nitro-cellulose out of trees. So in
times of peace the forest is made into cheap
literature to addle our brains, while in
time of war it is ground into powder to
blow them out.
•** * *
An old N.S.W. journalist, Allen Campbell
Wood, died in his native town, Yass, a few
days back, aged 67. He was formerly pro-
prietor and editor of the Courier, published
in his birthplace, and was connected with
almost every local institution, from the
church to the race club.
THE LAST HORROR.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
l-'aitb, Hope and Charity hail (lied,
Or so it seemed to me,
As I sat down my desk beside
Before some stone-cold tea.
And all the ghosts I had defied
Enjoyed my misery.
I don’t know what had burst my dream
And let Life’s joy run out,
But in my sky there was no gleam,
And all was fear and doubt.
And, as I said before, the team
Of ghosts sat round about.
I had no friend in all the world ;
I sat apart, alone;
1 was as one who had been hurled'
Into a Hell of stone.
The standard of my life seemed furled,
My fortune skin and bone.
Death seemed a boon too good to crave
For one in such a mess;
I do not think a galley-slave—
That symbol of distress—
E’er yearned as I did for the grave
And its forgetfulness.
And so at all I sneered and sniped,
And deemed I’d plumbed Despair,
While Thought’s sly devils leaped and piped.
But I had more to bear,
For lo 1 a poet came and wiped
His hot hands on my hair!
N.SAV.

The Road.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
OH, happy you who came to-day
A-down that old, enchanted way,
With nought but joy for load ;
M hat saw you as you travelled by,
From dawn to dusk, beneath the sky
Upon the Long South Road?
bile yet the world was dim and chill,
And every singing bird was still,
I saw six stars in an amber sky.
Each merry star with a twinkling eye,
Watching until the conquering sun
His race exultant had begun,
And wide his oriflamme had spread
Above the hills of dawn. Blood-red
Lapped every wavelet on the sand,
And upwards from the dewy land
There leapt that feathered lute, the lark,
To mock with song the routed dark.
The blithesome chorus of the morn,
Bell-like, on every wind was borne ;
The fern with myriad gems was hung,
And wayside spiders deftly flung
Their jewelled nets on twig and spray
To garnish well the winding way ;
And there was laughter in the breeze,
And joy upon the budding trees
As I came down. And then—and then,
As I passed through the dusky glen,
I met a maid so pale and sweet,
Who passed on swift and silent feet;
And by the fragrance in the air,
And by the blossoms in her hair,
Untold I knew indeed that she
Who trod the earth so soundlessly,
This lissom, lightsome, lovely thing,
Could be no other than the Spring!
And so I fell aside and followed her,
And as she hastened through the dewy grass
The tender ferns uncurled to watch her pass.
Aloft she tossed her shining, yellow hair,
And where it touched the branches overhead
The golden kowhai blooms their radiance
shed.
Bhe pressed her finger-tips on leafy trails,
And like a starry garland on the trees
The clematis swayed whitely in the breeze.
1 followed, followed still by winding ways,
J'o where, within the forest’s deepest gloom,
She kissed the rata vines to scarlet bloom.
She sped along the highway to the town,
And where she looked on flowerless garden
beds
Blue violets uplifted scented heads.
Bare orchard boughs bent low to see her
come,
And stole the roseate wonder of her face
To lend them loveliness a little space.
And then she paused, and sang for very joy,
And like an echo came the dulcet notes
Clear-ringing from a thousand wild-birds’
throats.
I crept to touch her where she stood
Between the river and the wood.
But she \vas gone. The quickening earth
Had swift forgotten winter’s dearth.
Each poplar in the little town
Had donned anew an emerald gown,
And by the lake the willow trees
Strung slender pennants in the breeze ;
And suddenly it seemed to me
The wide world sang in harmony
A deep, ecstatic, speechless song,
That filled my ears the whole day long ;
And even when the skies had paled,
And graciously the white moon sailed
Among the stars, I heard that strain
Ascend again and yet again,
Until the earth and sky and sea
Were swept with wondrous melody;
But you, who heard it not, will say
That out upon the road to-day,
Among the fields and trees and streams
I visions saw r , and dreamed wild dreams:
Yet nought can scoff the truth away,
That firing and I both came to day
A-down the Long White Road.
M. 1..
LHLA GORMHUILLE McKAY.
Roughing It.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
MOUNTED Oil my bay mare Judy, and
leading my other bay mare Julia. I
left the Mooni River for Charleville.
a journey of 300 miles.
At St. George, qn the Balonue, I picked
up “Little Peter,” who also had two horses,
and we became mates.
As his pet. name implies he was a person
of limited proportions, .but innocent of the
usually correlative defects of self-estimated
greatness and magnificent conceit. He wasn’t
much of a talker, but he smoked prodigiously,
and asked for a match pretty often —just to
show there was no coolness arising between
us. Occasionally he would bridge two five-
mile silences with a bushman’s song:—
I’m makin’ out where the diggers go,
Where the reefs run deep an’ wide;
I’ll wet my whistle at Tally-ho,
An’ I’ll yard me a Western bride.
If th’ tracks are barren this moke I’ve got
Will do with a . rnulga tree.
An’ th’ hobble-chains an’ the old quart-pot
Still jingle a tune to me.
. We spent a few pleasant days at Mitchell,
on the Maranoa. It possessed a happy com-
bination that appealed to us—fruit, running
water and jolly girls ; also there was good
picking among the cypress pine _on the sand-
hill for Judy and Julia.
About every third night a dance was held
in the hall, and as the girls were scarce
Peter and I had a glorious time whilst some
of the youthful locals stood outside, squint-
ing round the door-post or peeping through
the window, and making scurrilous remarks
about our boots and general get-up.
“They’re always like that,” said one of the
gills. “They won't come in themselves, and
they hate to see a stranger dancing with us.
When we want partners we have to drag
them in. They’re too slow to catch grubs.”
After many months of wandering about
the bush, mixing only with men, we were in
prime condition to appreciate a whirl round
with buxom little lassies in our arms. We
held them tight—we hugged them; if the
lights had gone out we would have kissed
them. We bought them caramels and choco-
lates and silk handkerchiefs, and before the
day of departure came we had got down to
our last shilling. Ninepence of it was in
my pocket, and threepence in Peter's. But
we’d had a good time, and didn’t mind.
We also had a slight brush with the law
at Mitchell. I was reading in the tent when
two mounted troopers pulled up at my front
door.
“Ilulloa !” said one, a sandy-bearded Irish-
man. “What ’ave we got ’ere at all, at allV '
He came closer and peered in. llis com-
panion rode on. “Come out ’ere till I ’ave a
look at ye,” he said. I sat up. “Come arn
—if you’re not too lazy !”
I stepped out, trying at the same time to
recall what I had been doing. I had not been
drunk and disorderly. I had not committed
assault. I had not coveted my neighbor’s
property—nor his wife. I had no recollec-
tion of having murdered anybody. So far as
I knew I was a law-abiding citizen
“Have ye a cut on your head?” he asked
after scanning me closely.
"I haven’t.”
“Take your hat off.”
I did so. He turned and twisted in his
saddle to scrutinise me.
“Sure, it’s not ye’self that has th’ cut
head,” he said. “But p'raps th’ cut was
only a scratch. Turn round an’ lift your coat
a trifle.”
"What's the meaning of all this?” I
objected.' He was obdurate.
"Lift your coat, an’ don’t be cheeky." I
obeyed. “Faith !” lie said, “it’s not ye’self
that’s been stuck on a palin’. Were ye up
town last night?”
“I was not.”
“Ye’re wan av tlr good boys, so. Good-
ma ruin’.”
“Am I not entitled to an explanation?” I
cried after him. “What have I done to be
treated like this?”
“Ye’ve been tlirated so to prove that ye’ve
done nothing,” he answered, and away lie
rode.
Peter, who had been “seeing to the horses,”
returned shortly afterwards, talking loudly of
suing the law for insulting behavior. The
cosherman had met him among the pine trees
and subjected him to the same humiliating
treatment.
We went up to the newsagent and made in-
quiries. It transpired that a man had been
detected in a robbery at the railway sta-
tion, and while expeditiously scaling a fence
the bulge of his nether garment caught on
top of a paling, where he was detained long
enough to be marked for future identification.
We decided to leave Mitchell while our pants
were untorn.
The traveller with a swag is always made
to regret the happening of anything crimi-
nal, especially a murder. If he shows
up in a disturbed district while the hue and
cry is on he at once becomes a suspect. Weak-
minded people rush inside at sight of him
and barricade the doors, whilst other luna-
tics fly to the telephone and call the police in
frenzied, broken gasps. He is dogged and
waylaid and variously persecuted at every
turn; while innocently boiling his billy under
a gum-tree he is suddenly surprised by a
rush of bobbies, “laid on” by idiotic residents,
who cover him with guns and roar at him to
bail up. The police cause might be materi-
ally helped if some hoodlums could develop
enough common-sense to reason that a man
is not necessarily a scoundrel because he
carries a swag.
Peter rode away in a melancholy mood.
1 lalf-way across Mitchell Downs he re-
marked : “She was just gettin’ nice an’
familiar,” and lapsed into silence again.
We had wet weather and heavy travelling
all the way to Each day was
begun with a wet tramp Mter horses, and
each evening, after pitching the tent, we had
to excavate a canal round it to carry off the
surplus wetness, and strip bark to make our
naps on.
At Tyrconnel we had to swim a flooded
creek to get the horses. We rode 28 miles
that day, via Dolvadilla, and camped near
Morven. This place was originally known as
Saddler’s Waterholes, and was one of the
oldest bush camping-grounds for teamsters in
southern Queensland. Here we spent all next
day looking for Judy and Julia, and found
them at sundown —in a patch of scrub only
half a mile from camp. 'Judy, in rolling, had
filled the bell with mud. I had strained my
ear over miles of damp scenery to catch its
musical tinkle, and was taking a straight cut
back to camp when I chanced on the wan-
derer.
“Rather too late to make a start now,” I
said on returning.
Peter picked up the billycan and went
towards the gully, singing:—
“Well, mate,” seel William, “I thought it out,
An’ I sez to myself, sez I:
There’s not much hope for the rouseabout,
As th’ rousy can testify”
“We’ll stop till mornin’,” he called out
from the bottom of the gully. Half-way
back with the water he broke out again :—•
I'll roll my swag when the sun gets up,
An’ I'll camp when the sun goes down.
The Warrego was running a banker. The
whole country was flooded, and the blatant
voices of frogs greeted us as we swam a wide
billabong to get into Charleville. The town
is noted for its omnipresent herds of goats,
and as the rendezvous of carriers and travel-
lers of all denominations.
It has a choice variety of mosquito (a first-
cousin to the Hexham Greys, of Newcastle,
N.S.W.), which suck blood till they burst.
It has a formidable trunk, this vampire,
which defies any kind of clothing or blanket.
An ancient whaler of cynical aspect and

doubtful veracity assured me that the abomi-
nations had teeth also.
"1 saw a blamed queer thing one day,” lie
continued as he spat through the smoke.
“ ’Twas a skeeta on a log a-beltin’ an' a-roll-
in’ of his trunk with his hind leg. Couldn’t
make out wot was the matter with him till a
blacksmith comes along. ‘Why,’ ses he, ‘that
skeeta wants ter reach something an’ his
trunk ain’t long enough. He’s a. drorin’ of
it out.' Wal, 1 watches, an’ by-’n’-bye he
darts for my boot. There was a crack in the
uppers, an’ In' sticks his trunk through an’
bores into me prince alberts till lie ’ad half
his head buried. Rut that trunk warn’t long
enough yet. So back he goes to the log, an’
he pulls at it, an’ rolls it, an’ joggles the
concern, to draw it out some more. Then it
commenced to rain, an’ I left.”
The greatest annoyance we experienced in
the neighborhood was from the sandflies,
which appear in swarms immediately after
floods. They were particularly bad in the
mornings, and from near sundown till dark.
At these times the horses about the town
would be frantic from stings, and there was
a continual clang and jingle of bells as they
galloped madly about the bush. If not
watched they travelled for miles in their
efforts to escape from the torment.
We found it necessary to keep smoky fires
continually burning—by lopping trees and
feeding with green bushes. The horses would
stand in the thick smoke, head to tail, for
hours. Now and again they would venture
away on a trial, and dart back again at the
first attack.
There was one bob-tailed horse there, and
lie hadn’t a friend in the district. No other
animal would associate with him. lake the
Chinaman with the shorn pigtail he had lost
caste. Now and again a strange horse would
drop in and pair with him. Presently
Stranger, who would be lashing Bobby all
the time with his tail, would begin' to look
round and wonder. The sandflies were sting-
ing him badly. What the deuce was Bobby
doing? It would become unbearable, and
Stranger would turn round and examine
Bob’s anatomy. Then, seeing he had been
imposed upon, he'd put his ears back, wheel
round, and lash out like fury. I pitied poor
Bobtail, and wondered who had robbed him
of his hereditary fly whisk.
Sometimes during the day the horses would
wander two or three miles away, and on the
appearance of the pest would return at full
gallop, with a deafening bell-clatter, to the
camp. There were at least 50 smoke fires
constantly going in the vicinity, and the
horses got mixed up among them, as they
generally made for the nearest. Thus every-
body called on everybody else pretty fre-
quently in doing the rounds of the smokes,
inquiring for his horses.
When the unfortunate animals stayed out
too long they would come in dripping with
blood. The chest and underparts suffered
most: while the eyes of some were bunged
up, lips and nostrils swollen, and the body
covered with little lumps and blotches. Con-
stant rubbing to relieve the irritation caused
o. loss of hair from mane and tail, and along
the back and rump, giving them the appear-
ance of having passed through a severe
attack of mange.
The sandflies disappeared at nightfall.
Then the mosquito with the long trunk “made
things willing” till it was time for the other
fellow to come on watch and hunt up a
breakfast. There was a respite during the
middle of the day, which we spent climbing
trees and lopping branches in preparation for
the onslaught.
After being flood-bound for ten days at
Charleville I started up the Warrego alone
with the intention of doing a boomerang jour-
ney until the country dried a bit. It was
Thursday morning. The river was still
swollen, and my only hope of getting over
was to risk crossing the unfinished Charle-
ville bridge. There was a four-feet drop at
the far side and the decking was slippery.
Two horses had been crippled there that
morning, and lay on the opposite bank.
I got a bag and spread it on the planks, so
that the hind hoofs would stand on the centre
of it. Then I led Judy across. She dropped
down very gently, and with a short, quick
jump landed safely. I had barely led her out
of the way when Julia, the pack-mare, who
couldn’t bear to be parted from her mate,
came after us. She dropped down all right,
but the old fool stood there—her hindquart-
ers up in the air. The pack, having no sur-
cingle, and the saddle being held on with only
one small girth, toppled over on to her neck.
Down she went on her head, then turned a
turtle that saved her life, and elicited a roar
of laughter from the bridge-men. Her look
of offended dignity when she got up was
almost human. She blew her nose hard and
often, and rubbed a heap of river-bank out
of her eyes on Judy’s tail.
Friday.—Spent eight hours horse-hunting,
and returned to my camp at the Twenty-
seven Mile with empty bridles. Many horses
were pasturing there, and as I had not
studied the shape and marks of Judy’s and
Julia’s hoofs, it was impossible to track
them. My anxiety at this stage was tre-
mendous, for it was likely the sandflies had
driven them away.
That same evening I walked . down to
Cowrie out-station, eight miles. No one
there had seen anything of Judy and Julia.
Waited till a beast was killed, and got some
fresh meat. It was then dusk. My legs
were chafed and my heels blistered as I
began that eight-mile walk—over sand-hills
and boggy flats —back to a wet camp. It
took me five hours —the hardest and weariest
journey of my life. I spread my nap and
lay down to rest while the billy boiled. Cot
cold and stiff, and had to crawl to the fire
on hands and knees to make the tea.
Saturday.—By chance I struck Nive Junc-
tion, a small squattage in ruins, and buried
in a wilderness of cypress pine, rank grass
and bushes. The only hands were a mar-
ried couple. The manager was a grass-
widower, a dapper little bank clerk, who
was endeavoring to pick up some bush experi-
ence among the pine trees when I met him.
“Any chance of a jobl asked, after
making fruitless inquiries about my horses.
“Ever do any fencing—repairing wire
fences?” lie queried. .
“Miles of it,” I said. R ire fencing is
mv specialty.” .
’“Well, 1 want a man to repair a lot oi
fencing that’s been washed down by the
floods. Let me see —to-morrow’s Sunday.
It' you’re here by Monday it will suit me.
Of course, I’ll expect you to get to work by
nine o’clock every morning. You leave off
at one on Saturday.”
1 thanked him and departed, making a
deviation on my way back in the hope of
dropping across Judy and Julia. But my
luck was dead out. .V ragged tramp had
possession of my camp, and was griddling
the remains of my meat on a bit of twisted
wire.
Ilis own camp had caught fire a few days
before, and he had lost everything but what
he stood in. “All me forty-years’ gatherin’
gone into smoke, an’ none of it insured,” he
said regretfully. Having no more conveni-
ent place to deposit himself after the con-
flagration, he had sat on a log and was
promptly bitten by a scorpion. He still
felt the effects of the sting, and wanted to
stop about the crossing so that somebody
could find him if he began to die. If he
recovered he was going to the diggings on
the Roper River to get a million pounds’
worth of gold that was in the ground there.
He was barefooted, and was looking for
someone who had a pair of boots to give
away.
“Been bare-footed long?” I asked.
“Only since the fire,” he answered. “In
most parts,” he went on, “when a settler’s
burnt out, it’s the custom to get up a sub-
scription for him. Three families that I
might consider my neighbors for the time
were livin’ within a mile or two o’ where I
pitched me camp. They were all sorry to
hear of my misfortune when I told ’em, but
none of ’em sent the hat round. ’Twarn’t
neighborly.”
“Did you mention the scorpion?” I asked.
“I did,” he replied, “but they said that
needn’t alarm me, as tli’ bite wasn’t always
fatal.”
Sunday.-—Hunting for horses all the morn-
ing. Searched the sandhills, and followed a
great many gohauna tracks. Found nothing
more important at the end of them than a
hole in the sand. Tracking gohannas is
wearisome. They go such a long way,
and wobble like tanglefoot. The man the
scorpion bit came along, and I set him dig-
ing up the holes with an improvised yam
stick. He got a black beetle and two centi-
pedes. lie also got tired; and likewise
hungry.
We might have caught a fish with less
trouble, but I preferred a carnie. It is a
delicacy much relished by overlanders. It
is also known as the sand gohanna, from its
habit of burrowing in the sand. It is much
smaller and of a lighter color than the com-
mon gohanna or lace lizard. The stump
lizard and the frilled lizard —the chap with
the Vandyke collar and mouth like a young
tank —are somewhat similar in flavor.
I was giving up the search in despair when
I happened across one near camp. Roasted
it on the coals and made a hearty meal.
The man the scorpion bit was down the river
chopping white grubs out of trees. He
came back after dinner, with yellow stains
trending downwards from each corner of his
mouth, showing that the grubs had been fat
and juicy.
I put my two saddles and swag into a bag,
and with this over my shoulder started for
the squattage at four o’clock. The whole
weighed about lcwt. Half-way up a heavy
storm caught me, and the rain for awhile was
blinding. A long black-soil flat stretched
before me. Where the grass saved me from
bogging the stuff adhered to the soles of my
boots, in thick layers of mud and grass.
Jerking this off by vigorous kicks partially
dislocated my legs. It was like walking on
stilts, with feet of lead.
My swag, which was sopping wet, had
increased twofold in weight. It was all the
more irksome as each foot slipped back six
inches at every step. Then there were
alternate sheets of Water to wade through,
with the inevitable swish-swosh in my boots
afterwards. Though I was wet to the skin,
the perspiration streamed off me.
When I reached the slip-panels near the
homestead I espied my strayed property
under a tree. I addressed some remarks to
them, applicable under the circumstances,
which no paper would print. Then I drove
them into the paddock.
Steady rain fell all next day, so I had
plenty of time to cook and “put things in
order.” My hut was Sft. x 10ft., with slab
walls, bark roof, and floor composed of ashes,
sand and mullock. It stood on the bank of
the Nive River, surrounded by a cluster of
trees, long grass and undergrowth. The
male part of the married couple recommended
it as a nice quiet place: and it was casually
mentioned that several snakes had been killed
in and around it. They were an ugly variety
of snake, dirty green in color, as thick as a
shovel handle, and grew to a length of Oft.
While frying some flapjacks I heard a
noise in a square iron tank which had been
thrown on its side behind the galley. Armed
with the fire-shovel, I stepped cautiously over
and peered in. The inmate wasn’t a snake;
it was the man the scorpion bit. lie was
crouched in a corner, naked, his knees under
his chin. His clothes, tied in a bundle,
rested on his thighs.
“What are you doing there?” I demanded?
“I come in out of tli’ wet,” he answered,
crawling out.
“And what did you take your clothes off
for ?”
“To keep ’em dry. They’re the only togs
I’ve got.”
“Better step inside and put them on,” I
advised. “It’s not respectable to be going
about like that.”
Ilis dress consisted of a shirt with no
buttons on, a pair of patched pants (one leg
of which was torn off at the knee), and a
holey concavity that used to be a hat. When
he had put everything on, and fastened
the shirt with a two-incli nail, and
tied a bit of rope round himself for
a belt, his appearance suggested a
sick crow in a storm. I gave him
a feed and a shake-down. Next morning he
got rations at the store and left for the
Roper. At Augathella an unsympathetic
policeman took him in charge for not having
enough sense to travel with. He had been
going round with the hat on behalf of II
imaginary settlers, all of whom had been
burnt out and subsequently bitten by scor-
pious,
EDWARD S. SORKNSON.
